THE RED PAGE.
The Sweet Uses of London.
ONE VIEW
I LANDED in London nine stone odd, finan-
cially weak, but with plenty of confidence
and an abundance of that restless energy
which is found in human weeds and living
skeletons —especially when the weed lands in
foreign parts under circumstances such as call
upon a weed to “ buck up.” I landed in London
on Saturday, had a look round on Sunday, and,
that week, I was settled pretty comfortably, and
writing, in a village in Herts.
I had a box full of old printed matter and copy,
finished and fragmentary, which I’d humped about
the world for years, and which I thought much of
and cherished, principally because it had been so
persistently rejected by The Bulletin and every
other paper I submitted it to. It got mixed up so
often and I wasted so much time sorting it out and
looking up parts of it to use in new stories (under
the impression that I wrote better long ago) and
trying to put it together for a book; and it
worried me so much for fear I’d lose a page of it;
that at last I made a heap of it in the back yard
of my last lodgings in City Road and set fire to it.
That was the only occasion I remember on which
I had words with my friends, the Metropolitan
police : the flame and smoke from my old copy
brought a fire engine, and the firemen got wild
and laid a complaint. (It cost me something in
drinks).
When last I roamed at liberty on this page I
advised Australian writers to go to London or
Timbuctoo, or shoot themselves, and I remember,
by the way, the scribb ers who got most excited
and barked loudest at me in print—and flatly
refused to take the advice 1 did n’t intend for ’em
—seemed to have altogether overlooked the words
“whose talents have been recognised.” But
what’s the use of raking up old things?—it only
leads to sadness and excitement.
My advice still is : Go to London—don’t bother
about Eternity or Tftnbuctoo. Go to London.
If you want to write popular sensational stuff and
make money, and if you have a gift that way, you
will probably make money, and get into society
that will do you no good, and live beyond your
means, and have illustrated interviews with your-
self in a magazine, and in the end you’ll be
dragging out a miserable existence slinging slush
for penny and halfpenny papers for a penny a
line or less ; your name forgotten, and not even
of any consequence to the editors of the rags for
which you write.
J*
I know several Australian writers who went to
London with letters of introduction, put money
on their backs, lived in expensive apartments,
hustled an article or two on popular subjects into
new magazines, and then kept alive by writing
articles, theatrical notices, and society columns—
to be signed by the editor and his wife—until they
got their passage money from friends in Aus-
tralia. But if you want to do good work, and
feel that you can do it, you will need in the first
place to live for, say, twelve months in London—
for London is n’t going to be hustled.
Take a cheap flat and live as cheaply as you
can in North London or somewhere, or a cottage
in a quiet little farming village up the Thames—
avoid a middle-class village, for the narrowness
and paltriness of people, whose whole soul is in
“ keeping up appearances,” would drive you mad.
And few Australians can stand “apartments ” or
a London landlady for any length of time. Don’t
bother about letters of introduction—l took a
parcel home, kindly sent by friends, and never
used one.
jft
There is room and a magazine for good work in
England and Scotland, and the editors want the
work—not letters of introduction. In good work,
simple domestic yarns and true sketches of the
better sides of human nature, of man, woman and
child nature, go best now. They don’t want the
other man’s wife in England—she’s done. Editors
of good magazines and readers for good publish-
ing firms read all MSS. that are not absolutely
hopeless from the start. A publisher’s reader
fears the possibility of his neglecting a story which
another publisher might make a hit with.
Work hard and do good work, and keep to
yourself. I had invitations to suppers at the
Authors’ and other clubs, but I had n’t time. The
big men are of no use to you—they have n’t time
to waste on you; the middle men want their own
advice. The little men only bother and-waste
your time. But I must say this: That the
smallest men of London journalism are many
times larger and more broad-minded and generous
than are the little men of Australian literature.
But writers only worry each other; they are of no
use to each other; each wants his ideas for himself;
and if you run after writers too much, in the end
you’ll be writing to please them, and not the
people.
First of all, get a good agent—and trust him
with your soul, and let him alone. I’m giving
you a lot of advice that I did n’t use myself—and
I want you to bear in mind that I’m giving you
the free benefit of experience that I paid ten per
cent, cash and gave the two hardest years of my
life to get. The agent knows the literary busi-
ness world, and you don’t. He reads your book,
and knows what publishers are most likely to
take it up and give a fair price for it; he reads
your sketch, and knows the magazine it is most
suited for. And he’ll get two hundred where
you’d weaken and take fifty. You’d be blunder-
ing for a year or two all in the dark.
And, another thing, which seems a little thing:
when you send out your first story, and it is
returned with thanks, you may be discouraged
and throw the screed aside or into the fire in dis-
gust. The agent is n’t discouraged : if he’s a
good agent, and your story is very bad, he does n’t
send it out at all. It spoils his business to send
out bad work. He returns it to you with advice.
But when he sends it out, and it is returned, his
clerk slips it into another envelope and sends it to
another editor right away, and so on until it is
placed. You may have to wait, but you only hear
the good news. You get no short and nasty
letters from publisher and editors which would
fire you to write cheeky ones back and spoil your
chances there for ever. No ; your agent gets the
nasty letter from a worried, overworked editor,
and he writes a polite and dignified reply and
sends another MS. tor the editor’s consideration.
J*
Don’t listen to the talk about editors and pub-
lishers preferring to deal straight with the authors,
and wishing to see them and know them.
Straight editors and publishers, when they mean
business, would sooner deal with a business man.
The reasons are obvious to editors and others who
know the writing animal. Should your agent
consider it advisable for you to lunch with an
editor, or a critic who seems really interested in
you, he. will stage the business for you. Another
little hint: if you have to leave London for a
distance—or if you stay in Australia and put your
work into the hands of an agent, tell him to open
all your London letters, private anA**! l erwise,
before he forwards them to you, are
editors and others who will persist in voting to
the author on business and ignoring the agent;
and in your case three or four months would be
lost in mailing. Get a good agent, trust him, let
him alone, and write with a mind free from
business worries.
H.L.
ANOTHER VIEW
TO shun editors ; to abjure society ; to trans-
act business through an agent; to live
secluded in North London But, if
this be the road to success, why go to
London ?
“ Editors want your work; not you,” Then the
post will carry your work. “Leave it all to the
agent.” A month later or sooner makes no
difference to the class of matter we have in mind.
Then why go to London ?
If you go to write Bush stories or sketches, there
is no reason for going. Going to spy out the
country ; going for journalism ; going for experi-
ence ; going for pleasure ; going with assured
means to a definite end : these goings are reason-
able. But, unknown, with a boxful of MS., to
go to London in the hope of “ succeeding in litera-
ture” —well, for the average person who reads
these lines, it is folly.
J*
Patchett Martin, who knew all about so-called
“success in literature”—from the mediocrities’
point of view—gave sound advice when he coun-
selled unknown Australians to sit tight and write.
Lawson gives unsound advice. There is in
London, for what is called “ Australian litera-
ture,” no market that cannot be reached just as
well from Australia. Without saying so, Lawson
himself says so.
And Lawson himself was an exceptional case.
He had made a name in Australia: he had pub-
lished a couple of successful books; and the
famous “shoot yourself” article, which was an
interesting statement of a peculiar attitude, had
been widely quoted, and widely misunderstood.
Despite his suspicion of strangers, despite
everything, the average Englishman remains
“a simple-minded bloke.” His emotions were
touched by Lawson’s picture of tragedy, just
as they are touched by yarns about Turkish
outrages upon the meek Christians (!) of Bul-
garia. He stood ready to pity and patronise:
pity and patronage being moral agents of self-
esteem. Besides, Imperialism was in the air;
and there was a general feeling that these wild
Australian geniuses really ought to be encouraged,
don’t-you-know.
And Lawson was the genuine article: there
was no mistake about that. There was enthusiasm
for him ten years ago : there is appreciation still.
When his first little book was published by his
mother, it was written for The Bulletin (5/l/’95)
that “Henry Lawson is the voice of the Bush,
and the Bush is the heart of Australia ” —and so
on with words of warm commendation and
encouragement. His work does njt amount to
much on the grand scale ; yet it is a big thing for
Australia, and a few Englishmen could under-
stand that.
J*
But when Lawson talks of “succeeding” in
London, one asks what that kind of success
amounts to. He was away from Australia a
couple of years, and he sold to English publishers
two or three books of old matter and new, and a
number of magazine articles. And he has sold
or will sell more. Probably his receipts were
about a book: possibly he drew or
jS7OO in all. That’s over and done with.
If he had “ sat tight ” in Australia and stuck to
work, and kept up the quality of his work, he
could have made as much more here—and the
agent would have done his business, on his own
showing. “ Steele Rudd ”is another typical and
popular Australian author —Lawson’s inferior in
some points, his superior in others. He sits tight
in Australia. This year his income from On Our
Selection, published four years ago, has been close
on £i a week. The book is selling better than
ever, and the income keeps on. The wholesale
agents’ order for Our New Selection, to be pub-
lished next month at 65., is 4000 copies—the best
advance order such a book has ever had iu this
country.
“Steele Rudd ’’deserves bis success. So does
Lawson. But “Steele Rudd’s” Australian suc-
cess is worth a lot more than Lawson’s “London
success.” There’s no sense in pointing Aus-
tralian authors to London while Australia is so
liberal in encouragement. If Lawson had sat
tight, and managed his business properly, he
ought to be drawing a steady £6 a week from the
sale of his books in Australia. With the “London
success ” thrown in—by the agent whom Lawson
declares should be left alone to achieve it.
J*
Apart from cash, there is no profit in “going to
London.” There appears to be in some Australian
minds an idea that London is a desirable
place to go to with the idea of “ succeeding.”
Daley writes vaguely of the fascination “ when
London calls.” Nothing in it. London is, from
one point of view, a nice, friendly, familiar city—
with that curious air of homeliness that Sydney
has, among Australian cities. From another point
of view, it is a filthy hole. The men are beasts of
burden and the women beasts of pleasure—hard
driven. The air is unspeakably foul. The climate
is vile, with variations. In good weather, it’s a
good place to visit. To live there is ghastly.
This is no wail of the disgruntled, because Aus-
tralian journalists do well in London, in their
modest journalists’ way. We ’ll talk of that an-
other time.
MEMORANDA
Pennell’s eulogium of Whistler m Sept. North
Amer. Review extends to books as well a9 pictures ; and
is so unmeasured in the one respect, where we can
measure it, that one doubts its validity in the other.
Whistler's hooks are clever, fevered, and (with the
expressive adjective) thin : they are in no sense great
literature. Suppose posterity were to apply a similar
judgment to the paintings'! 1
Pennell has no alarms. “ No one since Velasquez and
Rembrandt has had such an effect on the art of the
world. And not alone by the graphic arts will he live,
but by what he wrote and what he said. There is no one
who has carried on the traditions of English literature of
the days of Elizabeth and James, as this man has done, in
one book. (!) Nor is that all. As a patriot, as an
American, it would be hard to find his peer.” (! !)
“ He painted the White Girl and the Piano Picture, he
etched the French Series and the Thames Set. And they
are immortal.” “But then one is always surprised at
common sense in anything connected with British act-
criticism.” “It is a popular superstition that Whistler
was accepted in France. Until 1883, he was treated
rather worse, officially, in France than in,England, and it
is only this year" that, sending to, Budapest, he
found himself carefully ignored. Success for him
meant a fight in every country for recognition.”
“ Rembrandt has not changed, and Velasquez has not
changed, and Whistler will not change. For genius was
the portion of these three artists, and immortality is their
reward ...” “ He was in no sense an English artist;
English artists never did one single thing for him during
his life nor since his death ...” “He had no interest in
British art, save that which is above nationality, like
Hogarth’s. And near Hogarth he now sleeps.”
Old Times, a Sydney publication of some inte-
rest which collected anecdotes and memorabilia of “ old
colonial days,” has suspended publication with the fifth
number. •
To the disgust of local booksellers, an Edin-
burgh firm last week loaded a Sydney auction-room with
some 2000 books, which realised about £200. Prices
ranged a little under wholesale values, on the average.
The books, however, were not a very valuable lot—con-
sisting partly of remainders, impressions from worn
stereos, and general shop debris, sweetened with a few
good items. Competition was hot keen, and in compari-
son with local prices, purchasers secured bargains.
Reviewing On Our Selection, by “Steele Rudd,”
The Athenaeum of 5th September says“ Among the
small, the regrettably small, population of the Australian
Commonwealth, we understand that some eleven thou-
sand copies of this book have been sold. We think it
should find a few thousand new readers upon this side of
the world. It is certainly worth perusing, if only because
it is entertaining. But it has other merits. Its five-and-
twenty chapters depict with absolute truthfulness,
as well as humour, one kind of life lived in Aus-
tralia. It is nob the kind of life of which tourists are
shown anything. Newspaper correspondents send
us no cable messages about selectors, cockatoo
farmers, or life in the back-blocks. The selector has very
little concern with marvellous Melbourne, Sydney Har-
bour, the Federal Parliament, or Government House
entertainments. Well-fed city residents in Australia do
a great deal of talking about capital and labour, demo-
cracy and freedom. The selector has no breath to spare
for this sort of thing ; he is too busy fighting the droughts,
the bush, the forces of Nature in fact. He fights all day,
and half the night very often, over his share of making
the great country he iives in habitable for himself and
for future generations. He is further away than his
countrymen in the cities, less noisy, and more
modest. And so it happens that the world
at large knows very little of the Australian
selector, who is, perhaps, a good deal better
worth knowing than any one else in his country.
Novelists who lay the scenes of their masterpieces in the
Antipodes have a way of clinging to the largest and most
comfortable sort of stations, where every one dresses for
dinner and the company is select. Others, again, of the
realistic kind, tell us of sundowners and “spielers,” as
who should say that Australia is populated by wealthy
squatters and "thieving tramps. This book gives us the
selector upon his selection in the bush, with his brave,
rude, hearty wife; his ragged, mischievous, hard-working
children ; his hopes, and fears, and difficulties, and weli-
earned little triumphs. It is a volume of papers sketched
with absolute truth (the reviewer can answer to this from
his own experience) and with real humour.”

Justice Joyce, in Eng. Chancery Court (re-
ported August 2) reiterated the dictum that “ there is no
copyright in news, though there may be in expression
and in the language in which it is conveyed.” English
law in this matter would probably be held to govern
current Australian : both being vague beyond belief.
One hopes that Commonwealth Parliament will attack
copyright soon and sensibly.

The octave of a dedicatory sonnet “To Maurice
Maeterlinck,” by Frederick Langbridge—
Ah, you who dwell in a dim, haunted place
Where flesh is halfway spirit, where young eyes
Are large and heavy with innocent tragedies,
Where a strange, wistful twilight veils a space
Of ominous water, breaking round the base
Of an undated tower, in human-wise
Sad, and old woods in questions and replies
Rumour the sorrow of a fated race .. .
Wanted a clean copy of The Hidden Tide, by
R. Quinn. Two-and-sixpence will be paid at this office.

Rebel: Minor interest, and three weeks late
J.M.: Too vague. Want references, full and precise....
R.R. demands of the Infinite, “ Who isthe author of
‘Brother Ben,’ a droving yarn in verse?”.... Audie-
mur” asks for author of “ What from thy fathers thou
dost inherit, earn it, then it becomes thine own.”
Astonishing with what an impressive air these illogical
maxims stalk about. What you inherit from your fathers
is Yourself— i.e., you very ownest own, however (in
another view) it belongs to others your forbears. If the
reference be to cash and similar extrinsics, there are apt
retorts to every explanation of the mystery W.K.A. :
Kindly kill “the Hobart poet named Mason.” A.E.M. :
“ S.M. Herald attributes to Longfellow the boy that
stood on the burning deck”? Very 1ike1y....“ Burglar
Bill ’’agrees with Daley regarding the simplicity of the
ideal writing-room. “ I found half the prisoners in
gaol were poets : the other half would have become poets
had they stayed long enough. The cells were devoid of
ornamentation. I remember one simple horse-stealer’s
verse—
With eyes blood-flecked with the glare of Hell,
Face hardened with wind and hail,
Is the bull-faced hound who runs us round
The ring at gaol!

Dictionary, of which indeed it is practically an abridg-
ment. Gan be recommended for school use.]
Quid Bits frae Robert Burns; illus. by W. F.
Brown (Glasgow, Bryce ; Is. net.) [A choice of texts
from the Scottish bible.)
Henslowe’s Annual, No. V (Melb., .Henslowe ; Is.)
[This magazine of impudents and incompetents has
varied in five numbers from the tolerably bad to the
intolerably bad. The current number is tolerably bad:
it would have been tolerably good if the editor had per-
mitted it. He has the aft of getting contributions, but
not the art of getting good contributions. Yet there
are bright spots worth the low price.]
The Book of the Peach, by H. W. Ward (Walter
Scott; 2s. 6 d.) [Deals with Eng. conditions, and
peaches under glass.]
Frank Baylis, by John Crane (Walter Scott;
5 5.) [An inexpert writer’s novel. The plot is indis-
tinct : the subject is the experience of an English
youth in training for the Roman priesthood—treated
irreverently.]
Barlasch of the Guard, by H. S. Merriman
(Macmillan ; 2s. 6 d.) [Merriman has written better
novels ; but even these were somewhat stiff, lacking
dash 'and fascination. Barlasch is all stiffness —a
skeleton of deliberate “adventures,” inanimate and
uninteresting.]
A.G.S.
TO J. le G. B.
My eloquent fnend So you’ve come at the end to weep
in rhyme, and pray
For this Self, con temned, whose comrade Self lies mur-
dered by the way.
Fresh from the flowers and sun-lit hours, the evening and
morning stars,
You tip-toe along the dark prison of tcrong, and prattle
reproof through the bars:
To the whites of your eyes
You are shocked at the cries
Of your old chum, who dies
In the Valley of the Shadow of Death.
Where goblins peer and daemons jeer y o u uttered a cry or
two!
(You remember your rhymes of the terrible times when
you were battling through ?)
But suppose your feet like mine had failed, your heart at
last gone do wn f
Suppose you had carried my heavy cross, and lost my
splendid croum,
Would you count it a crime
Had you rapped out a rhyme,
As you sank in the slime
Of the Valley of the Shadow of Death ?
With the latest breath of this life in death, 1 swear, by
the Self I have slain,
Were I now on the track where I never turned back 1 would
tread the old foot-prints again—
1 would follow my weird till my soul was seared beneath
the fiery rod
Of the pitiless Fate whose name I hate—the God who is
not my God.,.
So—a quivering lip,
And a good-bye grip,
As I stwnble and trip
Down the Valley of the Shadow of Death.
D. O'R.
(with love to the Prisoner.)

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tired.
Tired of sins of mine, and sins of others,
So tired I faint upon the arid road.
The dust of failure ev’ry effort smothers,
E’en proud ambition answers not the goad.
This living death kills hope within the bosom,
I’m like a menial by a tyrant hired,
Who fruit demands from trees nipped in the
blossom,
None knows save God how utterly I’m tired.
The tyrant Life has bound me fast, in fetters
Forged ere my birth; I see, yet cannot reach
The goal the world says is for those, my betters.
The little world of volubles, who preach
From themes compounded from the vaunting
chatter
Of grinning Cant, by grinning Cant admired—
That I dissected things I could not flatter,
These have assailed me until I am tired.
What little seeds of Truth the God has given,
I sowed in love and watered with my tears ;
And more by sorrow than by anger driven,
I wandered down the graveyard of the j ears;
And marked the slain where slayers should belying,
And strove until my frozen blood was fired
To tell the Truth, and why the weak went crying,
Like me, the almost universal Tired.
Tired, deadly tired, of lying pictures, painted
To suit the creed and fashion of the day;
So ye grew strong, no matter what ones fainted,
Or lived in pain, or gasped their lives away.
One time, ah, fool! I took ye for your seeming—
That fool at least, I weep not, has expired,
He lived in days made up of hope and dreaming ;
Hope, too, is dead—and, losing hope, I’m tired.
Here on Hope’s grave, in most regretful slumbers,
I sit and nod and start to life in pain.
But what’s my plaint when Truth points out the
numbers
Of more deserving ones who strive in vain ?
Still one’s own battle always seems the wonder,
We think we’ve fought when only we’ve aspired;
A little sound we magnify to thunder;
A little toil and trouble, and we’re tired.
The world on weary, leaden feet is trudging
Its endless, changeless round amid the stars,
And on its dusty surface ants are drudging
And dragging weary loads on creaking cars,
And all of it is to such little purpose,
The workers are in sordid rags attired;
Some petty heap of soil they metamorphose,
And then they perish—just through being tired.
W.A.
M. ASTON.
Beside the River.
There’s a bend adown the river, where the noon-
day sun-glints quiver,
’Twixt where gum-boughs, gnarled and twisted,
the "water’s bosoms lave,
And each coy ripple on the stream parts with an
azure sparkling gleam
As sapphire fishers dart and dine upon the shoals
of baby bream.
There’s an orchard round the bend there, and the
autumn tintings blend there.
And ’mid the boscage all aglow the mellow cream-
bloomed quinces grow;
While below the reed-beds rustle, and the gnats
are all abustle,
As the bittern hunts, with stately tread, for water-
snail and mussel.
When my soul re-seeks its Giver, may I sleep
beside the river,
Where budless plumes of sombre green o’erpall
the water’s merry sheen,
And the blithe dawning of each Spring my timid
feathered loves will bring
To the trysting tree above my head their sweetest
notes to sing.
vio.
RITA SUNYASEE.
Love May Come.
Love may come a gentle thing,
Hovering on airy wing;
Love may come a burning fire,
Blazing with the soul’s desire,
Cleansing all of dross away,
Leaving nought but love to stay.
Love may come a mighty wave,
Hungry as the open grave,
Bearing out a life that lies,
Shrouded as death’s mysteries,
To cast on the mocking shore
Of a lifeless Nevermore.
Love may come a homely bliss,
For kind care and quiet kiss ;
Bear whatever guise it may,
True love comes to rule and stay—
Unknown how it came to me,
I awoke to love and thee.
N.S.W.
C. A. FORDE.
The Reason.
The lonely homeward track I went,
I met her at the back (I went
That way that I might miss her);
The night was inky dark, and yet
(There wasn’t one to hark) —and yet
I didn’t like to kiss her.
Next day the postman handed me
A scented note, which branded me
A hopeless mark for Cupid :
“You met me all alone, and yet
(You had me on your own)—and yet
You never—Oh ! you stupid ! ”
No brother near, no sister there,
And yet if I had kissed her there,
She would have screamed, I’m thinking ;
•She’d have been sure to smell it, and
Quite sure, likewise, to tell it, and—
The fact was, I’d been drinking.
N.S.W.

Dyspeptic Emesis
A LONG PERIOD OP SUFFERING.
A Permanent Cure in Newtown.
The Case of Mrs. C. WALKER-
(For tht “ Independent.”)
A subject necessarily inviting to all, and
the discussion of -which has of late years
employed many able pens, was recently
touched upon by Mrs. Gertrude Walker, of
No. 17 George-street, Newtown, when chat-
ting with our special representative.
“ It is in reference to the most effective
means of gettipg rid of one’s personal
enemjes that I wish to speak to you,” said
Mrs Walker, “and I think that I am
entitled to speak on the subject because my
liver was out of order for a long time and
my sufferings were extraordinary. lam
tweive years older since my troubles began
with an attack of neuralgia, which I
endured for three months without abate-
ment. >The terrific pains lodged in the back
of my head at first, then they affected my
gums, which became very sore, making it
impossible to properly masticate my food,
and then the tortures shot up to my eyes.
Later on there was not a part of my head
which escaped the maddening agonies of
neuralgia, and often I thought they would
drive me out of my mind.”
“ I expect you got but very little sleep ? ”
enquired the write?.
“ Sleep was denied me altogether,” re-
sponded Mrs. Walker. “Often I have paced
the floor for hours when everybody else has
been fast asleep, and when morning came I
was not fit for anything. My nerves were
all of a shake, nervousness having such a
hold upon me that I was afraid of my own
shadow, and when a knock came to the door
,t caused me to tremble so violently that I
? °”ld scarcely I was afraid at every
turn, an idea being iiTfriy mind continually
that somebody was standing behind me,
though when I looked round there was
nobody, and I could only reproach myself
for my folly. Still, I could not help feeling
timid, and to be so every moment of the day
had the effect of making me fretful and
despondent. I lost flesh till I became as
thin as a skeleton, for which I blame my
poor appetite and the trouble I had in keep-
ing my food down. My stomach was in a
dreadfully weak condition, a fact which
accounted for the frequent fits of vomiting
I had, for it was all the same in effect what
kind of food I took—it would not stay
down. Many a time I thonght I was going
to be suffocated by the sense of oppression
in my eldest, for when it was at its worst I
had to gdsp for breath in a most alarming
way. I could not make out what was
wrong a little distance below my neck.
There seemed to be something pricking me
there—a very tantalising sensation—although
at other times the pain was as great as if I
had strained myself. I cannot imagine
anything more vile than the taste which was
in my mouth, and to make it moi;e un-
pleasant still there was a coating on iny
tongue which made me feel quite sick. On
arising from bed I was distressed by the aches
running through my legs and across my
loins, for they set at defiance any attempt I
might make to do any light work about the
house, although at the best of times I was
so easily overpowered by exhaustion that I
had to let matters take their own course.”
“Did you obtain advice from any
source ? ”
“I should rather think so. A doctor
from Sydney was doing his best for me, yet
I cannot say there was anything satisfactory
resulting from his treatment. lam sure his
medicines did not benefit me, because my
internal troubles multiplied week by week.
If I walked a few yards I was brought to a
standstill by an attack of giddiness, and on
many sccasions when I walked a little
distance in the garden a mysterious sensa-
tion come over me which made it appear
that the further I walked the further the
fence seemed to be away. And then my
heart gave rise to serious reflections. The
slightest excitement or exertion always caused
it to flutter and throb, but even when I was
sitting still it used to suddenly start pal-
pitating with such vengeance that its
beatings could be heard quite distinctly.
The sharp pains which ran through my heart
from time to time also lent color to the idea
that it was diseased in some way or other, and
as the physiol w»* taking did not alleviate
my sufferings in the least, it appeared
certain that I was going to bo an invalid
for life. With my yellow skin and my
features distorted by the pbysicial agony
and the anxiety of mind I was enduring, I
looked as if my days were almost numbered,
but now, thank Heaven, there is a great
difference in my appearance, and although
I am over thirty years of age, I feel as young
and active as a'girl of sixteen.”
“Can you account for Buch a wonderful
transformation.”
“ Easily Clements Tonic did it, and only
for an old friend who told me about that
medicine I might still be suffering, or per-
haps be dead, as nothing else could cure me.
Several days passed before I felt any better
by taking Clements Tonic, and I4>waa
beginning to think that it was no benefactor
oi mine, but I kept on with it and then I
commenced to feel revived. The neuralgic
pains, I noticed, were dying away, and then
they suddenly stopped; but although this
was a great relief, I was more concerned
about my internal organs, and I was in a
state of painful suspense as to whether
Clements Tonic was equal to the occasion.
Sure enough it was. I became regular in
all my habits, freed from the unpleasant
taste in my mouth, my tongue was cleansed,
and I had an appetite to enjoy my food.
The wind which used to arise from my
stomach did not form now, and I never had
the least inclination to vomit my food. I
thought this was a remarkably satisfactory
test of the merits of Clements Tonic,
and when my first purchase was exhausted
I got more, as I meant to get quite well
whilst I had the chance. From that time
onwards I gained strength by leaps and
bounds. As regularly as I lay down to re.- 1,
I had calm and refreshing sleep, and I
did appreciate it after passing so many
wretched nights not long before. Tits
freedom with which I could draw my breath
was evidence that my digestive organs were
being benefited by the use of Clements
Tonic, and as my heart had resumed its
normal pulsation, without ever paining me
either, I knew that it was not diseased, but
that my anxious fears had been induced by
the bad state of my liver. For my nervous
system there was nothing I had taken which
could approach Clements Tonic for general
excellence, as my nerves were left without
a tremor and I could walk anywhere in the
dark without any fear. By adhering
to the use of Clements Tonic for some weeks
I became as healthy as I have ever been, and
for several years my constitution has been
perfectly sound. If these admissfons will
assist people similarly afflicted, you may
publish them in any way you please ”

EDINBURGH and BIRMINGHAri.
Wore a Torture
BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
sulphuric nem.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained Worrd
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen —In answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, I thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £i and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was -neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£ 4. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find - are
suffering from the distressing malady
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient,
W. FOLLEY

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 22, 1903.
The Decaying N.S.W. Labor Party.
The time has come when genuine reformers in
N.fe.W. must re-consider their attitude towards
the Labor Party in the State Parliament. Por
some time past, as that party acquired knowledge
it has lost a certain amount of its original earnest-
ness, has become more politic as it became better
acquainted with the temporary advantages that
can be won by skilful manoeuvring. This
process of acquiring knowledge and of losing
sincerity was emphasised at the inaugura-
tion of the Federal Parliament which took away
from the N.S.W. State party men like Watson,
Hughes, Spence, Thomas and Watkins, and
left behind several other of the more master-
ful members with a grievance against Federa-
tion because they had been rejected by
Federa.l constituencies. The gaps in the party
were filled in obedience to party considerations
rather than on the dictates of principle. The
Labor Party showed a willingness to take as its
candidate any man willing to sign its pledge and
subscribe to its platform, even though he alto-
gether lacked any previous good record as a
Radical, and was, so far as his public past was
concerned, of at least doubtful character. The
party, in short, began to rely too much on its
platform and to look too little to the character of
the men to whom it entrusted its representation.
The consequence was the intrusion of a few men,
who are not in any sense of the word “Labor
men,” or even sound Radicals, and whose sincerity
is not open to question only because the lack of it
is so obvious. All this—the loss of some good
men, the picking up of some very indifferent
men, the sore-headedness of otherwise good
men whose Federal ambitions were thwarted,
together with the fatal growth among almost all
of a spirit of compromise and bargaining—has
brought the party to a lower plane. Seeking
numbers rather than quality, it has lost influence
as it has gained numbers, and it threatens now to
merge into a party of the old familiar stamp,
vitally interested only in the question of “ins”
and “outs,” and ready to trim its sails to almost
any wind for the sake of “ practical politics.”
* * * *
At the time of their entry into Parliament, The
Bulletin welcomed the Labor members as bring-
ing a promise of honesty and sincerity into the
grossness of party politics; and, since, it has looked
to them as the best standard-bearers of reform.
But it becomes more evident every week that
there is no certainty that the present Labor party
?'.v any longer a satisfactory reform party, and that
democrats must cast about for some other hope.
It would be hard to name a single genuine reform
measure which the N.S.W. Labor party is now
sincerely striving to advance; but it is not hard
to point to many political misdoings which it
either tolerates or openly approves. Three of the
most serious complaints which must be urged
against the Labor party, in its role of a soi-disant
R form party, have been brought into special
prominence during the past week. They are :
The Labor party tolerates and encourages a system of
finance which is as dangerous as it is dishonest. It made
no protest against the reckless prodigality of the Owe’
bULMVAN, or against the mal-administration bj- which he
covered with discredit the principle of State employment.
JNow that the Government is warned off the London
market it supports a policy of scraping up every penny
possible by underhand borrowing, and, at the same time,
ot allowing the roads and public works to be starved of
their proper maintenance money, instead of insisting that
Che situation shall be faced, and an honest system of
squaring revenue with expenditure adopted. It is
willing- to follow the See-Owe’Sdllivan Government to
finance * n *^ le Quagmire of muddling Micawber
Through some of its principal members it flouts and
opposes the first great land nationalisation scheme ever
proposed in-Australia, and wails for the land increments
caused by the building of the Federal capital to be given,
in whole or in part, to the private landlord. This may
not be the official attitude of the party, but it is the atti-
tude of its deputy' head, Edden, and some other pro-
minent members, who are not contradicted by others of
the caucus in their public campaign of narrow Tory pro-
vincialism. r
It can no longer boast of that jealousy of the honor of
t would insist that in the administration
of the Government, not only corruption, but the appear-
ance of anything approaching to it, must be avoided.
• • • *
As to the first of these charges, it is now a
wearisome and oft-repeated tale, but none the less
still calling for serious attention. The’SEE-OwK’-
Sullivan finance is rapidly bringing the State to
the very verge of a crisis. Unable to obtain
money in London, all sorts of desperate expedients
are being adopted to scrape together loan money
localljr*ftnd these are of such a nature as to even
affect the credit of the State’s banks. Withal,
there is a big shortage in loan incomings and it is
being met by the simple and foolish process of
starving the maintenance of public works—one of
the worst features of Victorian Kyabramism.
ihe Labor party makes no suggestion as to
ending the muddle and extravagance, as to finding'
by taxation the money to which the State is
committed. It neither insists that economies
shall be honest and real (not merely the passing
on of the bill for repairs to another Administra-
tion), nor proposes to meet the deficit by the
direct taxation which is part of its platform. An
honest _ disposal on its part to meet the present
situation would please neither the See-Owe’-
Suluvan nor the Carruthers section, and
probably would not have the ghost of a chance of
acceptance in the present Parliament. But it is
what the public wants and what the public will
insist on having, one day, from some party. The
Labor party, it is becoming mournfully clear, will
not be that party.
• » « *
The second count of the indictment is, in respect
to its practical effect, not so important; in respect
w? a l *!L ’lustration of the extent to which the
N.S.W. Labor party has degenerated, perhaps
more important—certainly more significant.
What Messrs. Edden and Holman and Dacey
may say as regards the site of the Federal capital
wdl, probably, not have much influence, one way
or the other, in the Australian Parliament, but
when they— apparently with the full approval of
their fellow members—come out publicly against
land nationalisation, and demand that, not the
people of Australia but the private landlord shall
be the harvester of as much as possible of the un-
earned increment of the capital area, the effect
upon the Labor voters of this State must be a
disillusionment. The Labor platform would be
best served by the nationalisation of the largest
possible area as a Federal territory. Yet Edden,
deputy-leader of the Labor party, said in the
N.S.W. Assembly on October 13 :
I cannot for the life of me understand how the Federal
Government could have the audacity to demand, or even
to ask, from this State, 1000 square miles of territory.
What do they want it for ? If I had my way, the seat of
Government should be brought down somewhere about
National Park, and occupy about 50 acres. Let us tell
the Federal Government straight in its teeth that, as far
as this Parliament is concerned, we are totallv opposed
to the granting of any such request as that which they
have had the audacity to make. lam very pleased to
say that the party to which I have the honor to belong
are totally opposed to granting the request of the Federal
Parliament, and will oppose the proposal at every turn,
and do everything we can to prevent its accomplishment'
which is no doubt the wish of many of the leading spirits
in the Federal Parliament
Subsequently, in an interjection—
Mr. Edden : Do not let the hon. member be under any
misapprehension. I did not speak on behalf of the Labor
party. I said, “I think.” The Labor party are responsible
for their own actions 1
Mr. Wood : The hon. member spoke as deputy-leader of
the Labor party, and he spoke of his party.
Mr. Edden : I said, “ I think the majority of them, at
any rate ! ”
Which would seem to indicate that Edden’s first
most explicit statement was either a mistake on
his part, or an indiscreet admission of a fact which
it was considered best to conceal—that the N.S.W.
Labor party is totally opposed to the nationalisa-
tion, by the only truly democratic Australian
Parliament, of a territory large enough to secure
for the people the full benefit of land increments
caused by the building of the national capital.
But the disavowal does nothing but add to a
betrayal of principle an impression of lack of
candor. Holman and Dacey have, before this,
expressed the same view as Edden, and the Labor-
party, as a party, has not repudiated their views.
It must be concluded that, as a party, the N.S.W.
Labor party thinks with Edden, and is in this
important respect a Landlords’ party.
* * * *
As to the third charge—the most serious which
can be brought against a political party, for it
marks the last stage of degeneration when there is
no longer a zealous regard for the purity of
administration— The Bulletin will not say
that it is wholly established, but it is very
nearly established. The Labor party at least
brought itself under strong suspicion of con-
niving at a transaction which is certainly
an improper one, on October 14th. On that
night the Government brought down a Bill
partly to enable it to borrow more money through
the Savings Bank, partly to approve an agree-
ment into which it had already entered for
the lease of land from the Citizens’ Life Assur-
ance Co. for the purpose of building a new
Savings Bank. It is open to question
whether the present site of the Savings Bank
could not contain a sufficiently large build-
ing. It is certain that there are many blocks of
land owned by the State suitable for the accom-
modation of the Savings Bank if new premises are
necessary. No need exists for the leasing of land
from a private oi. lo enter into such an agree-
ment without consulting Parliament was a serious
error on the part of the Government. When it is
further considered that Federal Minister Lyne
and State Premier See are directors of the
Citizens’Life Assurance Co. which is to make such
a very good bargain with the State, it is apparent
that a strong additional reason existed why no
contract should have been entered into between
this co. and the Government without preliminary
Parliamentary discussion or approval. This view
was put before the House and should have been
endorsed by a repudiation of the contract. That
seemed to be at first the opinion of the Labor
Party judging by an interjection from Mr.
Edden. But subsequently the party gave a block
vote against an amendment by Mr. Mahony
which would have had that effect.
That mere suspicion, added to so much of
known fact, as to the Labor Party’s anti-Federal
and anti-democratic and dishonest attitude on
certain questions of politics, suffices to make.it a
pressing question whether the Party has not out-
grown its usefulness and lost its sincerity. That
question will be answered very shortly by the
electors, and it can only be answered in one way
Dy democrats if there is not a prompt change in
the minds of a majority of the N.S.W. Labor
members. The Labor party must not make the
mistake of “considering itself an end—a reform
in itself. It is merely a means to an end,
an instrument of reform. No actual good
is effected by creating a Labor party—taking
this and that publican, lawyer, or mechanic,
branding him “Labor,” and putting liim into
Parliament. He must, when there, be one of a
party to act for the benefit of the people at large.
That cannot be said to be the case now.
The N.S.W. Labor party seems disorganised,
demoralised. It must either be regenerated or
perish, and pass away into the political limbo
where rests Jacob Gabbard. Jacob Garrard
was once a Labor party.
Wanted—A Freetrade Leader who
Believes in Freetrade.
The alleged leader of Australia’s alleged Free-
trade party has made a preliminary announce-
ment of his policy for the coming Federal election.
George Reid and his dog have turned another
temporary somersault, and the great inverted
clown is, for the moment, in a state of violent
Toryism. He is against the law which prohibits
th» importation of contract labor; he is against
the employment of white labor on mail steamers
subsidised by Australia; and he is savagely
opposed to the education clause as a test for
excluding undesirable niggers. Because he, when
Premier of N.S.W., was the first man to adopt
this educational test; and because in his unspeak-
able political crookedness George Reid is always
most bitter in his denunciations of the thing he did
himself ; and because the tiling he says is most
degrading and loathsome always proves to be the
thing that used to be a leading feature of his own
■ policy—it is natural that he should be especially
violent over the educational test. But these, after
all, are mere side issues. The really important
facts are that George Reid appears again as the
leader of the Freetrade party in Australia, and
demands that he shall be made the Freetrade Pre-
mier of Australia, and he says that Freetrade is
the only sure road to political salvation —and,
behold, there follows the old, unaccountable dis-
appointment ! There isn’t a single promise of
Freetrade in all the Reid programme. The
Freetrade apostle asks to be made Freetrade
Premier on the strength of his assurance that
he won’t do so very much damage to the
Protectionist tariff after all. Australia is still
waiting for the Freetrade leader who believes
in his own creed; who doesn’t find his
own gospel too dirty to be handled, and his own
faith too brutal and callous to be practised ; who
has sufficient confidence in his own ship to put to
sea in it; who will fire off his own gun with an
honest assurance that it won t burst. And
Australia has waited a very long time.
• * * *
The essential feature of Freetrade, as the term
is commonly understood, is that no local industry
shall have any tariff preference over a similar
foreign industry. There is nothing in the policy
to prevent the raising of an adequate customs
revenue. A Freetrade Government can tax all
the things which, for climatic and other
reasons, can’t be produced here; where
there is no local industry to be protected
these duties are not protective. And it can
tax imported spirits, wine, beer, sugar, tobacco,
cigars, &c., and levy an exactly equal excise duty
on the local products, and thereby get abundant
revenue while preserving its cardinal principle
infact. Then if it charges the same postage on
local papers as on foreign ones, and abolishes all
radway rate and wharfage rate preferences that
now exist in favor of local produce, it can get,
under a strictly Freetrade regime, all the revenue
it can possibly want —unless the country goes-to
utter smash through Freetrade, and w'e'have the
assurance of Reid, M‘Millan, Bruce and
Pumpkin Smith, Jawbone Neibd, and other
eminent Freetraders that, instead of going to
smash, it will blossom like the rose and flourish
like the cedar that used to hang out on Lebanon.
« * * *
Yet all these people, when in power, have
avoided the policy which they say is the sure
harbinger of prosperity, as if it were a serpent.
When M'Miluan and Bruce Smith were in
office in N.S. W. they protected beer, dried fruits,
bacon, biscuits, butter, candles, cement, cheese,
cider, confectionery, fish, galvanised iron goods of
all kinds, tongues, jams and jellies, red and
\«4*ite.J#fd, preserved milk, molasses, treacle,
sugar, wire-netting, paints, timber of almost
every description, salt, railway sleepers, tobacco,
snuff, cigars, cigarettes, wine, varnish, news-
papers, &c., and they protected the local agricul-
turist by giving him special railway rates on his
produce. When George Reid was in office, he
largely protected sugar, beer, newspapers, wine,
tobacco, cigars,-cigarettes, confectionery, &c., and
he also gave a large general Protection to the agri-
culturist by giving him specially low railway rates.
These people were not driven to this iniquity
for the sake of revenue; they sacrificed a great
deal of revenue and their blessed principles at the
same time by not levelling up the local taxes on
beer, sugar, wine, tobacco, etc., the postage rates
on local papers, and the railway rates on local
produce, so as to make them equal to the rates
charged on foreign articles. All this is an old
story, but in view of the present attitude of the
alleged Freetrade Party That Is Afraid To Practise
Its Own'Doctrines the old story is always reason-
ably new,
■5 « * *
Australia is overrun just now with Reids and
Smiths and Cooks who are prepared to hold forth
at' almost any length about the blessings and
advantages of pure, unadulterated Freetrade; it
hasn’t even half a Cook— and it is difficult to
any thing much smaller than half a Cook —
who will 'pledge himself, if he gets into office, to
practise pure, unadulterated Freetrade. There
isn’t one leading Freetrader in the crowd who
isn’t, if his own words and acts can be ac-
cepted as evidence, either an unmitigated liar
when he says he believes Freetrade to be the
most beneficial policy for the country; or an
unmitigated traitor who practised a certain
measure of Protection in the past, and is prepared
to practise it in the future, for the deliberate pur-
pose of injuring the country ; or an unmitigated
cur who sold, or is willing to sell, his alleged
principles for office. On their past records and
their present statements, there isn’t one Freetrade
leader in the present campaign who doesn’t
deserve the unmitigated contempt of every honest
man. The old allegedly Freetrade Governments of
N.3.W. lied when they said they practised
Freetrade. They lied when they said that what-
ever prosperity the State then enjoyed was owing
to Freetrade, for there was no such thing in exist-
ence. If they acted as they did because they
knew that the country, when once it got an
experience of real Freetrade (when once it ceased
to have its beer and sugar and tobacco, and wine
and newspapers and agricultural produce pro-
tected) would turn and rend them, then they lied
when they said N.S.W. was a country with a
fixed and solid attachment to Freetrade prin-
ciples. And they either lied, when they said they
believed pure, genuine Freetrade to be the best
thing for the country, or else they lied when they
said they were doing their best for the good of
ij
old Freetrade Governments was a lie.
o * * *
If there is any real Freetrade party in Australia
it had better hunt up leaders who are prepared to
run the Government on real Freetrade principles,
and thus show that they are not ashamed of their
creed, and who are prepared toYeally confer upon
the country those alleged blessings which they say
Freetrade will bring. The idea of such a vast
stock of blessings lying handy on the shelf, ready
to be lifted down at a moment’s notice, and every-
body flatly refusing to lift them down, is pathetic.
But if the Freetrade Party proposes to get into
power, and keep in power, in the old way, by
denouncing Protection, then by practising Pro-
tection and declaring falsely that it doesn’t
practise Protection, by declaring that the results
of its semi-Protective policy are evidences of
the benefits of Freetrade, by subsidising with
heavy Protection alleged Freetrade newspapers
to bolster up its lie, by scoffing at the
political party whose policy it has stolen under
false pretences, and by delivering long speeches
about the beauties and advantages of the creed
that it dare not use—then it is too degraded a
thing for any honest man to touch. And if it
isn’t going to do this, then, as already stated,
it badly wants new captains. The present ones,
on the evidence of their words and actions, are a
bad lot.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Capital
In striking Tumut out of the Federal Capital Bill
and nominating Bombala, the Senate has acted
honestly and patriotically, and N.S.W. is the very
last State that has a right to complain. When
the Federal Capital question was younger than it
is now, the N.S.W. State Government nominated
a commissioner—selected on the ground that he
was the best man obtainable for the work—to
report on possible site. He reported that Eden-
Bombala was far and away the best—that by com-
parison the other sites were left hull down on the
horizon. This was the report of a man who had
no constituents to placate ; who didn’t depend on
the importers of Sydney for advertisements and so
wasn’t called upon to lie on behalf of the site
the trade of which would go to Sydney; who had
no land to sell to build a capital on; who didn t
require to consider whether any place was in Bill
Lyne’s electorate or in anybody else’s electorate.
The Senate has accepted liis report, and unless
N.S.W. is prepared to say that its Commissioner
is a liar or a fool it has no reason to object. Also,
unless the other States can show better reason
than they have done they have no reason to object
either. _ The Senate has simply disregarded all the
log-rolling of the House of Representatives and
gone back to the merits of the case ; and it must
be remembered that at ballot, when every
man presumably voted for what he believed to
be the best site, and before the logs began
to roll, the House of Representatives gave its
heaviest vote for Bombala, thereby endorsing the
action of the Senate and the report of the N.S.W.
Commissioner. It now remains to be seen what
the Representatives will do with the matter. The
action of the Senate gives the other House a
chance to repent of its provincialism, and fall in
with the national Australian view. Those mem-
bers who fought for N.S.W. interests, or Queens-
land or Victorian interests, may now expect the
parochial section of their constituents to be satis-
fied with the efforts already made, and may regard
themselves as free to vote as the public interest
directs. _ The parish-pump interest has been pla-
cated quite sufficiently. There is not the slightest
doubt that the majority of the Representatives
is, at heart, just as sure of the suitability of
Bombala as the majority of the Senate. Except-
ing a few dainty weaklings who fear the winter
cold of Bombala (there is no reason, by the way,
that Parliament should meet in winter), and a
few slaves of the Age newspaper, who are willing
to do anything to keep the capital in Melbourne,
or a few slaves of the Telegraph to whom
Sydney’s calico importing trade is the beginning
and end of all things, there are no members of
the Representatives who do not honestly consider
Eden-Bombala the best site. Electioneering
exigencies have, so far, interfered to some extent
with the honest expression of that opinion, but
since the Senate has declared definitely for
Bombala, the opportunity is given for a re-
consideration of the matter on patriotic prin-
ciples. The N.S.W. and Queensland members,
in particular, who are anxious to have the question
settled by this Parliament, should hasten to fall in
with the Senate’s decision. That is the only way
to reach a settlement 1 in this Parliament. If the
question is allowed to stand over until the next
Parliament, the delay that will thus be caused
may stretch into years. Following the example
of the first Parliament, the second would proba-
bly postpone action until the closing days of its
life. That is a habit which so many Governments
have —of keeping back any proposals which are of
value for “bait” as long as possible. In the
game of politics it would be considered foolish to
settle to-day that which, if hung up till to-morrow,
can be used as a threat or a means of cajolery.
In the closing days of the second Parliament
another disagreement might arise, and the
question be again left unsettled. All things
point to the necessity of pressing on a
settlement now. It is now or some time
in the very indefinite future. As regards the
people of Tumut, who hung out their premature
flag and drank their premature beer, there is this
to be considered. Just now they have a chance of
a Federal capital about 100 miles from their doors.
By next Parliament the Armidale interest may
have looked up, or a movement may have set in for
repealing the Federal capital clause altogether, and
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, and a dozen other
places may be clawing wildly for the honor and
dignity. Therefore Tumut had better instruct its
Bill Lyne to reach out for the half loaf that is
available, for next year there may be no bread.
There very often is no bread next year.

George Reid “ Can’t Sufficiently Express
His Contempt.”
George Reid, in his speech at the opening of
the Imported Shoddy campaign:
There is another thing to which I want to refer, about
which there will also be a difference of opinion. I allude
to the Immigration Restriction Act. I cannot sufficiently


express my contempt for the lines on which that Bill was
drawn. The Act says that any person arriving in a ship
at any Australian port depends for his admission on a
possible ordeal, that a Customs officer may come up to
him, and if he is a YorUshireman ask him to write from
dictation fifty words in French or German —(laughter)—
or if he is a German fifty words from dictation in French
or English ; or if he is an Italian fifty words in Russian.
(Renewed laughter.)
Extract from Coghlan’s “Wealth and Progress
of New South Wales ” for 1900-01, page 955 (words
in brackets are The Bulletin’s comments):
In 1896 (when George Reid was Premier) the question
of the influx of the alien races was deemed by Parliament
to be sufficiently grave to justify it in extending the pro-
visions of the Chinese Restriction and Regulation Act of
1888 so as to exclude from the State the colored races of
Asia and Africa and the colored inhabitants of the islands
in the Pacific arid Indian Oceans. An important feature
of. the Bill was that it proposed to restrict the immigra-
tion of persons belonging to the inferior races, whether
they were or were not British subjects by birth, and the
existing Act was so amended that Chinese born in British
territory were shut out from free entry into the State.
. . . This measure, which was termed the Colored Races
Restriction and Regulation Act, 1896, was reserved for
the assent of the Queen, which was withheld. . . . The
Immigration Restriction Act of 1898 was accordingly
passed (being drafted and introduced by the. Reid
Government, and passed by the Reid majority),- and was
assented to on the 3rd August of that year. This Act,
which came into force three months after the Royal
assent was proclaimed, prohibits the immigration into
Keib South Wales of any person who, when asked to do so
by a properly-appointed officer, should fail to write out in
some European language and sign an application to the
Colonial Secretary in a specified form.
George Reid looks at his own idea adopted by
the Australian Government, and “cannot suffici-
ently express his contempt.” If he means that he
cannot sufficiently express his contempt for him-
self a good many people will agree with him.
Propsting’s Troubles.
Tasmania’s reforming Government is making so
little progress that people have almost forgotten
the good intentions with which it started busi-
ness. The Propsting Ministry commenced by
making the usual bad mistake ; it decided to be
strictly constitutional in its dealings with a grossly
ignorant, selfish and unpatriotic Upper House —a
House of narrow-minded and parochial bigots, in
dealing with whom constitutional methods are as
out of place as is remonstrance when applied to a
bear, or the offer of a tract to a swordfish. The
Propsting Government should have set out with
a clear understanding that its House of Dodery
cared nothing for justice, political honor, or politi-
cal decency ; that that House was fixedly resolved
never to see the necessity of anything that could
lead to it being called upon to pay its fair share of
taxation ; that it was political hog and had to be
driven accordingly. Instead the Propsting
Government treated the lamentable institution as
a gathering of rational beings. The results have
been remarkable. The House of Dodery rejected
the Bill for economising on the salaries of future
Governors by 11 votes to 3, The same day it
rejected the Lotteries Amendment Bill by seven
votes to six. About half-an-hour later the
Government’s Early Closing Bill went down the
Council stairs in a heap by seven votes to four.
The Bill for the abolition of the tax on incomes
derived from personal exertion received the same
sort of treatment. Probably Premier Propsting-
would have begun to fight the gang of brutally
selfish landlords about this time if he had
known how, but he didn’t ; it requires a
stronger man with a far more original mind
to get out of the constitutional rut at such
a time. Propsting allowed his own House—-
the Assembly—to reduce his proposed graduated
land tax, and to almost abolish his proposed
absentee tax. He “ conferred ” with the Council
about its refusal to abolish the tax on in-
comes derived from personal exertion; and the
Council, with its usual resolve not to tax itself as
a landowner, proposed to sanction the abolition of
the tax if it were replaced by a house duty of 6d.
in the £ (or less when the finances allowed) on the
rateable value of each house worth £lO a year or
more, said tax to he paid by the tenant in every case.
It was not to be a charge on the property ;
it was not to be recoverable from the land-
lord; and it was to cease while the property
was unoccupied. This would be, to all intents
and purposes, the tax on small incomes in
a worse form than ever. The present tax:
strikes the man with over £lOO a year income ;
the proposed one would strike everybody who-
lived in a house worth 4s. a week or upwards, SO'
it would even rope in the man with 30s. a week.
Yet there appears to be a tendency to view the
occupiers’ tax seriously, and to describe it as a.
reasonable compromise. The weakening Prop-
STING has given an assurance that he will propose
no fresh taxation on land, and that about knocks
out any hope that he will be able to keep his other
promise—to charge in future all unproductive
public works to revenue; he won’t have the neces-
sary revenue to pay for unproductive works. Im
the early days of the session, when the Council
first began to throw his financial measures down
the stairs, Premier Propsting, instead of declaring;
war against that disgraceful institution, asked the
Governor for a dissolution of the Assembly, and:
was refused. If he had got the dissolution it.
would have made no difference—the Council
doesn’t mind how often the Assembly dissolves ;.
but up to that time the Assembly had been,
supporting Propsting strongly, and the base
ingratitude of the proposal, to put its members to.
the trouble, risk and expense of a dissolution,
because the other House was cantankerous as.
usual, didn t make it feel better towards Prop-
sting. The Propsting Government now proposes-
to bring in a Bill providing for a joint sitting as a
remedy for deadlocks between the two Houses,
and when the Council has thrown that measure-
outalso it will be about time to close the session,
and let the well-meaning failures go home for
a rest. What Premier Propsting has plainly
failed to realise is that there is no possibility of
reforming anything in Tasmania until he has.
first radically reformed or utterly abolished the
House of Dodery, and unless he is prepared to-
stop supplies, and let the whole machine of State
go on strike till the House of Dodery consents to.
its own reformation or abolition, he may as well
leave the business alone. There is no strictly
constitutional means by which a constitutionally
immovable body, full of the worst intentions, may-
be shifted.

The Weeping Sharebroker.
The cowardly weepfulness of the Australian e®nr-
mereial classes is one of the most depressing;
features in this country’s outlook. As a sample;,
this is taken from the report of T. J. Thompson;
and Sons, sharebrokers, Sydney (most share- re-
ports contain the same kind of weep):
It is one dead level of stagnation. We are taxed! alll
round up to the hilt, and nearly every penny we earn goes-
to support an improvident Government. The Labor Party
look upon the position thus created as a noble object, no-
doubt, but the shoe is beginning to pinch with a vent-
geance. The labor leagues and unions are-the masters-of:
the situation, and this fact alone has successfully stopped!
immigration and encouraged emigration. There are-no-
inducements whatever for any man to seek for a home ini
Australia, and, as an influx to our shores means-capital
brought to the State, the Government thereby kills the
goose laying the golden eggs.
Australia is full of this poisonous literature, issued
by a tearful crowd which is very, sorry for itself.
In Sydney the municipal taxation is about one-
fifth of the English average. The income-tax is
less than half the English figure. Dozens of small,
vexatious imposts that afflict the Britisher are
unknown in Australia. Australia is not taxed up
to the hilt ; it is, on the whole, a very lightly-
taxed country. The labor leagues and unions,
being only a small minority in Parliament, are
not masters of the situation, except through the
incapacity of the class to which Thompson and
Sons belong—a class whose unfitness for self-
government is something pitiable. If that class
didn’t vote for Carruthers in State politics, and
for the Reid push in Federal politics, then both
the State and Federal Governments would he
independent of the Labor party and it wouldn’t
dominate things. It is the irreconcilable attitude
of a hopeless and sore-headed Freetrade minority
which gives Labor its commanding position. The
Labor domination didn’t stop immigration. As
regards Australia in general, immigration practi-
cally came to an end in 1892 when the incom-
petence and dishonesty of the class which Thomp-
son and Sons admires brought about the disgrace-
ful bank smash ; but the State where immigration
stopped most definitely was Victoria, where there
never was any Labor domination. The commercial
class of Australia, with its lies, its weeping, its
self-pity, its unmanly lamentation because it has
to pay taxes which in . other countries would be
regarded as very light, its maudlin helplessness,
and its complaints about not being treated pro-
perly, is a sorry spectacle. The Labor papers,
when things go badly with the cause they repre-
sent, are angry and defiant; the commercial pub-
lication suggests a henpecked individual shedding
tears against a horse-trough.
Tiie Meddling Country Councillor.
It is surely time that the Victorian Government
spoke a few plain words to the bustling riobodies
■who infest the country municipal councils, and
;gave them to understand that their business is to
attend to their roads and bridges, and »thistles,
and insanitary cow-yards. Just at present, the
councils are out on one of their worst jamborees,
in connection with the Government eoirtpulsory
land purchase proposals. The North Ovens coun-
cil opened the ball with a circular to all other
municipal councils, asking for their “ co-operation*
and support” in opposing such an unjust and
unnecessary law; and, all over the State, fat
squatters and landlords are “ co-operating ” with
the greatest vigor. These country councils are
almost wholly composed of large landowners, so
that their unanimity on this question is not sur-
prising, though their opinion is about as valuable
as that of a conference of dingoes on the merits of
a Scalp Bonus Act. For example, one of the
councils—Mortlake—which has decided to “co-
operate,” contains (out of 12 members) eight squat-
ters. So far, only one council has decided—by a
majority of one ! —that the matter is outside the
:scope of its municipal duties, and in this case
the Argus is careful to point out that the
•opinion of the majority was not in favor of the
Act. A few years ago most of the councils
kept religiously aloof from political matters, but
the glory of the Kyabram conferences and the
importance of the delegates, together with a cheap
trip to Melbourne, have demoralised most of them,
and the old style of council that attended to its
work, rooted up its thistles, and built its own
bridges, is rapidly becoming extinct. These very
same councils which are spending the ratepayers’
money in trips to conferences lately quoted the
Local Government Act as a conclusive reason for
not spending money in stamping out the swine
fever in their districts. Nowadays the Govern-
ment cannot open its mouth without a long pro-
cession of fat, short-breathed, long-whiskered
country aldermen wending their way to Melbourne
a few days later to enter a “protest.” Even in
the reign of the Tory Irvine the, streets of
Melbourne are half the time blocked with muni-
cipal delegates and deputations protesting against
his departure from the worst Tory principles, and
what will happen when a Democratic Ministry
comes into power is too awful to think about. The
Ministry has a new Local Government Aot in
hand, and the best advice that can be given is that
a clause be inserted making it : a felony for any
council to discuss or pass resolutions concerning
political questions; and providing that any council
infringing the law shall get no Government money
either as endowments, grant, or “compensation
for license fees” for a period of three years.
Unless something of the sort is done, Local
Government will become a dead letter in Victoria.
The election of persons to spend their time circu-
larising each other about the doings of Parliament
isn’t Local Government; it is debating society
with whiskers on it and grass-seeds in its hair.

The Splendid National Resources Again;
A pitiful little deputation waited on N.S. W. Lands
Minister Cp.ick, tne other day, and implored him
to help the people it represented to exchange their
selections in the Central Division for something
that would give them a chance of a decent living
closer in. They did not come from the farthest
west, or even from the west either; they came, in
fact, from a part around Condobblin, less than
270 miles from Sydney, which the men farther out
regard as at least half way to Eden. Yet of the
place that looks green, compared with the western
half of the State, the deputation drew this
picture:—
Originally, in 1890, 85 selectors took up land on the Mel-
rose Estate, and at the present time there were only 41
there, and out ol that 41 he did not think a single man
could say that he had earned a shilling during the last
three years. He pointed out that the estate was taken
up 33 years ago, the area comprised being 535,000 acres.
Soon after being taken up there were 220,000 sheep on it,
bur, now there were only about 7000 sheep there alto-
gether.
Ami the Minister could, for the moment, only
sympathise—and promise not to ask for any rent
for five years, if necessary. Beyond that there
was no power to go. Certainly, though the selec-
tors had undertaken to pay £1 an acre for it, the
land was obviously not worth 55.; and, even if
there were any provision for making an exchange
—which there isn’t—how much good land, he
wanted to know, could they expect to get in ex-
change for land that they had proved to be not
worth ss. ? Also, if the State took back the land
on even the 55.-an-acre basis, what was it going to
do with the country when it got it back? The
deputation didn’t know; and neither did
the Minister. The only thing that anybody
could suggest was that, anything approaching
close settlement having proved a disastrous and
cruel failure, the State should simply lock the
land up again. That was the suggestion of Scobie,
M.L. A., who knows the Far West as few men do ;
while the deputation believed much the same
thing—that the district could never be handled
except in large blocks at a peppercorn rent; and
this i 3 probably the unpleasant fact. So far as
the State is concerned the incident discloses,
indeed, two unpleasant facts. The first is that,
even the more favored parts of the West having
been proved unfit for settlement, an enormous
sum must be written off what were thought to be
the national assets; the second that, the State
having spent a vast sum in “developing the
natural resources of the country,” and a great deal
more than half of the country having been proved
not to have any natural resources that will stand
developing, the vast sum spent to develop them
must be written off, too. N.S.W. (that is to say,
Sydney) has had the vague idea that there were
almost boundless areas behind it, to which it only
had to give railways and post-and-telegraph offices
and Government dams to settle upon it producers
in vast numbers. Now it is finding out that it
isn’t that sort of country at all, and that, as the
result of attempting to make it carry more, it
really won’t carry half as much as it would before
it was “improved ” by all this vast expenditure of
borrowed money.
