A New Blind Leader.
Brisbane’s new paper, the Daily Mail, began
its career with one article on “ Public Borrowing”
and another reviewing generally the Australian
financial situation. Apart, for the moment, from
what the Mail has to say, it is, anyhow, some
satisfaction to find that these matters are now
reckoned as of so much importance that a
new paper must say something about them with
its first breath. But, beyond that, there is not
much satisfaction to be got from the Mail’s atti-
tude. True, in the vague and general terms in
which the older papers expressed themselves even
at the height of the borrowing boom, it says
that we must be careful that we must
see to it that capital is ; only “judiciously
expended.” For the rest, ipstead of stressing our
Vast borrowings, it stresses “ our vast natural re-
sources.” “ This State comprises nearly 428
million acres of land, of which only 17 million
acres have been alienated,” it says. “The un-
alienated balance, at the average price of ss. per
acre, forms a State asset worth 102 f millions
sterling. . . Our State debt is £39,300,000, much
of the interest of which is met by net earnings
from railways and other productive works.” This,
and the rest of it is the language of a paper whichi;
can be trusted to advocate more borrowing so soon
as anyone can be found who will lend—of a.
paper which is satisfied that Queensland can be.
pulled deeper yet into the mire without smother-
ing it. What Queensland needed was a paper
which would tell it that already it carries the*
greatest public debt per head in the world ;:
and the Daily Mail tells it, instead, that,
it has plenty of assets' yet to put into pawn,,
and that its public lands, which, under the pre-
sent system, are all required to pay current ex-
f enses, can be relied upon to pay its debts with.
t needed a paper to tell it that, while Victoria,
has spent only £6 per head of its borrowings on
works which do not yield revenue, Queensland,
has spent over £29 ; and yet Victoria finds itself
driven into an ignominious position in the house,
of Bull-Cohen. But the Daily Mail doesn’t
remind Queensland of that; it tells it instead,
that “old residents unanimously believe that-
good seasons have now set in, and that
we shall soon find adversity succeeded by
a high degree of prosperity.” Queensland
needed a paper that would tell it that even its so-
called reproductive works are a failure, and that,
the railways, which are the most important of the-
revenue-producing undertakings, don’t now pay,
and never have anything like paid, their way.
It needed a paper to tell it that, in order to keep
the railways running—the railways which repre-
sent the best part of the debt—the State has hiid
to subsidise them to the extent of over £3,100,000
during the past 10 years ; and it needed a paper
to ring the fact in its ears that that subsidy
has been provided simply by borrowing the
money. But the Daily Mail tells it
none of these and the other things it
needs to be kept reminded of—of the other fact,
for instance, that; within 20 yfeara, it will have to
raise £26;000,000 to pay and that
it has not even the beginnings of a.sinking fund.
Instead, it drones out the old* familiar feqngs of
“ Never Mind and “ Let it Slide; ’■’ and shows
itself one of the essy-going, shiftless' crowd “that
will at the first opportunity invite Queensland to
go and “ have another;” . . t

The Australian Fatman and his friends,-who write
in the Sunday Slimes that socialistic legislation
and a tyrannical Industrial Arbitration Court are
driving people out of Australia to make fortunes
in U.S.A., must have had a bowled-out feeling
when the American files brought full accounts of
the trial of the Yankee union “ walking delegate”
for alleged blackmail of the master builders of
sNew York and Chicago. During the trial, it was
alleged that, in 12 months, the master builders
had been bled to the extent of $400,000 under
threat of strikes. Therefore, according to the
local Tory’s own yarn, the employer is Worse off
,in the paradise of private enterprise, where there
is no Industrial Arbitration Court, than he.is in
N.S.W. where the nefarious court exists. Of
course, in a community in which the law of simply
and demand is supposed to be the sole arbiter
between employer ai:d employee, the extortions
of the walking delegate were quite logical. This
workmen were justified in asking for all they
could get, and if it meant ruin to the master
builder it was the master builder’s funeral. If
the master couldn’t apply the starvation argument
he ought to have given in, according to the rules
of the game as laid down by the Telegraph and
the Sunday Shellback. Such a process of blend-
ing individual employers is impossible in N.S W.,
the Arbitration Court attends to such things:
Most often it is Capital that plays the tyrant, and
for that reason Capital usually objects to arbitra-
tion courts, and demands that disputes between
employer and employee should be settled by free
fight—the employer giving as little as he can, and
the employee exacting as much as; he can. And
when he gets the worst of. the deal, he cables all
over the earth about the iniquity of Labor.

About a returned M.L.C. :
When this country’s M.L.O. comesback from “ foreign
parts ” and is interviewed, the barrenness that he brings
back with him is often painful to contemp’ate. ; The
latest sample is R. J. Black, of N.S.W. Upper House,'
just returned from Canada. Black, M.L.0., found
Canada prosperous, and tells Australia to go- and ,do like-:
wise. He doesn’t suggest, puriously enough, that Aus-
tralia should do likewise by establishing high Protection,
by resolving to get its debt down to about one-third of
what it is now, by resolutely providing out of revenue a:
big sinking fund, and by doing everything, through Pro-
tection and bonuses, and other aids, to create local manu-
facturing industries that will enable us to do with less
imports. These things, which are all within our power,
do not seem to occur to Black, M.L.C. But he thinks
that we should imitate Canada in giving away,
our public lands gratis. The idea is worthy of a
M.L.O. Canada has so much land which is at [least
moderately capable of settlement that it is giving it
away. Australia has so little available that when a good
piece of soil is offered at a respectable price there are on
an average about 40 applicants. A vast amount rnore
land could certainly be made available by forcibly resum-
ing the big estates of the class to which the Tory M.L.C.
belongs, but Black, M.L.0., doesn’t seem to suggest that,:
His idea is apparently that Australia, which hasn’t,
enough good State land available to anything like meet
the demands of its own purchasers, should give the little
it has away for nothing t-o foreigners—because Canada is
doing so. It is a pity Mr. Black didn’t carry his imita-
tion a little further and make things perfect.' Canada is
giving away the lands of its centre, west, and north-west,
Australia wouldn’t seriously object to doing likewise. If
any rush of European immigrants will take up the
M‘Donnell Ranges, or the Great Victoria ,Desert, or the
great sandy wastes further north, Australia will, doubt-
less, let them go quite as cheaply as Canada is doing.
But Australia hasn't followed Canada’s example in adver-
tising that it will give away its far interior gratis to
farmers, simply beoause it doesn’t want to have the bones
of thousands of dead farmers on its mind! It recognises,
that circumstances alter cases. Some Ms.L.C. don’t seem
to recognise even that much. , : ' .

Sydney Telegbaph lately came but again with its
old grey-haired howl on the subject of Federal
old age pensions. The atrocities of the Irvine
regime in Victoria having attracted, attention in
the Australian Parliament, the scared Telegraph
arose to point out that it was better that the pen-
sion matter should be left in the hands of the
State, because the Common wealth hasnomachinery
for distributing old age pensions, and it would
mean the creation of a large and.expensive depart-
ment ; and furthermore, the Commonwealth could
only “obtain the money required by raising four
times the amount in taxation.”-- The last item was
deliberate lie; even the Telegraph knows that
though the Federal Government can only retain
one-fourth of the tariff revenue, it can raise the
money required by direct taxation,, to which the
Braddon clause doesn’t apply. The statement
that the Australian Government has no machinery
for distributing the pensions was also a lie ;. it has
the whole post and telegraph system of the Com-
monwealth. Therefore the statement that a large
and expensive new department would be required
was a third lie.

Some extracts from Mr. E. C. V. B. :
Last December I brought before the House the case of
Mr. Watson, who has been licensee of the Paragon. Hotel’
for 35 years. When the land on which the hotel stands wap
sold by the Crown manyyears ago, a certain promise was
made that his view of the water would not be interrupted.
One day I went with Mr. Watson to see the Prime Minis-
ter. The Prime Minister sent out a memorandum
requesting me to put jay. wishes in writing. But I said
I would not tolerate that sort of thing. Mr. Watson is-
more precious to me than any Prime Minister. ' Govern-
ments come and go, hut constituents remain for ever;
and I said that unless the Prime Minister was prepared
to receive Mr. Watson, I would move the adjournment of
the House. I have always found that threat very effec-
tive. The result was that the Prime Minister saw Mr.
Watson, and received us in a most gracious manner. But
we received no satisfaction from the Government, and
when the time came I voted against the Government in
the House to show my disapproval of their action. I
then went to Mr. Watson with Hansard, and I said to
him, “ There is no vaporing or blank cartridge about me.
I was logical, and I followed up my protest by my vote,
and if you had been King of England I could
not have done more for you.” The result was
that Mr. Watson, who was strongly opposed' to me
at the last election, became my supporter. .\. . I
was made a scapegoat, but, thank God, I have lived it
all down, and I am now in this position : that I can look
every man straight in the face, because I have done
everything a man possibly can do to vindicate his posi-
tion. After I had severed my connection with the
'Government, I found that my credit in the city had gone

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Sing a song like ancient Homer,
Song of genuine bardic fire
Let it tell of noble conflict,
Champing steeds and hero pyte.
Sing it, with a voice of rapture*
Till the hearers sing as well,
Till we catch a noble chorus, ✓
Like a thunder pealing o’er us
Till the chant of dauntless heroes rings like distant
ocean swell! ”
’Twas the band of little people, .
They who live by truck and trade,
Cried unto the casual minstrel
And he hearkened as they bade ;
And he took his lyre and smote it
In the face of all the throng,
And he smote it till he hushed them
Into silence, when he crushed them
With the fury and the laughter and the'loathing
of his song.
“ Sing a song (ye cry) of Homer !
Homer flung his ballad-peal
In the face of brawny warriors
Who had heard the clang of steel;
Not in alleys mean.and narrow
Did he chant his splendid lay,
But he sang to sturdy yeomen,
Gallant chiefs and brawny bowmen
On the hillsides, in the valleys, of the .Amland far
away.
“ What would ye with glorious Homer ?
Would ye deal him out a dole,
If he sang the things ye treasure
In your little peddling soul ?
Would ye pay him weekly wages
For his rnetred minstrel time? v
Would ye make a paltry proffer
Of your farthings—would ye offer
Of jwiir pence and smallest silver when ye heard
ihassong sublime ?
“ When he called the steeds to battle
Ye would dream of vending ware,
Ye would wander to your ledgers y‘ ' .
When he blew his battle blare ; -
Ye would bid him sing of profits
And of bullion by the ton,
And his song would be forgotten
While ye clavered all of cotton
And the doom of mighty Hector find you yawn-
ihg every one ! ”
And the little trading people 4,
Held their heads a moment so.
And they whispered soft together : -r
“He is crazy—let us go !
Let us back to mart and counter,
Let us set the trader’s gin,
Let our hearts be close and chary
Let us watch in manner wary,
And when strangers come to greet us—let us take
the strangers in ! ”

ARE THEY TO BE
A PROFIT OR PEST?
It rests entirely with our settlers whether it shall cost them and the State a large amount'
annually to continue the war which has been waged against “ bunny ” for years past,
whether they will take the trouble to turn him into an asset which will bring a steady
revenue to the country and provide employment for THOUSANDS of its people. The
Rosewarne Packing Company, Ltd., is now receiving rabbits at its works in Young,
Narandera and Sydney. The highest market price is given in return for freshly-killed
rabbits free of any commission or charges. F „ furlher addm,-

——
A Sydney Lyceum play, which includes a
bathroom scene, with the principal comedian in
the bath, makes out a prirhd facie case for favor-
able consideration. Not that the bath has any-
thing to do with the story ; so far as that goes,
Bert Bailey might have crawled up the chimney,
or gone down to Melbourne to back something for
the Caulfield, and “ Her Second Time on Earth ”
would have moved along to the same gory conclu-
sion. Unfortunately, when the author put the
comedian into the bath he just about exhausted
his stock of original ideas. Even the name, it
turns out, is a bit of a fraud. It isn’t anybody’s
second time on earth. It is all, in fact, about the
inopportune reappearance of Inman’s old wife just
as he has married a new sweetthing. The old wife,
having a husband or two on hand already, isn’t really
Inman’s wife ; but he doesn’t know that. Still,
it is about time Inman began to have his suspicions
about wives who turn up in this way ; they have
done it so often, and there hasn’t been a genuine
one in the whole crowd. Until he does find out
the truth, Inman, as usual, has a bad time, for
what particular mischief Miss Duggan can’t think
of, her friend Willard suggests. Most times
they dip pretty deeply into Inman’s purse ; this
time, however, they don’t—but that’s the other
barrel of the story. Captain G.P. Carey has been
away making a fortune in unspecified parts, and
is taking it home in his cabin ; and nobody knows
that,he has it. Inman’s beautiful first wife (who
isn’t his wife) happens to be on board, makes love
to the captain, womans out his secret (this verb
is usually misprinted “ worms”), drugs him, shoves
him overboard, thinks him dead, and gets away
with the fortune. But he isn’t dead; a passing
liner picks him up—mad. Now, by one of
those remarkable coincidences that occur
oftener at the Lyceum than anywhere
else in Australia, Inman’s second wife is the
daughter of the captain whom his first wife has
treated in this unladylike way. And that turns
out badly for the lady ; for the captain is naturally
taken to'Tnman and his daughter, and he sees the
lady as she hangs round to do more mischief; then
he recovers, and recognises her; so virtue and
Inman are again triumphant, and the curtain falls
on Miss Duggan swallowing poison and Willard
mortally wounded by a shot fired from the wings,
the proper gun, held by Miss Duggan, having
refused to go off.
* \ * * *
G. P. Carey, for the second time this
season, makes his part the most conspicuous
feature. The quiet of Carey’s madness Comes
as a blessed relief; and his pathetic but
futile efforts to remember things that he has
a sudden, fleeting idea he ought to remember
area striking part of an exceedingly well-preseuted
anff interesting character. The rest of the co.
just about as well as usual—perhaps a little
better, especially the cab-horse that draws Inman
toi h,is wedding. This horse, on the opening night,
slipped on the stage and fell under the cab; and
it lopked as though, the ceremony would have to
be hung up long: enough to allow Miss Duggan
and Willard,, wrho were i,n the neighborhood, to.
overtake thp contents of the vehicle; but matters
were, arranged. This part of the proceedings
(watched by one of the largest and most excited
audiences of : the season) will probably not be
repeated ; but the other parts will be continued
until further notice.
*• # * *
“ Man to Man,” as produced at Sydney
Palace by the Fitzmaurice Gill Co., must needs
suffer by comparison with previous representations
of the same piece on a much larger stage, and with
a co. which was one of the best of melodramatic
organisations. But the difficulties in placing
a piece of this character on a small stage are
mastered very well--the large “sensational ef-
fects” are crowded in without wearing the
crushed expression that might be expected, and
since the first night the co. has played
without a hitch, and its performance has shown
a great deal of merit. The prison scene and the
big railway disaster are especially well-managed,
and to crowd a railway disaster of such magni-
tude into so small a space and yet make it look a
real thing—not a toy train crumpled up in a band-
box—is something of an achievement. The comely
Miss Fitzmaurice Gill is not seen at her best as
Ethel Maythorne; the cares of management seem
to sit somewhat heavily on her and interfere with
her acting. A lady with a railway disaster on
her mind has something pretty serious to think
about. In the humorous-villainous part of Bill
Burkeley, Gus Neville is very successful; and
Albert Gran is effective at times as Philip Ormond,
though he shows a tendency to over-act in places
“ Man to Man ” has been received very cordially
so far.
*# * *
The reliable “It is Never Too Late To
Mend,” which Charles Reade wrote with the
object of exposing some of the brutalities of the
old prison system, was revived for Alfred Dam-
pier’s last week at Sydney Criterion. Both the
novel and the drama were written just at the time
when the first gold discoveries were being made
in Australia; and Tom Robinson (Dampier),
the man who is Mending, comes out to
Bathurst, or thereabouts, to live his new life.
Now, Reade knew a great deal about the gaols
of England, but he didn’t know much about the
goldfields of Australia; so when we find the re-
formed Dampier and the spotless young man,
Vivian Edwards, coolly striking a prospectors’
camp, and making the diggers unload all the gold
in their possession, we begin to have doubts
about the reformation, though the dramatist
apparently had no idea that that wasn’t
quite the usual way of doing things in
Australia. Also, the Australian diggers
who, having found gold, are disturbed by
Dampier and his mate, are armed, every man
of them, with bowie knives —and that wasn’t
quite Australian either. These reflections on the
manners of Australia are not Dampier’s fault,
though, and they are not Charles Holloway’s.
Dampier did his part thoroughly, playing with all
his old captivating ease and breeze ; while Hollo-
way mounted the piece admirably. The set
showing the creek where the gold discovery
was made was especially good. Several others
did better than usual—Miss Beatrice Holloway,
as the small boy-victim of the gaol Bystem (on
the whole, Miss Holloway is a better ragged boy
than anything else so far); likewise V. Edwards,
and chaplain Ashton King; while Miss Conroy
put in good work as she always does. This Satur-
day Charles Holloway himself makes his first
appearance for this season in “ The Shaughraun”—
another romantic play of which this paper seems
to have heard favorably before.
** * *
Fisk Jubilee Singers are having such good
audiences at their concerts that it looks as if they
would have a Saturday night lien on the Town
Hall for many weeks to come. “He Moves in
the Middle of the Air ” does not refer to an
aeronaut. It has a sacred significance and is a
melodious chorus in which the company takes
part. “Religion is a Fortune” is another sooth-
ing song. “Elijah” Dowie and others could
make affidavit as to its truth.
** * *
“Toreador,” at Her Majesty's, Sydney,
will run yet another two or three weeks. Some
seasonable racing gags are introduced by Lauri,
and Celia Ghiloni has got a new song: “That’s
What I Want to Know.” There is about as much
melody in the song as there is in the crowing of a
bantam, but the knowledge the lady seeks is of
an amusing and faintly blue nature. She “ wants
to know,” for one thing, how an attractive young
laundress can wear diamonds and keep a motor
car on the proceeds of the laundry. Personally
speaking, writer is quite sick of so much Sir
Archibald Slackitt. R. Roberts, who takes the
part of the drawlsome baronet, has a most agree-
able voice when he lifts it in song ; pity he can-
not sing some of the drivel he drawls. But as
there is so much talking in the piece it were well
that the talkers speak intelligibly. Pat Bathurst
and Beattie often drop into patter speech, or bite
their words to death, or smother ’em. For the
rest, the superb staging is as it was; Florence
Young’s singing of “My Toreador” is as full of
fire and fine notes as ever; and the Spanish dance,
with scarves, by four handsome girls, deserves
this rather belated mention.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli the return of Fuller and
Hallen —American sketch artists—does not serve
to, withdraw any attention from Marzella and her
wonderful birds. Pretty nearly all the attention
that the audience has about it is required for
Marzella and her heap of strange fowl. Some
new arrivals are promised at this theatre shortly
—including a female Sandow with the physique of
Magog. There is a disappointed impression
abroad that, for obvious reasons, her display of
muscle will not be as complete as was Sandow’s.
** * *
“ The World,” staged for twelve nights, is
far from being Bland Holt’s most effective produc-
tion of that old-established melodrama, but it will
suffice to fill time whilst a perfectly new show is in
the throes of preparation. There are a good many
stirring occurrences in “The World,” and the
excitement begins early, which is better than
having the first act padded with a mild birthday
party, or a prolonged love scene in the vicinity of
a “ dear old home.” Nobody has a dear old home,
neither does Baker play the hero. Albert Norman
is the hero for a change, and Holt is the sham-
nosed Mossy who plants an infernal machine on
the ship which bears most of the other principal
characters from Capetown to England. When the
infernal machine has blown them on to a raft in
mid-ocean, it happens that a casual parent with
many diamonds concealed about his person confides
a heap of jewels and a long-lost daughter to the
care of Albert aforesaid. Then the poor old
gentleman dies of thirst, and the survivors get
mixed up in London. A nice girl from the raft is
engaged to marry Albert’s wicked brother,
whereas the brother is already married to the
rather bad girl who has inherited the diamonds
Another man from the raft covets those diamonds,
also the bad brother and Bland Holt are anxious
to remove Albert the Good. The first result of
these complications is the murder of a villain by
mistake; secondly Albert is confined in a lunatic
asylum where the keepers lure him on to deeds
of violence; thirdly, the bad brother falls down
the lift hole at a hotel; and, finally, the rather
bad girl dies in a ball dress. Her decease seems
quite unnecessary, but it leaves her at liberty to
join her late husband in some place beginning
with H. Bland Holt is likewise dead, or within
easy distance of the gallows, when the curtain
falls; so, take it altogether, “ The World ” is very
strong in retributive justice. Miss Harrie Ireland,
as the penitent adventuress, puts a reasonable
amount of earnestness into her work. Miss
Brightie Smith plays a trim-built youth in an
almost convincing manner, and Brown, who offers
a murderous suggestion to the polite villain, is an
impressive solicitor. Bland Holt is a popular
Mo3sy Jewell, as before, though he doesn’t seem
to make as much of the part. The other parts
are not very well played, Albert Norman being a
conspicuously unsuccessful hero on account of his
unnatural voice and weird, peculiar grin. Mrs.
Holt and Walter Baker are standing out of the
proceedings.

Miss Nellie Stewart, who is reputed to
have gathered in a lot of money backing that
namesake of hers who won the Caulfield Cup,
returns.to Melbourne Princess to play a brief and
sudden season of “Sweet Nell ” during Melbourne
Cup week. With this added to the other attrac-
tions, and Bent’s train to take one to the scene, it
is difficult to see what more even the most grasp-
ing Cupite could expect.
* * * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), the light and
garish romantic comedy of “ Monsieur Beaucaire”
draws large audiences and pleases most people.
Silk clothes don’t make charming stories from a
mere man’s point of view, but a costume play
which positively reeks of the best Bath society in
the year 1735 is calculated to interest every woman
who has ever fought for refreshments at a
Gov’ment House ball, or devoured a love story in
the Family Herald. And the fact that it pre-
sents the best dressed member of the cast as
an irresistible Royal Highness is a matter for
rejoicing. “ Beaucaire ” is commonly said to be
a “ sweet ” play. According to an advertisement
quoted from the local evening journal it is “a 3
sweet as the scent of new-mown hay.” All the
characters are powdered, patched and heavily
perfumed, the men toy with lace-edged hand-
kerchiefs and walk through life as though it were
a minuet, and the sole subject of discussion
throughout the whole play is a supposed barber
who is thought to have contaminated the Bath
pump-room with his low-born presence. This is
the comedy that smells of new-mown hay 1 The
critic who detected rural fragrance in the
neighborhood of Beau Nash’s citadel would be
capable of gathering buttercups and daisies in
Zaza’s dressing-room. The performance at Her
Majesty’s has gained a little polish and “finish ”
since the first night, but it still leaves a lot
of better acting—which in this case mostly means
better airs, graces and deportment; especially
Deportment to be desired. The , minuet
is ill-done, and the Major, whose story excites the
ladies to strew red roses for Beaucaire to walk
upon, is not at all elegant about the legs. Eardley
Turner, as Beau Nash, hardly realises one’s con-
ception of a historical master of frills and cere-
monies, yet he and the young man who leads the
“ comic relief ” are much more at ease than the
other minor characters. The Duke of Winterset
is an ineffective card-sharper, and Julius Knight,
intelligent, painstaking, and handsome enough,
plays the unparalleled French hero with a rather
heavy English touch. Miss Maud Jeffries makes
the mistake of trying to get too much sympathy
for her character, which is only the character of a
supercilious Bath beauty deeply in love with her-
self, like the Lady of Lyons.

iff * * *
Fitzgeralds’ Circus, opening on Caulfield
Cup night, had a distinctly horsey audience. The
heroine of the night was Mdlle. Onra, piquant
poseuse of the silver wire, whose “ turn ’’ goes as it
went in Sydney. Mam’zelle trips in, dressed as if
for a fete, in pale-green muslin. The roses wreath-
ing Mam’zelle’s rustic hat are pink; her toy parasol
is parrot-blue. These alien colors, so far from
quarrelling, kiss one another, as it were, for
toilette and wearer are of the Frenchiest.
The vision floats on to the wire—train,
parasol, and all—posing most charmingly, and
in-ofessing to be shy. The vision is about to
undress on the silver wire. The sunshade is
tossed to an attendant; the hat follows. Off
comes the muslin gown, revealing a beautiful
under-bodice and a petticoat, blue like the para-
sol. The small, white vest is discarded, the blue
skirt should fly after it, but first night the run-
ningstring jibbed; and the jupon prudishly clung
to its owner. Circus-men, with bungling fingers,
pawed sheepishly at the tape-ends, but without
avail. Mam’zelle, in despair, ran to the end
of the wire, where she could lean against
an upright and’ wrestle with the too Puritanic
tie-lace—undress herself by force, so to speak,
At last, Beauty stood revealed in pink tights,
and, after all the exciting suggestiveness, the dis-
robed one was clothed as fully as most circus
fairies are when they start work. Mam’zelle is a
pretty woman, with ankles and wrists of surpris-
ing slimness in comparison with her otherdom.
Sensationalists, who take their pleasure thrill-
fully, are well catered for by the Fitzgeralds. A
suspenseful act is supplied by the “Flying
Meteors,” one of whom, blindfolded, and with his
head tied up in a bag, leaps from a swinging bar,
aloft, hangs by his mate’s hands, and swoops back
unseeifigly to the bar he started from. Gee-gee
Newhaven, who, at the Bendigo show (15/10/03),
made his record jump of 6ft. llin., continues
to shine as an Excelsior bounder. Manzoni’s
trick-dogs, Barn a to, the educated donkey, Gil-
leno, a musical joker, the Tylers, La Belle Rose,
and the clown that never dieth keep the ball roll-
ing. Elephant Lizzie shows as a more accom-
plished performer every time she visits Mel-
bourne, but, like the rest of her sex, her temper
sharpens along with her wits, and she grows more
and more an identity to be respected rather than
loved. Last time she eloped (in Gippsland, just
t’other day) she met a man and paused in her
flight to chastise him. The man hopped through
a fence and congratulated himself that the virago
was too fat to follow, whereupon she began to tear
down the barrier. But the moment Lizzie saw
her keeper, Zapf, coming she dropped her eyelids
and sighed heavily, and began to pose as a lonely,
home-sick, strayed-lamb kind of elephant.
** * *
For the alleged sake of charity, two crowds
of hardy amateurs have been enjoying themselves
at Melbourne Princess’s. “Bob Roy,” by the
Caledonian Society, was not a shocking maltreat-
ment of the queer old musical play. Bailie
Nicol Jarvie has, in his time, been many a worse
amateur than J. T. Pielcen, who exclaimed “Ma
conscience! ” (which is a diverting Scottish re-
mark) on the two nights when John MTntyre
didn’t star in his stead. The bold Macgregor’s
middle-aged wife was evidently a younger lady
than the damsel of the piece, and the old man who
fell out of bed made some palpable preparations
for the unexpected event; but any flaws in the
performance were nothing to laugh at. The
present historian has always regarded “Rob
Roy ” as a serious play when acted by profes-
sionals, and no feeling person could contemplate
last week’s ceremony with a dry eye.
* * * *
On Saturday the “Arion" Operatic
Society applied itself to a moderately tuneful
exposition of “The Mikado,” and made steady
improvement as the evening wore away. One
expects the chorus to be a redeeming feature of
an amateur opera co., but the volume of sound on
Saturday was an inferior volume with ragged
edges. Also the principal men, who have been
creditable amateurs on sundry bygone occasions,
were less creditable this time, especially in the
first act. They did some things well and other
things horribly, and where they were chiefly to
blame was in allowing their less experienced
colleague, Katisha, to appear on the stage with
the facial make-up of a spectral rag-doll. The
next band of amateurs will appear on Saturday,
with Purves, K.C., as interlocutor.

blossoming plum-tree. Her figure has caused much
discussion, and is the subject of a mildly con-
demnatory sub-leader in the Register. The nude
lady out in the open air is always regarded with a
certain amount of gloom in Adelaide.
The Wild Cat Column.
Westralia has had more surpluses than any
other State during the past 10 years ; and yet it has little
reason to be satisfied with its position. Its balance-sheets
have shown the following results :
Revenue. Expenditure.
1894 .. .. £681,246 £656,357 Surplus £24,889
1895 .. .. 1,125,941 936,729 „ 189,212
1896 .. .. 1,858,695 1,823,863 „ 34,832
1897 .. '.. 2,842,751 2,839,453 „ 3,298
1898 .. .. 2,754,747 3,256,912 Deficit 502,165
1899 .. .. 2,478,811 2,539,358 „ 60,547
1900 .. .. 2,875,396 2,615,675 Surplus 259,721
1901 .; .. 2,964,121 3,051,331 Deficit 87,210
1902 .. .. 3,354,123 3,151,427 Surplus 202,696
1903 .. .. 3,630,238 3,521,763 „ ' 108,475
The surpluses, it is true, totalled £823,123, while the
deficits total only £649,922, leaving a net surplus of
£173,201. The argument which is used to support a
borrowing policy is that the loans are necessary in order
to develop the resources of the country, the presumption
being that the country, when developed, will go on
yielding wealth indefinitely. But that argument, though
it might apply to an agricultural district, certainly
doesn’t apply to a mining field; for the more gold you
take out of a field the less there remains to be taken out.
Moreover, the early profits of a mining field are almost
invariably the greatest; the exceptions to the rule
are so few that they are not worth mentioning; and,
anyhow, the Westralian goldfields are not among them.
The past 10 years have thus seen the cream
skimmed from the goldfields; no less than £41,338,707
worth of gold was exported between July Ist, 1894, and
June 30th, 1903. By that much was the natural wealth
of the country reduced. Moreover, nearly the whole of
the profit derived from skimming the cream was sent out
of the country. Through the absence'of local industries,
even the money spent in equipping the mines and in
feeding and clothing the miners for the most part
vanished. Avery large amount of the surplus earnings of
the miners was also sent out of the State. Altogether, it is
hardly possible to conceive of circumstances under which
a State should have more strongly insisted upon its works
being paid for out of current revenue. Instead, however,
of doing this it has increased its net indebtedness (after
allowing for the sinking fund accumulated) by £11,693,421.
The State let the cream go, and now the bill will have to
be paid out of the skim-milk.
The State that could only just pay its wav even

The Pioneer Tin Mining Coy., of Bradshaw’s
Greek, Tasmania, which has had a most successful
career, has decided to increase its plant, and has
placed an order for a very large and powerful suction
dredge and appliances, with the Austral Otis Engineering
Company, South Melbourne, who built their original
plant. The new dredge will be fitted with a 15" sand -
pump, and a compound condensing vertical engine of
450 horse power, and two boilers of the marine type, for
a working pressure of 1601bs to the square inch. The
pontoon to carry this plant will be 60ft. x 50ft. by a depth '
of 5ft. The plant will be capable of raising 100 cubic ;
yards of tin drift per hour to a height of 80 feet into ■
sluice boxes. The new plant is very similar to the older
one but contains improvements in detail suggested by
the two years experience of the older plant. The latter
has done excellent and continuous service since its first
start. The average cost of raising tin drift to a height
of 70ft. during tne period above mentioned, including
cost of all repairs and renewals, has been less than 3d.
per cubic yard.*

British manufacturers have been beaten in ah
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

* », • *
Even if Westralia gets no worse a deficit than
.treasurer Gardiner foretells (so far, it looks as though
tiardmer s estimate will easily be almost doubled), its
record will not be far from the worst in Australian
history. At £230,147 its deficit would be equal to
us. 7d. per head of the population as at the commence-
", of the financial year. N.S. Wales reached its
j Heights when, in 1886, it managed to accumulate
a deficit of £1,484,462, being at the rate of £llos. 3d. per
bead. Victoria’s worst, or thereabouts, was in 1890, when
the gay boomsters let the State drift £1,126,578 to the
bad, besides adding 2£ millions to the ordinary debt ;-the
drift was equal to about 19s. lOd. per head. Queensland
was fruilty of £528,189 in 1901, or £1 os. 7d. per head, and
of £494,299 in 1890, which was equal to £1 ss. 2d. per
head. S. Australia has not done anything famous in this
way; while Tasmania managed £132,038 in 1892—equal
H® I'®- 6d. per head. Thus Westralia’s Treasurer quite
cheerfully looks forward to his State putting up very
nearly the worst Australian record. And He does it in
spite of the fact that Australia is supposed to have
learned a lesson, and in spite of professions that it Has
resolved for the future to go financially straight.
Honestly, just how much of the lesson has Australia
learned, and has it even seriously thought yet of going
straight ?
* * * *
N.S.W. State Savings Bank pays interest on
deposits only up to £200; but Treasurer Waddell has
casually dropped before the Assembly a proposal to raise
the limit to £SOO for ordinary depositors and to abolish
the limit altogether for friendly societies. At the same
time he even more casually mentioned that an agreement
had been made with the Citizens Life Cos. (chairman,
John See) for the lease of a building to be erected opposite
the Citizens’ office. Perhaps if it hadn’t been for the
latter part of the arrangement the Bill would not have
had a bad reception. But it came out that what had
been agreed with the co. was that the latter should put up
a building to cost £27,000, and charge £2OOO a year for 16
years, the State to then have the right to purchase build-
ing and land (value of land not quite certain) at £54,000.
That means, boiled down, tfiat the State borrows
£54,000 from the co. for 16 years at about 4£ per cent.
The Government cannot do any more straight-out bor-
rowing except on disastrous terms ; yet, so saturated is it
with Owe’Sullivanism, that it must Owe’Sullivani3e some-
how ; and when it can’t borrow in an ordinary way, it
must invent new ways of borrowing. And it doesn’t im-
prove the smell of the thing that the new style of borrow-
ing begins with a co. whose most prominent director is
also head of the Government.
So the mixing up of the Citizens’ with the busi-
ness leads to a close inquiry as to what the new Savings
Bank Bill itself is stuffed with. On the face of it, any
measure to make the Savings Bank more like what a
State Bank should be would commend itself; and a Bill
authorising it to accept and pay for deposits of over £2OO
ought to be good. But the Bill stops right there. It
doesn’t propose that the Savings Bank shall take over the
work of the State Advances to Settlers Board, and put it
on a proper footing. It doesn’t propose to make it free
of political control, as it must be —so free of political con-
trol that if it preferred, for the good business reason that
they yielded a better return, to buy the bonds of Victoria
or Maoriland instead of those of N.S. Wales, it should be
at perfect liberty to do so. It proposes, in fact, nothing
more than that money shall be encouraged to flow into
the Savings Bank ; and, as it doesn’t propose new ways
for it to flow out, the only possible presumption is that it
is intended to flow out in Owe’Sullivanism, because the
Government has command of all the bank’s unused
balances. Therefore this, too, becomes a mere round-
about way of State borrowing—another way of prolonging
the drunk even after the regular liquor-sellers have
declined to serve out any more poison. And the proceed-
ing is more odious than it would otherwise be because it
trails the good, democratic idea of a State Bank in the
gutter; it suggests nothing so much as the man who,
having pawned every stick of furniture, continues his
orgy Hy rushing his daughter’s charms into the market
place. That looks about as low as any Government can
get to ; but there is no telling—the present N.S.W.
Cabinet is a Government of great resource.
* * * *
A George Turner, jun., trained in the old man’s
office, might have delivered Queensland Treasurer Kid-
ston’s Budget speech. The Morgan-Browne Government
came in at the end of September ; and Kid3ton at once
set to work to measure up ex-Treasurer Crib H’s estimates
on the basis of the three months’ results. Cribb’s idea of
things was :
Estimated Revenue .. .. .. £3,680,000
Estimated Expenditure .. .. 3,702,144
Estimated Deficit £22,144
He reckoned to get £895,000 from the Commonwealth,
while Commonwealth Treasurer Turner calculated only
£791,090 —or £104,000 less. Kidston wrote to George
Turner, asking him whether, after the three months’ ex-
perience, he could not hold out hope of something Hetter
for Queensland ; and Turner replied that He couldn’t.
Kidston therefore followed Turner’s safe lead, and
reduced the estimate to £791,000 ; then revised tHe other
figures in the light of the quarter’s experience ; dealt with
the expenditure returns in the same way ; and finally pro-
duced this :
Estimated Revenue £3,495,000
Estimated Expenditure (on the basis
of Ist quarter) 3,687,476
Estimated Defieit .. .. £192,476
The deficit estimated by the Philp Treasurer meant only
something more added to the debt; the deficit shown by
Kidston means, he says, one of two things—more taxation
or less expenditure. The difference represents the
beginning of the whole difference between honest finance
and the other sort. More taxation or less expenditure,
Kidston says ; and the House says it must not He more
taxation. Queensland certainly does already pay con-
siderably more per head than any other State in the
Commonwealth except Westralia, whose circumstances
are exceptional ; in fact, Queensland was paying £3
Is. Bd. as against the £2 16s. lOd. of N.S. Wales, the next
highest, even before the poll-tax was put on. Still, this
can’t be helped ; if a State will have the Biggest Debt on
Earth, sunk in things that don’t yield anything or in rail-
ways that yield little more than half the interest, it must
pay for the honor. (So far this year the interest has
sucked up 51 per cent, of the total revenue received 1)
Blocked, then, from wiping out the deficit by
taxation, the Coalition has to rely on reduced ex-
penditure ; and how it is going to _ reduce it
enough it has not yet quite decided. It is a tough
problem—tougher than it looks, because the savings
can’t be made out of the whole £3,687,476. No less than
£1,550,000 has to be paid to Bull-Cohen as interest; and,
whoever else has to be pinched, Bull-Cohen must be paid.
Further, Queensland last year spent £1,022,405 of loan
moneys, which was much less than usual; for years it
has paid out of loans for many services which ought to
have been paid for out of revenue ; and as the Coalition
does not intend to borrow anything—does not at present
even intend to try and place the unsold portion of the
last loan authorised—it will have to pay out of revenue
for things which its predecessors paid for out of borrow-
ings. Truly, the loan policy of the past has left the
honest financier with a hard row to hoe. Yet it must be
hoed. The only alternative is further drift; and that can
mean one, and only one, thing—ultimate repudiation.

* * * *
That great, noble, robust, upstanding thing,
Private Enterprise, is at it again. A while ago a syndi-
cate that said it had any amount of money and energy at
command obtained authority from Queensland Parlia-
ment to build a railway from Normanton to Cloncurry.
It, of course, didn’t build a mile of railway; it was the
Adelaide Tramway syndicate and the Tasmanian Great
Western Railway syndicate over again. It now proposes
that it should build the line with borrowed money— the
State guaranteeing the interest ; in other words, that the
State shall stand the loss when there is one, and the
syndicate shall get the profit when there is one. And
there is quite a hostile feeling in the lair of Capital
because the Morgan-Browne Ministry declines to enter-
tain this impudent demand for State interference with
Private Enterprise.

The reserves now consist of £24,126 contingent reserve,
£3OOO reserve fund (anew creation, to which it is proposed
to add steadily), and the balance undivided profit. The
contingent fund is held specially against property, in the
realisation of which there may be a loss. There is very
little to show how this realisation is panning out, because
until 12 months ago present value of loans and book value
of foreclosed properties were bunched into one item. In
the past 12 months, however, properties having a book
value of £2926 have been realised, while the contingency
fund has been reduced by £853 ; hut it is not certain that
the whole of this was loss on the transactions. As, how-
ever, the total book value of foreclosed properties is now
down to £65,931, a contingent fund of £24,126, which
would allow of a depreciation of 7s. in the £, should be
ample. It is further in favor of the Langridge that it is
working almost exclusively on its own capital, on which
its year’s earnings have been equal to a highly respectable
£5 4s. per cent. The only disquieting feature is that the
directors excuse themselves for only paying 3J per cent,
again. Directors of a co. with a capital of about £77,000,
and reserves which probably don’t amount to more than
£6OOO, need not excuse themselves for not paying more
than £3 10s. out of £5 4s. ; but they would have to excuse
themselves very hard if they paid a cent more.
* * * *
“V.”: During the present session of M.L.
Parliament, 84 petitions were presented, asking that the
Bank of New Zealand be taken over bodiiv by the Govern-
ment and turned into a State hank. The Public Petitions
Committee, after brooding on the subject for a while,
reported suddenly, t’other dajq that it was unable to
recommend the absolute taking over of the bank, but
suggested that whatever State support is granted at the
end of the present arrangement should take the form of a
partnership, the State joining as chief shareholder, and
taking a proportionate control of the management of the
institution. This is the system on which the Bank of
Norway is run, and it has proved very successful in that
cold country.

The paid capital is £250,000, on which, combined with the
reserves as at the commencement of the year, the profit,
before the writings-off for depreciation, was equal to .just
under a very moderate 44 per cent. The principal item
in the balance-sheet is “ properties, buildings, machinery,
plant, goodwill, &c., £213,938”; and, by reason of the
inclusion of this goodwill paper item, the reserves are
described above as nominal. Further, the item (the
amount of which is not disclosed) is not being written
off ; all the writings-off are directed to machinery and
freehold property. To go on year after year including
goodwill as an asset is bad enough ;to bunch it with
other items is worse still, and the habit should be sup-
pressed. The co. is still working on a heavy overdraft
(£39,230), together with £10,643 of deposits; but the
amount is not increasing very rapidly. Twelve months
ago the net indebtedness on these accounts was £39,298 ;
on September 30th of this year it was £43,235, but the
increase is all represented by cash on deposit.
* * * *
Silver has moved steadily up to 2s. 4d.; last
year’s average was a fraction under 2s. ; even in the early
part of this year it was down to Is. 9d. and one-sixteenth.
Threepence per oz. means, on the Broken Hill Prop.’s
present output, about £28,000 per half-year to that com-
pany alone. The improvement is probably due to an
increased demand from India, the Straits, and China, but
whether it is likely to be permanent it is impossible to
say. The market is likely to be a little better than it has
been during the last year or two; but there is no pro-
spect of any substantial rise.
* * * ♦
Broken Hill Prop, can do remarkable things
when it tries. In the last four weeks an effort was made
to wipe out the shortage in the returns due to the July-
August water famine, and the result was an out-turn of
5090 tons of lead and 366,1160 z. of silver—at the rate of
66,000 tons of lead a year, equal to about one-seventh of
the whole world’s production.

The Sydney promoter of what London Critic
calls “an obvious wild cat ”is issuing with the
prospectus an open letter, purporting to be signed
“ John See, Premier and Colonial Secretary of the
State of New South Wales in the Commonwealth
of Australia,” and running that the bearer “ con-
templates visiting Great Britain on business matters,
and it will be regarded as a favor by my Government if
any attention or information can be afforded him which
might assist in accomplishing the object he has in
view.” Did Premier See, if he wrote that letter,
find out what the traveller’s “ object ” was ?If
he didn’t, how could he say that the Government of
the State would regard it as a favor for persons to
assist in accomplishing it. London Critic refers to some-
one who is co-operating with the visitor as “ a notorious
individual,” and adds that the fact that the letter is being
used by this man “to inveigle unsophisticated British in-
vestors into what is obviously a wild-cat scheme should
make Premier See a little more careful.”

Another of the rare finds of opal which run
into thousands has been made by a small co-operative
party working on the English company’s blocks at White
Cliffs (N.S.W.). The blocks are now worked on pay-
ment of a small monthly fee, so the hopeless co., which
has not got within coo-ee of a dividend for years, makes
nothing directly out of such hauls.
• * * ,
J. B. Jaquet has been appointed Chief
Inspector of Mines in N.S.W. in succession to W. H. J.
Slee ; and the appointment is likely to cause ill-feeling.
For one thing, Jaquet has previously had no experience
in this branch, and the fact that men who have had years
of experience in it have been passed over isn’t likely to
add to the popularity of the service. The Department
has had great difficulty hitherto in retaining its best men ;
two of the seniors lately went out—a mining company
took one of them at double the salary the State
paid him. In future, as a result of this blocking
of promotion, the Department is less likely than
ever to retain its best men, and mining accidents
will probably increase in consequence. For another
thing, the only mining the new Chief Inspector of Mines
has ever done was for 12 months as a cadet in Cornwall—
presumably the 12 months which it is necessary for a
student to put in in order to get a London School of
Mines’ diploma. It is of very great importance that
the Chief Inspector should have the confidence of both
managers and miners, and 12 months’ service in a Cor-
nish mine as a student-cadet isn’t at all likely to give it.
Asa geological surveyor Jaquet has done very good work,
and there is much work of the same kind still waiting for
him to do. To take him from that and pub him on the
other job is like taking a good chemist and putting him
in charge of aP. and O. liner because he knows the com-
ponent parts of salt water.
* * * *
All Adelaide’s pet gambles for the past 12 or 18
months have had a shocking ending. Stannary Hills,
boomed beyond half-a-million four months ago, are back
to £130,000. The whole crate of Arltunga cats are
nominally worth £5900 now; six months ago they
represented £300,000. Smith’s Creek has gone from
pounds to pence in 12 months. Practically all the copper
specs., floated when the metal jumped over £6O, are dead ;
and the oil boom, which showed its nose over the hills,
never got any further.
* • • •
Field’s Reward, Yalgoo (W.A.), which was re-
constructed in February last, i 3 now under the control of
W. H. Hobby, well known in the eastern States. The co.
is in 320,000 shares of 10s. each, of which 313,750 are
issued paid up to Bs. 6d. After reconstruction the co.
started with £7BOO odd, cash in hand, and has still £23,500
to call up. It owns an old 20 head mill and 36 acres of
land. The old co. treated some thousands of tons of
14dwt. ore, and spent £21,000 cash, in addition to pro-
ceeds of gold won, before landing on the rocks. Whether
the venture under present management will repay outlay
is a moot point.
• * * *
Paddington Consols (W.A.) has apparently seen
its best days, and is quite unable to provide stone to keep
the mill fully employed. Barring sinking the main shaft
from the 500 ft. to the 700 ft. level, by contract, very litt #
development is being 1 done. Unless the; next level shows
a vast improvement the life of the co will last just as
long as current funds.
• •« » *•
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), is a good
example of too many cooks spoiling the broth. All ex-
perts who have seen the property agree that it is a
good one, but it has never been a success. The
mill recently erected is a ramshackle concern, bought
at a low price; but by the time it was erected it cast!
really more than a first-class mill could have been erected .
for. Now it is found that there is insufficient water to
run full time—a fact which might easily have been fore-
seen. Yet the directorate consists of mine managers and
ex-mine managers. Until water is Obtainable the mine
can never do itself justice, and will certainly never be in
a position to earn divs. ; and running the mill one shift is
simply wasteful.
The consignment of the old 50-head stampers
of the Lake View Consols (W.A.) to the scrap heap will
come as a relief to the staff. The 25-head mill erected
by manager Hartmann just prior to his dramatic exit,
together with the new 50-head now on order, should
treat about 8000 tons monthly. As the grade of ore will
be considerably lower, the current yield of, gold is not
likely to be exceeded. Last year, according to the
monthly reports, the total local costs panned out at 39s.,
but the yearly accounts showed 75s. Unless a very
material reduction can be effected, it is impossible to see
how lOdwt. ore is to meet current expenses. Mine is
now being opened up at 1400ft., and the main
shaft is on its way down to 1600ft., where the
next level will be opened up. The co. has paid
£1,312,500 in divs., but none have been forthcoming for
two years, and, unless a vast improvement occurs in the
grade of ore, it is impossible to see where any margin of
profit is to come from.

,The High Court should keep clear of the
adversely - criticised practices of State courts.
Chief Justice Griffith was not warm in his seat
when he appointed as his Associate, at £3OO a
year, his son, E. P. T; Griffith. In such cases
the Judge simply quarters his son, who may or
may not be competent, on the taxpayers.
“ Werrimee”: London journals are already
exercised with regard to a name for the Federal
capital, and with the Britisher’s usual originality
in matters of nomenclature, as shown in New
South Wales, New Zealand, New Guinea, New
Caledonia, New England, New Ireland, New
Britain, New York, &c., such abominations as
Londona, New London, &c., are suggested. The
name of Tumut is considered a monstrosity ; yet
England teems with such malformations as Chip-
ping Norton, Pokestogg-on-the-Hike, &c, One
Godfrey Burchett, “son of a pioneer colonist,”
proffers his views—a sample of many :
Might not the new capital become Alexandra, a lasting
memorial to one of the most gracious figures in British
history, wife of a king who has the interests of all his
subjects at heart, and mother of a Prince who under-
stands and sympathises with the spirit of the colonies ?
George Pile understands and sympathises with the
spirit of the cawlonies much more than the prince-
ling can possibly do, so why not Georgepile, or
Pilesdog, or Dogpile, or Pilegeorge ? Again :
Henry Parkes was among the “ Fathers of Federation ”
(the curse of having too many fathers), so why not
Parkestown ; Lord Hopetoun was your first Governor-
General, so why not Hopetown ?
What about our explorers, the first man to settle
on the site, or the aboriginal monarchs of the dis-
trict ; are they not more entitled to the honor
than Lord Hopetoun ? Besides, Queensland
already has a Hopetown, and Victoria has a
Hopetoun, and Westralia has a Hopetoun, and
S. Australia had one, but it died ; and there are
other Hopes kicking about —mostly in despair.
It is noted a little hopefully that some of our
legislators are patriotic enough to put Australia
first, and are putting aside their Bibles and Han-
sards, for the time being, to study aboriginal
dictionaries with a view to obtaining a name that
is both euphonious and appropriate.

A passer of valueless cheques with a mar-
vellous steel-plated cheek turned up in the Vic-
torian criminal court ’tother day. He had been
convicted nine times. Asked if he had anything
to say, he remarked: “If it were not for the
facilities offered to commit crimes of this sort I
would not be here awaiting sentence. Hotel-
keepers and others are far too ready to accept
valueless cheques.” The injured ring in his
tone as he lectured the people he had swindled
sounded like the cry of an importer, who had
diddled the Customs, complaining of the facilities
offered by the department for passing off goods
under their proper value. It was the Rev. Dr.
Marshall who, when a “respectable firm of inir
porters,” the members of which were members of
his congregation, was potted for Customs swind-
ling, alleged that the Federal Parliament, was
“making laws which manufactured crime.” Ap-
parently, the Victorian steel-plated person is of
the same opinion about the law as to valueless
cheques.
Lunacy is still on the up grade in Victoria.
The annual report of the Inspector-General of
Asylums for 1902 shows that there were 4547
lunatics under his charge on December 31 as
against 4501 at the beginning of the year.
Out of 784 lunatics admitted during the year
the Victorian natives numbered 357 —a very
small proportion considering that the
native born are nearly three-fourths of the
population. There were 96 English, 83
Irish and 30 Scotch. The religious table
shows 246 Anglicans, 243 Roman Catho-
lics, 102 Presbyterians, 69 Wesleyans,
24 Lutherans, seven Pagans, four
Hebrews, and one Mahommedan. The
ratio of lunatics to the whole population
rose from lin 268 to lin 265. The
age table shows that the greatest num-
ber of admissions take place between
the ages of 30 and 35 years, and the
greatest number of deaths between
40 and 45 years.
Everything seems to be in the con-
spiracy to freeze out the last traces of
the Jingo fever in Maoriland. W"ar
medals have been at a discount for
months past; but the warrior who wore
the D. 8.0. continued to expand his
manly bosom, until the Royal decree
came along that the R.V.O. ranks ahead
of it. And as the R.V.O. has already
been awarded to the head steward for the
nice way in which he served out ice-
cream at the State ball at Delhi, as well
as to the German hotelkeeper who made
up a parcel of sandwiches for the Duke of Con-
naught before he went out tiger-shooting, the man
who simply owns a D.S.O. has begun to seriously
reconsider his position, and his head isn’t nearly
as swollen as it was this time two years ago.
Melbourne University authorities propose
to celebrate the jubilee of their institution, and
discover that they are as ignorant of its birthday
as if it had been found on a doorstep in infancy,
with a note pinned to its clothes urging some
kind old female to take it in and.bring it up on
the bottle. The Act of incorporation was passed
in 1853, aud it is thought that teaching was com-
menced shortly afterwards ; but it was not till ’5B
that the Sovereign was memorialised to recognise
Melbourne University degrees, and it was ’59
before letters patent in answer to that prayer
were granted. It is proposed to split the differ-
ence with an axe or some other handy weapon,
and make the jubilee year 1906.

“ Notice : Should the petitioner
or respondent contract marriage before
the decree shall be made absolute,
then such contracting party will be
guilty of bigamy.” Such is the
warning now printed on every
decree nisi pronounced by the
Divorce Court in Maoriland. The
law does' not require it, but the
Judges have adopted it as a matter
of practice, for the reason, as one Judge
put it recently, that the anxiety of one of the
parties to, get free is generally due to a desire “to
run the risk of another shot at it.”
• • * •
Casterton (Vic.) has produced—and just
lost —a remarkable girl. She was 16 when she
died. Her mother told her to darn her own
stockings. She protested, and as the mother
insisted, went into her bedroom and consumed
the contents of a small bottle of strychnine kept
bv her father for poisoning foxes. The girl who
will take her life rather than mend holes in her
hosiery is certainly something new. If she had
been asked to darn anyone else’s stockings she
would probably have slain most of the population
of Shiels’ electorate.
* * * •
The judicial demand that there should be
sworn evidence that Pitt-street is a street, or The
Block a public thoroughfare, sometimes strikes as
absurd. Yet it is not a bad principle that the
Judge should strive to know as little as possible
outside of what is directly sworn to him. “Well
any sensible man knows that,” said a Judge at
Broken Hill the other day, “In the street there,
I see a newspaper office with the Silver Age
advertised on it, and I naturally know that a
newspaper of that name is published there.” And
then the Court roared. The Silver Age had
stopped publics lion years previously.
UNNATURAL AUSTRALIAN HISTORY.—
THE SHORT SUNFISH.
Orthaqoricus (extraordinary cuss). —This order derives
its name from the curious structure of the jaws,
which are fixed together in a very peculiar manner.
The short sunfish has been frequently seen on almost
all parts of our coast. It is of a most singular shape,
looking as if three-fourths of a very large fish had
been cut off, leaving only the top half—like a marine
Baron Munchausen’s horse. It attains to a very
large size, round, and has been known to weigh
30ulbs., its length being only 4ft. sin. It lives mostly
at the bottom of the sea (of Freetrade), but frequently
rises to. the surface, and lies, perhaps asleep— but
always lies — floating. When struck, it uses very
desperate, but exceedingly ludicrous, efforts to
escape.. It is not good to eat.
A FRIEND IN NEED.
THE CHICKEN : “ Thanks, awfully, I’ve been try-
ing to get out all the week.”
It didn’t matter much, if it mattered at
all, how many pamphlets that amiable Melbourne
crank, Bookseller Cole, published in Australia on
the wickedness of this, continent in looking un-
kindly upon yellow immigration ; but the fussy
old meddler has now gone to Japan, where he has
published in Japanese, and widely scattered, a
treatise of the same silly and superficial character
inviting local people—“editors and other thinkers”
especially—to say what they think of the Aus-
tralian wickedness. For a start, some of the
“editors and thinkers” are telling Cole things
that he hardly expected to hear. One of
Cole’s great points is that man is one, only for
the color of his skin —“a color which everywhere
has been dyed upon his flesh by God’s glorious
orb, the sun” —this is addressed to a people who
are accustomed to see the snow four inches deep
in Tokio, and who recently had several
hundred soldiers killed by frost, but whom
Cole evidently takes for a tropical race
fried brown.” To that the Yokohama Daily
Advertiser says the local equivalent for “Rats!”
and asks Cole to “ remember that the Eskimo
denizens of the zone of the midnight sun are not
described by travellers as pale-faces.” The Japan
Advertiser says that “nis free and easy refer-
ences to the ignorance, &c., of the white man in
Australia may not impossibly rouse unexpected
emotions in the breast of the brown man of
Japan,” and adds, “Mr. Cole will leave for home
on the 17th inst. It may be pointed out that we
advised him to do so some days ago.”
OUR BROTHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bishop of Carpentaria says :“I have just returned
from a most hopeful missionary journey to the aboriginal
station reserve on the Mitchell River. The people are yet
unspoiled by drink, opium, or disease, and we have a
great future'before us.” He proposes that an expedition,
in which shall be included troopers, shall start for those
happy hunting grounds.— Daily paper.
The Bishop wandered northward and he struck on virgin
soil,
Way upon the Mitchell River, where the heathen do
not toil;
Where they dwell in naked modesty, and never deem it
sin,
And extend unto the stranger a simple, friendly fin.
There no pub, nor gaol, nor church is ;
There no social gimblet searches,
And no reeling soaker lurches
In that happy, northern clime.
’Tis a Land of Milk and Honey, as fair Canaan was of old,
Ere Joshua’s freebooters stole the corn and wine and gold.
There the grenadilla blossoms and the ruddy pine grows
wild,
And you find the fried bear pleasant, and the ’possum’s
good when biled.
There life a thing of ease is ;
There no virulent disease is ;
There the furtive ’gator seizes
Upon Nature’s happy child.
We will hasten there and teach the .heathen murder is a
, sin.
We will sell to him the axe with which he shouldn’t smite
; his gin.
We’ll take him rum, and tell him all the evil it has done
(Haw can he learn to shun it till he has some drink to
shun?)
Men should learn to love each other,
But we can’t convert our brother
From shooting at his mother
When he hasn’t any gun.
yic..
HET THULE.
THE HEATHEN IN THE SLUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He drinks a stinted draught of light,
lie breathes a breath of fetid air,
His day is reft of visions bright,
His youth is carked with sordid care ;
A human wolf in city lair.
He grows with soul and senses numb
To all that’s good and true and fair—
Ih: scunted heathen in the slum !
He knows the lore of vicious Night,
He kens the crew the gutters bear,
His eves are blear, his form is slight,
His forehead hid in towzled hair ;
He sprang from some degenerate pair
Whose souls were stricken deaf and dumb,
And so he grew—a human tare—
The stunted heathen in the slum !
Yet some day—if I read aright—
His savage kin shall do and dare,
S isll swing the blazing torch with might,
And light the town with hellish glare ;
Shall dance on all our treasures rare
While Ruin beats her rolling drum,
And princes die beneath the blare
Of shouting heathens in the slum !
May Heaven forefend that we should share
A fate so black in days to come !
Oh ! Hear the voice that bids, “ Beware
The stunted heathen in the slum ! ”

. The National Service League ” —a new
British organisation to urge the vital importance
to the empire of compulsory naval or military
training, which is seeking members in Australia—
has an ingenious way of catering for the large
rabbit-brained section of humanity that has a
craze for titles and scraps of alphabet. You can
become a member for a guinea, but by sending
hve pounds you become an “Honorary Vice-
President. It ought to be worth the money—
Honorary V ice-President of the National Service
Eeigue! But if Alderman Biggins wanted to
be Honorary Emperor or Archbishop of the show
W 1 ij u tltle “His Tremendous Grace”—what
would that cost ? Writer inquires feelingly, having
a personal interest in these matters; he is himself
an hereditary honorary private in the Polish in-
surrectionary army when there is one. It came
about in this way. Writer’s father was a Con-
servative locally, but sympathised much with
liberty abroad, and gave his cheque manfully
to aid oppressed nationalities, red-shirted Gari-
baldi ans, and so forth. He handed the usual
donation to a Polish collection taken up in
England during the 1863 insurrection, and was
formally thanked and enrolled as something or
other—Friend of Oppressed Nationalities, or
something.. Anyhow, honorary private seems
abott the size of it, and writer reckons he inherited
the honor.

M’s.L.A. Ramsay and Williams have before
Victorian Assembly a brief but sudden measure
entitled the Barmaids Restriction Act, which sets
forth that no person of the female persuasion
shall henceforth attend to customers in or about
any licensed victualler’s premises, excepting said
person be the 'wife or daughter of a licensee or a
female licensee. The restriction is not to apply to
existing barmaids until January Ist, 1909, which
gives Polly five years in which to get married.
And if Polly can’t get married in five years, with
no new competitors coming into the business, she
is a slower girl than she looks.
»** * -
Melbourne’s 'Anglican bishop, Clarke,
lately bewailed the increasing tendency of wealthy
citizens to ignore the church when making their
wills. “ One of these wills not long
ago obtained probate at £106,000,
and not one penny was left to
the diocese for church purposes,”
wailed the bishop, “yet there was
scarcely a will made in Yorkshire
by a well -to- do person of the
mediaeval period that did not con-
tain a bequest for the building of
York Cathedral.” One fair infer-
ence might be that the church has
been found out in the interim.
Another way of putting it might be
that it is the church’s business to
convince people of the advisability
of leaving their money to cathe-
drals, and it has failed badly.
When people no longer pay to see
a show, the assumption is that
there is something wrong with the show—at
least, the actor and manager are expected to look
at the matter in that light. When J. C. William-
son or Alfred Dampier fall in they are not allowed
to threaten the public with the horrors of a -here-
after for not liking the show.

Years ago there was a great siege of
Truth office in Sydney, when A. G. Taylor
ran it.. -Another siege took place recently at
the Melbourne office, when old ex-P.M; Ogi'er
obtained a verdict of ,£5OO and costs against John
Norton. Justice Hodges refused stay of execu-
tion, unless a big sum was paid into court,
and Ogier sent a warrant of distraint. The
great John was at the time in Sydney, but his
manager erected fortifications and threw up
entrenchments. Papers were sold through the
iron bars of a window, and copy was received
through the same medium. There was a bum/
bailiff-in the front of the office and another at the
rear, but nothing very sensational happened.
The siege lasted a very short period. A cheque
was signed, and the prothonotary holds the
money pending the appeal which is promised.
* * * *
For years past the Victorian Government
has had a lightship off Williamstown, which
always breaks adrift when a storm arises and
dodges about like a will-o’-the-wisp, to the bewil-
derment of mariners. At length it has been
decided to replace it by a pile light, and the old
burlesque of a travelling lighthouse will soon be
a thing of the past.
A Melbourne parson has been lecturing on
" Purgatory Completely Upset.”
“Marat,” having collected his thoughts,
sends this :
This barbaric Eight Hours procession doesn’t make me
enthuse one bit. Went to look at it the other day, and
came away disgusted. Fat marshals, and vice-marshals,
who had never been on a horse since last Eight Hour
Day, led the way, and pushed people out of the path of
huge trollies full of the Fat Man’s goods. Slushing along
in the mud behind each load were scores of persons
stuffed full of pride because they, made the goods. The
fat one looked out of the window and cheered ; he was
getting a great advertisement. A couple of lions and
tigers were there in a cage. They also were slaves of the
big man, and it was quite right they should march with
the other toilers. One banner had a picture of a king,
archbishop, and laborer. The laborer was between the
two, and he looked rather crushed. A knacker’s cart
with a nearly dead horse on it finished the procession. I
fancy the horse represented Labor. I went home and
took a Bilious Beanseed. I felt bad.
* * * *
Australia breathed a great sigh of relief on
Monday when the cable brought the assurance
from Northcote, the new Governor-General, that
he intended
to render justice to all dwellers under the Southern
Cross.
There had been quite an amount of fear that he
might have inaugurated a regime of oppression
and spoliation, with knout accompaniment, over
Australia, S’America, S’Africa and other terri-
tories under the Southern Cross. But all that is
now removed. Perhaps G.,G. Northcote might
further add to Australian comfort by pledging him-
self that he will well and truly lay all foundation
stones submitted to him, and not hang by the neck
until they are dead such church bazaars as come
within his range of duties ; and, further, that he
won’t insist on using the Australian forces to
coerce the S’Americans if they object to him lay-
ing their foundation stones and opening their
mothers’ meetings.

Chief Justice Way, at Adelaide last week,
passed sentence of 12 years in gaol and 20 lashes
on two larrikins, aged 18 and 20, who fired at and
wounded a policeman. His Honor added that he
had reduced the sentence on account of the
prisoners’ youth. What is his Honor’s complete,
unreduced sentence like?

The Canterbury Domestic Helps’ Union talks of
presenting the “log” to the Arbitration Court if
the local ma’ams don’t take to it a little more
kindly ; and if it does it will also ask that a “ pre-
ference of employment to unionists ” clause be
added to the award. The “log ”of the Canter-
bury Dish-droppers’ League looks very reasonable
anyhow.
Infant mortality in Westralia is still
appallingly great. In 1901 the record
was down to 128’89 per thousand, reckon-
ing children under five years, which was
about 30 per 1000. above the Common-
wealth average; but last year there was
a deadly spring to 147’9 per 1000. These are
almost the highest figures in the world—
higher even than the average of those English cities
which have so unholy a reputation. Perth ought
to be one of the healthiest cities in Australia, but,
having no proper sewerage system and the surface
being extremely porous, the sub-soil has become
an abomination.
* * * *
Melbourne’s quaint Chinese strike among
the furniture-making heathen, which was sup-
posed to be practically ended, seems to be still
alive. The settlement has broken down badly.
In addition to their first demands—that the 10
per cent, reduction be restored, and that the
objectionable employers abandon their combine—
the yellow workers now insist, as a condition of
their return to work, that the employers shall
first offer two pigs as a sacrifice to Joss. As most
of the bosses are quite civilised, and “ belong allee
samee Church of England mission in Lilly Buck
stleet,” the desired abasement and recantation of
“faith” is anathema. Also, in addition to the
unions, two semi-religious societies have taken a
hand, viz., the Bo Leong and the Gee Hing,
Corresponding to the Free and Accepted Giraffes
and the Enslaved Bricklayers of more civilised
races, and these threaten trouble if Joss is not
immediately appeased. This intrusion of the
religious element into the business is likely to
make very serious trouble.

In reply to a Melbourne clergyman who
wrote him on the question of the danger to health

'i he matter is one which is receiving very careful con-
sideration fronrthe church authorities, but the highest
medical authorities think that there has been great mis-
apprehension and needless alarm upon the subject.
Who are the Archbishop’s “highest medical
authorities?” So many big doctors have spoken
out in the open on the other side that the Arch-
bishop must specify his witnesses.

the person who embezzles a herd of Cattle should
be all right if he can bring evidence that he is a
vegetarian and so couldn’t possibly have intended
to eat t,l e herd.

Said seniors started with the wise suggestion that
if Way didn’t say anything he probably Wouldn’t
be interrupted.
£ * *' *
A case at Dubbo(JSl.S.W.) recently, where
a girl swore that her father assaulted her, and that
she recognised him by the light of the moon which
streamed into her bedroom, whereas an almanao
showed that the moon was in its dark quarter at
the time, recalls a celebrated case in Ireland. It
was an agrarian charge, where all the Crown wit-
nesses swore that the moon was at the full, and
that all the accused were distinctly identifiable.
An almanac was produced in evidence, and it
testified that there was no moon. As in the
Dubbo case, the Irish accused were instantly
acquitted. Subsequently it was found that the
Irish almanac was a manufactured one, printed
for the occasion. The late Judge Dowling always
had an almanac as part of his desk requirements,
and when evidence was given as to dates, days,
moons, and such things he consulted the oracle.
* * * * '
N.S.W. Education Minister Perry is fruit-
ful of new rules. He now ukases that inspectors on
their visits shall ascertain “the local character
and reputation of teachers.” How will they do
it? They collect information about the local
character and reputation of teachers now in an
unofficial sort of way, mostly by gossiping in
hotels. Where the inspector puts up he generally
confabs with Mrs. Public-house. If the teacher
drinks there, his character and reputation are
probably high ; if he doesn’t drink, or if he drinks
elsewhere, verdict accordingly. Teachers did not
mind that much, as only rarely did the inspector
take any official notice of Mrs. Public-house, but
if he goes about now clothed with a mandate' to
ascertain and officially report, some new course
should be marked out for him. Will he ask the
police? Or will he, which looks a fairer way, call
a public meeting and take a show of hands? Or :
will he aSk the parson, the priest, the publican
and the bobby? A teacher who was well spoken '
of by all four should be a pretty comprehensive
sort of man.
