Melb. press item.
THE WHITE CABINET-MAKER (frozen out, and now
a diver in rubbish-barrels): “Great Scott!
more Chinese competition! ”
Characteristic bit of sanctimonious blather ;
by General Booth (due to stump the Common
wealth early next year, age and feebleness per-
mitting), contributed to the local War Howl
under the self-denial heading of “Smashing the
Target!”
A little fellow, a cripple, was saved in an Army meeting
just about the time of the self-denial week. He was •
present when the offerings of the corps were brought inl-
and wept, heartbroken, to think that he had nothing to
lay at his Saviour’s feet. Then and there, however* he
resolved that during the coming year he would lay aside
a halfpenny a day, and in spite of all sorts of trouble and
discouragement he stuck to it. A few weeks before self-
denial came round again typhoid fever carried him away.
When dying in the little top attic, the boy gave instruc-
tions to his captain to sell his crutches to make up what
was short of his promise, and, “Mind, captain,!’- he
added, “ don’t spend it on the box in which you put me,,
or the hole where you leave me till the resurrection-
morning. Let it all go to my self-denial card.” This is
all very beautiful, is it not? And yet, notwithstanding
all these toilings, and prayings, and pleadings) and -
sacrificings, I cannot help asking myself the question
whether we might not—nay, whether we ought not—do
a great deal better with this wonderful opportunity than
we do.
This is Booth’s creed all over—take the cripple’s
crutches and let the ungodly pay the burial
expenses.
STARSHINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a bushman, tall and brown,
And she was queen of the bar ;
He was a reckless devil-may-care,
And she was a tavern star.
Oh ! why did she gaze in his eyes that night,
In the glare of the heated room ?
\nd why were her lingering arms so white
»n the witching light of the moon ?
And why has he kissed her lips so red,
And fondled the dull, gold hair ? I
And why does her color come and go ?
And why is her face so fair ?
Oh, the why and the wherefore of love and hate
Are questions beyond our ken ;
But the fisher is Fate, and the hair is bait,
And the fish that are caught are men.
RIFLE SMOKE.
LOVE AND PAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Know ye not my name is Pain ?
1 I am Love’s twin brother.
No art o’ thine can break the chain
That binds us to each other.
I let my brother lead the way,
And then his keys I borrow ;
Fond heart, you oped to Love to-day,
You ope to Pain to-morrow 1

THE ABOVE TRADE HARK 13 A GUARANTEE OF THE HIGHEST ART IN CORSET HAKING
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 20, 1903.
Mt Diab Arini,—
Some of those who liberalised their education
by travel to Tumut during the Governor’s visit
complain that “local” color of the proposed
Capital site was submerged, so far as theTumutoes
were concerned, by the influx of Sydneysiders and
Melbournians. On the racecourse were dresses
good enough for the forthcoming Cup; at the
ball were gowns correspondingly choice. The
Melbourne women were specially smart, wearing
those “ vive Varmie ” coats with military buttons.
But if the invading army suppressed the inhabi-
tants, it didn’t obscure the landscape which will
remain shrined (or framed) in the hearts of the
Rawsons and their aides what time they remain
in the land. Reason : It reminded them of some
parts of dear old Fogland with its rolling, curving
distances, and its river bordered with alders,
poplars and willows (a Tennysonian stream in
which the wine flask might cool in moss), and its
cottage gardens, over the fences of which the lilac
waved its plumes, white and amethyst, at the
amber-eyed goats —and at the aides.
Not many Tumutoes were at the ball and the
banquet. The tickets for the first were a guinea
the double, and for the banquet 15s. The toast-list
was as long as the river, and somewhere after 11
his Excellency said good night and went back to
the train and berthed himself. The sight of the
long lighted train, silent and standing at ease in
the deep velvet darkness of the country night,
helped to raise Tumut’s self-respect, and its hopes
for the capital. There was the ducal car for the
Rawsons, their suite and servants; then the Pull-
man, holding amongst others, State Premier
See, his dame and the fair Miss Lily, some
of the Sam Sees, and Mayor and ’ess Hughes,
and the Ministerial car with Minister O’Sullivan,
Mrs. and Miss, another See girl, Engineer-in-
Chief Deane, and Dr. and Mrs. Nash. The Vice-
regal party had its meals aboard its car ; Minis-
terialists and Pullmanites went up into the town-
ship hotels for nourishment. A ’bus used to come
down and fetch them. Hotels were too congested
to offer more than a straw mattrass on the floor of
a washhouse to anyone arriving later than the day
on which the Governor’s train puffed in.
From the neighboring townships families came
in caravans and camped in good style. One of
these caravans, which had been three days doing
the journey, had its milch goats with it.. There
were babies amongst the immigrants.
Tumut has a hospital with a fine view. But
there are only two patients to enjoy the outlook,
and one of these will presently be well again.
Premier See coruscated, saying “ they must have
been run in for the occasion.” One is an old man
of 88 who was once a reporter on the S.M.
Herald. Mrs. Donaldson, wife of the member
for the candidate village, came in for
considerable vice regal attention. She is de-
scribed as having very black curly hair and
milk-white complexion—which pounds Spanish
enough. Mrs, Mason, wife of the local doctor
(he is a sportsman as well as a physicker), enter-
tained (I believe that is the proper term) the
vice-regal and Ministerial parties at after-
noon tea on the racecourse. Lady Rawson
is reported as not looking as well as she
might. Her snowing-up near Kiandra, and
the inability of some accommodation houses to
provide her with any sustenance but tender
gum-tops and such like native-bear and ’possum
rations, isn’t exactly the regimen a doctor would
S rescribe for one only just recovered from in-
uenza. Yarrangobilly provided so much bacon
and eggs for the famishing tourists that they are
now ashamed to look a hen in the face orjneet a
pig in the street.
There were ten ladies in the jury-box, as spec-
tators of course, at opening of Federal High Court
in Sydney last Thursday; still, they didn’t save
the function from being rather mouse-colored.
The three Judges seemed oppressed by the majesty
of their new offices, and it was not their day to be
eloquent. Mr. Wise only pumped up a two-inch
enthusiasm, and Mr. Julian Salomons got tangled
in his metaphors. He said he felt that “the
hinges of the door opening out to a better world
were now rolling back on its hinges.” And in
expressing his regret for the fewness of the Fede-
ral Judges, he remarked that “it was wrong, now
that we have an admiral as Governor, to spoil the
ship for a few pots of paint.” Chief Justice
Griffith is a distinguished personality; also, he
has a cogitative nose and a resemblance, in his
wig, to the Sinaian law-giver Moses. When the
Three took their seats, they went through the
farce of pretending to write something—there was
nothing to write about. In the evening of the
historic day the Chief Justice of Australia, Justice
Barton and State Attorney-General Wise dined
on board the new Federal liner Dorset, with Mr.
Owen Cox, the managing director of the line, and
the new ship’s “ old man,” Captain Prentice.
Mrs. Commanding-Officer Douglas Mackenzie
removes to Tasmania presently. Her husband
goes there to command the forces. The “forces ”
of the Flyspeck ! V ictoria Barracks is humming
like an apiary at swarming time over the depar-
ture of the dominating lady. She kept the
barracks in a wholesome state of scare and marched
at the head of the society column all the time.
The paragraphist of penny novelette leanings
has started on Lady Northcote: “ A certain Lord
Mount-Stephen and his wife,” runs the story,
“were so struck by the beauty of a little girl
walking in the snow, and dressed in the scantiest
of rags, that they took her home and adopted her,
and from that day forward all things prospered
with them. The little one proved a mascotte.” It
sounds like a flagrant ease of child-stealing. Any-
how, it’s not true. Mr. Stephen and his first wife
adopted, in the first year of their marriage, the
child who is now Lady Northcote. The Stephens
at that time had no arabesques or state or dollars
about them. They were plain canal folk. That
was over forty years ago, and the “ Lord ” and the
“ Mount ” are recent accomplishments. The
Mount was assumed as a sign that Stephen had
become elevated, no doubt—just as a Jones rising
to greatness might call himself Himalayajones.
There was a fashionable wedding—that of
Mr. Kenneth Duncan, to Miss Violet Rawson—in
Wellington (M.L.) the other week, and the struggle
amongst the uninvited public to get into
the church and secure good coigns of view is
described by a spectator as being worse than
a panic he once witnessed in a crowded lecture-
hall which was jolted in three directions at once
by a bull-earthquake. In both cases women
fought and scratched and butted and tore each
other’s clothes. In the one case it was to get a
view of a girl in the conventional bridal raiment.
In the other it was to avoid getting too close
a view of Archangel Michael or others of the
Heavenly Host.
Fresh Air League Ladies had a very swagger
annual meeting in Sydney Town Hall. Mrs.
Hugh Dixson provided an afternoon tea in Gov-
ernment House style. Mrs. Matthew Harris tore
herself away from her Japanese room and inhabited
the platform with Miss M. Harris, Mrs. See, Mrs.
T. Hughes, Mrs. Kellick, Mrs. Caro, Dr. Mary
Booth and others. F.A.L. charity sent during
the past season, from Nov. to April, nine men, 136
women, and 141 children to the country for rest
and change. Since the League was started in ’9l
4,416 persons have had the benefit of it.
A good idea is that of the N.S.W. Bookstall
Co. to issue booklets of colored views of nature’s
pet spots and of Australian birds, in place of the
time-worn Xmas card with its New Testament
greetings, or its Tennysonian, or Adelaide
Proctor or Ella Wheeler Wilcox couplet. Our
friends in Pokestogg-on-the-Hike (England) and
Auchtermuchty (Scotland), and in these notable
Irish centres, Ballymena, Cullybackey, Bally-
money and Coleraine, will look with interest
upon the eleven views in this booklet—Sydney
Harbor from Circular Quay, Waterfalls at
National Park, the castellated humpy where
the Governor-General lives, the Bulli Pass
(what a honeymoon haunt it looks), and
others, including a view “In the Far In-
terior of Australia.” This last picture adds the
ginger to the pictorial pie. It may be the cause of
some titled stockman being called ’Ome to take
his proper place in the House of Lords. Aback-
ground of gums, a middle distance furnished with
an old shed and a dog-leg fence, and a foreground
inhabited by white stockmen and blacks A fine-
looking white varlet stands well in the front, with
one hand resting on the shoulder of a gin whose
lack of clothing is partly compensated for by the
long grass in which she stands. Her nude rela-
tives “pose” with spears and boomerangs.
The other booklet—a colored series of birds by
Neville Cayley, shows, amongst others, our
beautiful blue wren, rosella, galah, rifle bird, and
the kookaburra with the Mark Twain expression
of humor in its eyes and beak.
From Messrs. Paling and Co. comes Nevin’s
setting of “Oh, That We Two Were Maying,” the
song to which Miss Crossley gave such an impetus.
Cover bears a fine portrait of the contralto. But
the title sounds incongruous. People don’t
May in this country, though they November
occasionally. “’Neath de Silber Moon,” by Claire
Newton, is a jolly little coon song and dance. The
Hawkesbury Waltz, by Harley St. J. Wilshire,
has a lot of good work in it. It ought to become
beloved of dancers.
In the ladies’ cricket match, in aid of Children’s
Hospital and Distressed Actors’ Fund, to be
played to-day at Sydney Cricket Ground, a team
representing Mr. J. C, Williamson’s Royal Opera
Co. and another selected by that enthusiastic
cricketeress, Mrs. H. Donnan, will compete.
There will be other attractions, as, for instance, a
contest between a Roman chariot drawn by four
horses and a motor car. A large doll, “Sweet
Nell,” will be raffled. A lucky name that. The
play and the horse were winners.
Miss Fitzmaurice Gill, who, on the authority
of competent judges, is an unusually fine sample
of the Australian native-born, opened in the
melodrama “Man to Man ” at the pretty Palace
theatre last Saturday. Miss Gill gives a good
picture of the melodramatic heroine militant and
triumphant, but her make-up was inartistic.
Roman knighting of Mr. Dan O’Connor is said
t d be the work of the Armidale prelate, Bishop
Torregiani. It causes a tenth of a smile to see one
R.C. press organ coldly quote a daily paper as its
authority for the knighting, whilst the other
R.C. paper gives columns and a portrait.
If a certain divorce case now in its initiatory
stage gets as far as the court Society will be con-
siderably shocked, startled or pleased, according
to the mood it is in, and the quality of the sermon
it heard from the last fashionable preacher.
I saw a wonderful specimen of the straight-
front tourist a month ago. She was doing George-
street very slowly on large tan feet, a wide tan
belt encircled her waist, her frock was hopsack,
quite short; a pith helmet with a fringed
scarf and green veil were hers; also tan
gauntlet gloves. From that belt depended
a swag of tricks, including a combination
knife (scissors, corkscrew, and button-hook
and blades) a scent bottle, a pencil and
tablets. But it was her figure (as she had made
it) that magnetised all who met or overtook her.
She was a thin., narrow woman, but she was
stuffed back and front to get the sensational
figure effect that you see in the. pianola poster
girl She would have made a capital illustration
for: •' ‘ And the vessel that he made of clay was
marred in the hands of the corsetihre. ” _ As the
tourist loped along she exclaimed, in high, thin
tones to a companion, ‘‘ O, look at the lofly
flawhsl” (flannel flowers in Jean’s window).

Did you ever hear of a Billposters’ Union ?
There is such a body in Sydney, and it threatens
to make trouble. Some person, who deserves the
largest monument we can borrow, has a measure
before Parliament to do something towards pre-
venting any further defilement of our Harbor
shores with advertisements. He wants to abolish
the huge placard showing Uncle Jim leaping
a fence while in a state of ancient and
unnatural vitality brought on by using some-
body’s Short Pills for Long People, and to
obliterate the garish thing about the three-and-
threepennjr waistcoat. And the Billposters’ Union
urges Parliament to reject the measure because
the Union lives by pasting up these disfigure-
ments. I don’t want to see anybody de-
g rived of his living, but the harbor should
e kept sacred. If anyone will start a sub-
scription to find work for the dispossessed bill-
posters at painting the houses of the members who
vote against the Bill—painting them blue with
diagonal yellow stripes—l will give my mite. Any
billposter can paint a house quite as well as the
house that that sort of man lives in deserves to be
painted.
Canon Sharp specially invited a large number
of medical men to the sermon which he preached
last Sunday at St. Andrew’s Cathedral, on the
text “ From the Most High cometh healing.” The
medical man is nearly always the unbelieving
man. He is so used to dissecting bodies and find-
ing no traoe of what lives after death that when
the human machine ceases working, he remarks
“The engines have stopped.” Canon Sharp
replies, “No, the engineer has left.” A pious
doctor is as rare as a religious sea-captain is
oommon.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The south wind was tempered to the summer
chiffons on Saturday, so the Caulfield Cup had
larger and more joyful crowds than for many a
season. One vice-regal pen was full to the steps,
the second had nothing substantial inside its circle
<>f palms. The Clarkes, with a suffering aide in
tow, were off to Gippsland—the bereavement
period not yet admitting of gee-gee frivolities.
The Terrace swarmed with the most gorgeous
figures. The approved summer charmer is a grace
with a general slope on. Her shoulders, her
chest, her waist girdle, her skirt shirrings are all
on a declining grade. When the effect is shown
in liberty silk, a smoothed-down Gainsborough
girl is the result. Shoulders dip down the arm,
the seams appearing above the locality of the vac-
cination brand. Only the hips rise to the occasion,
mostly by the aid of light, reliable pads.
“ Mrs. Clive Miller, with a billowy blue Liberty
frock and the clear shoulder slope, struck the eye.
A fragile bit of millinery gauze, a cobweb rim,
and an occasional pink rose, supplied a capital
top piece to the frail sweet frock. Miss Vera
Clarke might have “ arrived ” with her classical
silk garb, but for a hot flame-red hat. Mrs.
Langtree had a truly wonderful dress. It was a
white glace, mounted in costly black lace, which
had an elaborate trellis design in black mirror
velvet tabs. Mrs. D. Mackinnon, Mrs. J. Grice,
Mrs. Edward Miller, and Mrs. E. Raleigh, won
points for either handsome or telling raiment.
“ The most jubilant soul in the Caulfield throng
after Sweet Nell’s victory was Rupert Clarke.
He and trainer Scobie and the mare held a levee
in the bird-cage. ‘I knew it; oh, I knew it all
right! ’ said Rupert, chewing his cigarette and
stroking the quivering steed. ‘ I wired to the
West last night—l told all I knew to plank their
last penny on her. She gets a Roman Catholic
church a stained-glass window. Yes, I said they’d
get a slap-up stained one if Sweet Nell won. Isn’t
she great? Yes, like her namesake and god-
mother ! ’ The crowd gathered. Society dames
upbraided the dear baronet for not forcing the
certainty on them. A highly excited female went
to deposit a kiss on Sweet Nell’s nose, but the
mare wasn’t taking any. She reared, and slapped
the lady’s cheek with her tail.
“ Congratulations on their engagement crowded
on Miss Annie Mitchell—the golden-haired sister
of Melba—and Mr. Harry Box at the recent
races. Miss Mitchell wore a gorgeous white frock
with large, florid applications of black lace. It
was a Paris-stamped toilette with points to spare.
Mr. Box is one of the dear pets of Society. He
has revolved at every function of note for the last
ten years, and has never looked blase.
“There is ho shining happiness in Justice
Barton’s visage. Nothing sorrier could well be
imagined. ‘ Oh, I’ve done it now ! ’ is the mes-
sage in his woebegone expression. It is well-
known in the House that there is absolute regret
down in the deep corners of the Barton heart that
the political turmoil is over for him. And his
new judicial expression says as much.
“ The she-riflers are coming on. It’s the correct
thing to dine and toast these young women shots.
A Recent Saturday saw a Ballarat team come
along to knock bulls-eyes off the Commonwealth
contingent, which is mostly an Essendon institu-
tion. The programme for the day included a
municipal welcome by Mayor Gillott and a drive
to the Town Hall. There breakfast, with cham-
pagne, toasts and the wine circulating freely.
Then came a drive through the city and St. Kiida
in open drags. A heavyweight luncheon followed
at the Vienna. Then a drive to Essendon and
the match. Of course the Ballarat team was
defeated. After all that hospitality what else
could be expected ?
“They say there is to be another effort to
achieve that liquor license for the Alexandra Club,
and that the dames are confident of success. The
new woman will have really arrived when, meeting
a male friend on the Block, she can take him into
her club for a peg.
“When the American beauty-rose comes here,
as come it will, lofty floral adornments will
prevail, because the beauty-rose grows on stems,
tall as a man. A fortune awaits any gardener,
who has it all a-blowing, all a-growing here,
in readiness for the beginning of its boom.
Growers, who had in stock the 12 roses which
topped a recent press rose-poll, have been bought
right out of the winning varieties. Suspicious
people fancy that professional gardeners wrote
most of the letters in said competition, signing
different names, but voting every time for the
roses that it would best suit the writers’ pockets
to bring to the front.
“ The local Caledonian Society has been serving
up ‘ Rob Roy ’in aid of hard-up Scots. On open-
ing night, Mr. George Gibb, president of the
society, took the front of the d.c. in impressive
kilts, whose tartans could hardly be seen, owing
to big, braw, siller buckles and buttons and
siccan. For that reason beholders remain
ignorant as to the color, etc., of the Gibb tartan.
President Gibb, in appearance a big go-ahead
Scot, is a widower with an only child, a daughter.
Said wee lassie is already being instructed in the
art of playing the bagpipes. The McGibb had
with him various school girl members of the
Frank Madden clan, his Kew neighbors.
The M‘Eacharn, brand - new Lord Mayor,
came with his clan. Miss Vera Clarke (Cliveden)
looked very dainty and girlish in palest-blue,
with knot of darkest red roses nestling in corsage.
Her school-sister, in all white, seemed to look
upon a ‘ nicht wi’Scotch amateurs’ as excellent
sport. On the stage, Gregor Wood, as the young
hero, appeared on somewhat stiff terms with his
well-dressed arms and legs, but acquitted himself
fairly. Mrs. Hugh Paterson, all of whose stage-
business was connected with a small, pliable
riding-whip, made, on this account, a slightly
monotonous though meritorious Diana Vernon.
Secretary Hector M’Lonnan as Rob Roy, and
Messrs. Picken and Skinner as the Bailie and
Dougal respectively, were all refreshingly con-
vincing. The keening of the lament for Rob Roy
and the subsequent reels and strathspeys in the
rocky pass were good bits of work.
“Donald Macdonald, war correspondent, in-
tends, I hear, to run for a seat in the Senate.
“Suburbanism, pining for the necessary frou-
frou, has been provided with an execrable lining
called ‘rustle.’ This is supposed to give all the
frou-frou charm at a trifling cost. Really, it
crackles like stiff brown paper, and you wonder,
when your ear is struck with the sound,. whether
an animated draper’s, parcel is marching towards
you. The device of the flutterer who, not being
able to stand the expense of silk linings, makes
for noiseless movement and glides silently through
the season is worthy of imitation. If one can’t
frou-frou in the one and only fascinatingly subtle
way, then the thing is to get effect out of noise-
lessness. Be fairy-like and mouse along softly.
It’s a sweet, refreshing contrast.
“Rain drizzles have spoilt all the recent garden
parties. The second Tennyson party had to
abandon its garden intentions for mackintosh
reasons and go indoors. As nearly two thousand
processed along and deposited cloaks and gamps
in the outer hall, the sun came out. So the fountain
court became a temptation to the choicest trailers
and the smartest sunned themselves, thankful
that the weather vagaries had left the field
almost to themselves.
“ Probably never did the spring chickens and
the poultry who are old enough to know better
make such an exhibition of their banality as they
did at the Tennyson shivoo, when, in honor of
Julius Knight, they wore the red rose 3 of the
stage Bath romance. Those who had omitted to
corsage a rose annexed one from the table decora-
tions as soon as Julius came in sight. He himself
wore the romantic blossom with a lavender striped
shirt, a lavender tie, and tan kid gloves, and
some other fixings. He was brilliantined and
toned up beautifully. I have a lively fear that
one day one of these stage beauty men—Julius
Knight or Wilson Barrett of the short white
garment —will run for the Senate, and get in oh
the silly-female vote.
“The fashionable are in the throes of new ex-
citement —a croquet tournament. :It comes off in
a few.weeks under the supervision of the dimpled
Clarke aide. The dear young captain sails off
home soon to ‘join his regiment,’ which has a
ping-pong tournament, or a big ‘ ragging,’ or
other martial task of importance before it. They
say that croquet is growing in favor in the British
army—it is such a ladylike game.

tary or any kind of a working partner to her
Crossley. Pure, disinterested friendship inspires
all her boom efforts ! £?o that the advance-agent
look of the thing is all wrong, and Miss Murphy’s
preliminary visits to all the localities where the
star is to sing count for nothing. The pen-woman
admits that, whatever little help she may lie able
to give towards swelling the receptions to Ada,
she gives and gladly.

“In Fighting Form.”
KIDNEY TROUBLE knocked me clean out,” says Mr. Wally Dawson, the popular
professional boxer, 8 Blanche-street, Collingwood. “ First I thought it was a cold
in the loins, but I got so bad that I had to see a doctor, and he said my kidneys were all
wrong. I had to be on the everlasting go, for the pain wouldn’t let me stand still five
minutes. My nerves were broken-down and shaky. The least thing made me jump like a
nervous school-girl. I couldn’t sleep. I used to double up in all shapes to ease the pain.
You can just imagine what this life was for an athletic fellow like me —hobbling about
with a stick. Patent medicines did me no good. I thought I’d have to throw up the
sponge, when a friend made me take Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. He said they actually
made new blood, which flushed the kidneys clean and swept out the painful poison. The "Just hobbling about."
first box showed me they were doing what was claimed for them—and the second box conquered the pains and
steadied my nerves. My face regained its healthy look and I began to brighten up-
Five boxes made a new man of me. Now lam stronger than ever I was and feel in
true fighting form—thanks entirely to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
And Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure headache backache, rheumatism, sciatica, neuralgia, and anemia, just as surely
as they cured Wally Dawson s kidney trouble. But you must get the genuine-always in a package just like Mbs-
never sold loose or in bottles. Price, %a. 9d. a box ; six boxes 15s. 3d. Sent Post Free.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The remains of John Norton were buried
last week at Rookwood. But it was another
John..
George Reid, with an affectation of
philosophy, repeated his old gag: “There is no
reason why I should live my life over again.”
“ Oh, yes,” said a hearer; “you could then start
No-Yes.”
Latest news of Mr. Jack Want is that he
is improving in health and is taking the waters at
Bad-Langen-Sehwalbaek, which is probably as
near to bad language as the saintly Jack has ever
managed to get.
The clergyman who celebrated that Sydney
marriage service which has led to sensational
police-court proceedings was a parson of “ The
Reorganised Church of Jesus Christ of the Latter
Day Saints. ’ And yet he only charged “a quid
a hitch.”
The recent anonymous donor of £lOOO to
Melbourne Hospital said he did not wish his name
to be made public, as he had nothing to advertise.
He didn’t even want to call attention to his
seventeen-and-sixpenny pants now going at a
great reduction.
The Epping (N.S.W.), prisoner who
didn’t kill anybody—was recommended to mercy
on account of his youth, whereas the jury should
have rather made allowance for the fact that his
name was Butler. Butler seems a most unlucky
name to wear in the dock.>
Knight of the Gregory-powder Danyell
O’Connor intends to send to Rome for the uniform
of his new decoration. It is said to be a beautiful
combination of parrot feathers and beads, designed
by a saint whose personal taste in clothes was of
the stained glass window order.
“When I see a high-salaried officer in
another House carefully place the mace on the
table, and then carefully take it off again, and
retire to a seat to do nothing, I often wonder
whether we are really fit to conduct the business
of the Commonwealth.” —Senator Stewart, on the
Black Rod.
“Hop.,” at latest, was at Toledo, Ohio
(U.S.A.), where he was born. He reports, as
many others have done, that going back to look
a.t old scenes isn’t all it is cracked up to be.
Somebody has always been building on the old
scenes. You go back to gaze on the tree you used
to climb as a boy and find a ten-storey hotel.

The Revs. Curnow and Kelynack, Wes-
leyans, arrived in Sydney by the same vessel half-
a-eentury ago. They worked for years in various
parts of Eastern Australia. One turned to
journalism, but the other kept on at church work.
Now they lie in adjacent graves in a Sydney
suburban cemetery.
Senator John Downer will not stand for
re-election in December. There is a steady ten-
dency on the part of big men to drop Australian
politics ; the financial sacrifices necessary are too
great. Australia will have to soon decide to pay
more to, or to expect a good deal less from, its
members of Parliament.
John See says he will instruct his colleague,
the N.S.W. Secretary for Public Works, to take
steps to prevent steamers controlled by the De-
partment being made available in future for ex-
cursion purposes on the Sabbath. “The Sab-
bath! ” One almost sees a Sussex-street purveyor
o£ spring beans with a gilt-edged Bible under his
left arm. t
Died, last week, Mrs. John Blaxland, relict
of John Blaxland, who came to New South Wales
in 1807 with a large capital. This John Blaxland
was born at Fordwich (Kent, England) in 1769
He died in 1845, so that the lady recently de-
ceased had remained a widow nearly 60 years.
She was Mr Blaxland’s second wife, and a
veritable link with the long past. -
The death of F. A. Wright, “ the
old grey horse” of the New South Wales
■[Northern rivers, has inspired some of his con-
stituents to compose very bad verses in his
honor. The late member for Glen Innes was not
so .much a politician as a father to the people in
his district. He knew most by their Christian
names, and of late years liked to be called “the
old grey horse.”
Maoriland has just got on to the track of
one of its long-forgotten children. A former
Surveyor-General, Ormsby, had a son, born at
Auckland, who went to Ireland as a young man
and didn’t return. He is now president of the
Royal College of Surgeons of Ireland, the author
of many surgical works, inventor of many surgical
appliances, and was K.B’d. lately.
South Australia has lost its hoariest
cyclist—John Miller, town clerk of Unley, the
largest of Adelaide suburbs. Right to the end,
which came when he could throw a brick and
strike his 70th birthday, he used to bike over the
hilly roads of his district, pursuing the wandering
cow to her lair and seeing that the culvert near
the councillor’s residence was kept in good order.
Quinn’s Bill, before N.S.W. Assembly, to
make newspaper writers sign their names to
articles might do some good. A little personage,
the other day, said he wrote all the important
leading articles in a certain daily paper during the
past twelve months, and if the Bill becomes law,
and a new signature appears in that paper’s lead-
ing column, his friends expect to enjoy hearing
him explain.
Dr. Chisholm Ross, who, as Superinten-
dent of Callan Park Asylum, did much to help on
reform in the treatment of lunacy in N.S.W., has
given up his official position to enter upon private
practice as a mental specialist. The State service
thus loses an excellent man through “ retrench-
ment.” N.S.W. is not as bad in that respect as
Victoria, but still is reluctant to pay a fair price
for high ability.
Miss Vida Goldstein, speaking at Korum-
burra (Victoria), in reply to a question as to whether
she had not been selected by the Reform League to
organise the women’s vote, said “ Heaven forbid !
I am a thorough-going Democrat, and if ever
there was a rank Conservative body it is the
Citizens’ Reform League.” The chairman of
Vida’s meeting was also the Chairman of the local
branch of the league, and he smiled a sad, sad
smile.
Labor member Peter Airey (“P. Luftig ”
of The Bulletin) refused the position of Whip
to the new Queensland Government, preferring to
take up the Labor Party leadership, left vacant
when Browne joined the Morgan Ministry, The
billet of W hip is worth about £3OO a year, with
free Parliament House lodging and clerical assis-
tance thrown in. The Labor leadership brings in
no extra money, but a tremendous amount of hard
work and responsibility.
There are only two members, King
O’Malley and Mauger, who have attended every
sitting of the Australian Parliament. This session,
however, there are 11 in the Reps who have no
single absence against their names —Hume Cook,
Crouch, Fisher, Holder, Kirwan, Mauger,
M‘Donald, O’Malley, Tudor, Turner, and Wilkin-
son. As in the State Parliaments, the Labor men
show up conspicuously here also. Maedonald-
Paterson, M'Lean, and Spruce Myth are at the
other end of the record.
That worn veteran, “Foxy” Graves, is
turning out just such another failure in the
position of chairman of committees in Vic.
Assembly as Duncan Gillies proved when jockeyed
into the Speakership. The Iceberg Govt, insisted
on appointing Graves for the same reason that
actuated it in appointing Gillies —the other
candidate was a Labor member. Graves never
quite has his House in hand, and frequently tries
to deliver long harangues from the chair instead
of just mentioning that he wants order and intends
to have it.
The Rev. £ £ Bevan’s half-given consent
to run for the Federal Parliament in Victoria
sent off an opposition crowd to Rev. “Larry”
Rentoul. But Fighting Lawrence wasn’t taking
i any. “I pushed away from me chances to go
into politics in the old lands, and I shall continue
for the same reasons to put them away from me
here.” Parsons, of course, aren’t barred from the
Australian Parliament as they are from some
State Houses, and there is one member of the
Rev. Rentoul’s kirk —Ronald—in the Reps,
already.
ViscountessGlentworfch, who died the other
week, claimed to be a centenarian, but the old
lady had probably added a good few years to her
life. The daughter of a country squire, she was
still a spinster at the time of Queen Victoria’s
coronation, therefore her reminiscences of the
coronation and “the dignity of the Queen’s bear-
ing throughout the ceremony ” were probably
imaginary. Westminster Abbey is a small place
for a show, and there are no seats to spare for
unmarried daughters of squires on very special
occasions.
The crowd with which the late Duncan
Gillies was connected, and which he for years led,
talks a lot about the necessity for a man to have
“ a stake in the country ” before he can be trusted
to manage its affairs. Yet it turns out that
Dunky had been for years a director of an insur-
ance co., on the strength of a mere handful of
shares of which he was only the nominal holder,
they being mortgaged for far beyond their value
to another director. And he ran the stake-in-the-
country party while borrowing small sums from
another Tory to goon with.
Belated by Frank Lawry, in the M.L.
House of Reps., that in the old provincial days
when Auckland province was hard-up, George Grey
asked the manager of the B.N.Z. for £lO,OOO for
the Treasury until the financial weather broke.
The manager agreed on condition that “ Hori ”
would stand security. The great pro-consul glared
at the banker, thanked him for nothing, and drew
out his own cheque for £lO,OOO.
“ B.’' writes :
Daniel Henry Deniehy’s death has just been
commemorated by a few old associates.
It is nearly forty years since the
ill-starred solicitor died, un-
honored and unsung. “ The
greatest orator and literary
power Australia has pro-
duced,” said one- of
these associates the
other day. I do not
agree. At all events,
I ask evidence. I
was invited a few
years ago to compile a
biography of Deniehy, with
copious citations from his
speeches and literary essays,
but after going through the
materials supplied, had to
report that the work was
not worth doing. Deniehy’s
charm of delivery must cer-
tainly have been great.
Parkes, Dailey, and others
attest that. But his
literature is a painful
struggle to be smart
and learned.
The retiring
Westralian Sen-
ators are De
Largie, Harney,
and Saunders.
Harney will not
run again, and
Saunders was
only lately sent ’
up as a stop-gap
by the State
Parliament in
place of Ewing. The local Labor'
will run, for the three seats, De Largio,
George Henderson, and J. W. Croft.
In the recent ballot of unionists De
Largie had 8535 votes, Henderson 4671,
Croft 4491. The fourth man scored
only 2780; so that this preliminary
ballot was decisive. Henderson is well-
known in N.S.W., especially on the
southern and western coalfields, where
he was miners’ secretary—a position he
now holds over in the West. Croft is
secretary of the Coastal Perth) Trades
and Labor Council.
At Lawson, on the Blue Mountains
(N.S.W.), a dairyman named Brannagan won a
large prize in a “ Tatt’s.” sweep, and, pious man,
put a good deal of the windfall into helping to
build a R.C. church in the township. He has his
reward, for they call it locally—but not officially
—St. Brannagan’s Church, and popular rumor
(which is a lying jade) says that Mr. Brannagan
and family have provided for them a gilded pew
with two candles burning in front of it.
When the late Lord Salisbury was in Aus-
tralia, he was a guest of the late F. H. Dutton
and his brother, F. 8. Dutton, at Kapunda, the
site of the earliest copper-mine worked in 8. A.
Said mine was discovered by F. 8. Dutton in
1843, on his brother’s run, and yielded splendid
profits for years. Francis S. Dutton went into
politics, and was Premier of 8. A. in 1863 (for 11
days), and again in 1865 (for six months). In the
following year, he became first Agent-General for
the State. Frederick H. Dutton stuck to copper
and sheep, and died in 1890 worth several hundred
thousand pounds.
“ T.D.” : Speaker Beazley has now bossed
Vic. Assembly for a month without wearing the
traditional wig, and considering the severe nature
of this handicap, he hasn’t done at all badly.
Beazley is rather badly furnished in the matter of
hair, too. It may sound treasonable, but I do
believe Barton and the others could have got
along well enough without covering themselves
with horsehair. If some of the people who wear
wigs could only get horse sense along with the
other part of the horse it would be all right, but
they don’t. I never drive behind a horse without
wondering if I will one day be convicted by a
judge with the animal’s tail on his head —or is the
blessed wig made of jute after all ?
Still another female candidate for the
Senate is announced. The latest upheaval is
Mrs. Moore-Bentley, who used to hang her wash-
ing out somewhere up Parramatta way. Mrs.
Moore-Bentley is going to support George Reid
because “Protection did not bring about that just
and equitable distribution of wealth which legis-
lators had promised ; nor did it result in that
measure of good to organised industry and labor
that had been claimed for it.” Perhaps this is the
real Freetrader at last —the one who is going to
abolish all George Reid’s protective duties on
sugar, wine, beer, tobacco, newspapers, etc., and
his special railway rates in aid of the local pro-
ducer, and to advocate the real nude Cobden
gospel for the first time in Australia.

One of the things which the world of
science wants badly is a reliable anemometer, -an
instrument to measure the force of the wind: The
German Naval Observatory (that is about as near-
as the translator can go) recently offered a big
prize for one, and inventions poured, in from all
over the world. The judges weeded them out till
at last they are sitting in consideration of the last
half-dozen or so, and one of those still in the
running is the device of a Sydney man, Mr. Henry i
A. Hunt, of the Observatory. Mr. Hunt iai
almost the only man in Australia who has eontri-.
buted anything of value to the literature of-
meteorology, but Australia isn’t wildly scientific,
as yet, and a man who does things like that gets
more recognition in Germany than here.
Late Mr. Curnow--even allowing him all >
the extra credit that comes with death—can'
hardly be called a great journalist. Origi--
nally a parson of the Methodist Church, he *
was reputed to be a brilliant preacher. In time ho ,r »
drifted on to the editorial staff of Sydney Mornino
Herald, which had then a very strong taste for t
parsons, and afterwards became editor. But the >
man who makes a first-class parson rarely makes :
a first-class anything else. Mr. Curnow kept the -
Herald tame and colorless ; under his leadership
it always limped in tardy imitation after its rival, a
and never boasted even a flash of brilliancy. Still
it was, compared with that rival, honest and fair,
and that is a great thing to be said to an editor’s,
credit. Personally, Mr. Curnow was a likeable
man and held in very high esteem bv his intimates..

So changed is the climatic condition of
Broken Hill that a recent Barrier Miner prints
a half-column account of a serious boating acci-
dent. (Imagine boats on the Barrier !) It appears
that two ladies who were out on the raging dam
were upset and got really wet. And there has
been a boating fatality, too. Five men built a
galvanised iron punt, and went sailing on the
tempestuous waterhole, and the galvanised iron
fleet capsized.
* * * *
People living round the shallow lakes at
the mouth of the Murray have for nearly six
months been watching an interesting struggle
between the sea-water, driven in by the tides, and
the rising waters of the river. For . weeks the
volume of fresh water made no appreciable differ-
ence in the lakes; then little by little the salt
water was shut out, then the mixed water was
gradually driven out, and now, five months after
the river started to rise, the lake water is again
fresh enough to drink.
* * * *
“Rita S.” (who seems to have been there):
The paragraphists who have been giving women teachers
“lip” in your columns, are astray in their surmises. The
stiff lip of the pedagogue in skirts comes of the same
microbe family as the lip worn by successful
actors, actresses (down the gamut of histrionic art, from
heroine of a problem play to warbler of coon hums),
judges, lawyers, &c., &c., who have to use their tongues,
nerves, and faces. In repose, the average mummer s
face is a cast-iron facade ; in play, an embodiment of the
emotions —as required. When “ off the chain ” the
woman teacher’s face is at rest, the lip hard-set and pro-
jecting, the face like a wrinkled meat-hatchet, with
freckles. See the same woman (especially if she likes her
calling, and some do) in the classroom, and her face is a
picture of kindness, patience—and intelligence. Women
teachers have to play their parts on the schoolroom
boards, and what nerve-racking, patience-exhausting
parts they are, where young Australia is over-brimming
with healthy animal spirits, and has a locust in each
pocket! * * *
At a small Victorian assize town, recently,
where Justice A’Beekett had been engaged on
circuit duties, four prisoners sat at _ the police
station awaiting the arrival of the daily train to
convey them to their “retreat.” The sergeant in
obarge, who had the “ calendar ’’—setting out the
names, crimes and sentences—in his pocket, went
to stable his horse. While standing by its head
for a moment the curiosity of the animal was
aroused, and, with an almost human interest m
anything blue, it extracted the document with its
teeth, and reduced it to a pulp. Then everything
in the vicinity got blue to match the envelope.
The prisoners could not be landed at their desti-
nation without the authority, and the horse
couldn’t be committed for contempt. The diffi-
culty was overcome by the Judge discovering a
duplicate “ calendar ” in his bag.
Wool-robberies
were common occur-
rences out-back before
unions were established
and wool-shed watch-
men appointed. Sheds
on the banks of the
Darling and _ other
rivers were special vic-
tims. The wool thieves
used to row up at night
in a boat, at shearing-
time, sneak up to the
shed, and carry down
whatever loose fleeces
were in the bins. In
the eighties two wool-
robbers on the Paroo
had a complete. wool-
pressing plant in the
bush, and used to send
four or five truck-loads
of wool to Sydney in a
season. It is common
back- block talk that manywell-to-do pastoralists of
to day made a start in that manner. Wool was
plentiful and valuable in those days. “Scrub-
shearing,” i.e., mustering and shearing the other
fello ws’ flocks, was also a common crime in the
good old fat days which are so awfully dead now.
The wool some large Western stations lost thus,
years ago, would now pay off their mortgages.
A PATRIOT.
BRUMBIE : “ My word. Jacky, you bin go terry grey."
JACKY : " Budgery, mine believe it in White Australia pretty quick I
“Ed." : Near Heathcote (Victoria), on a
range of lulls of volcanic formation, is a large
quarry where the blacks obtained the stone for
their tomahawks. All about are to be found the
heads of stone axes partially
shaped out but spoiled by a false
blow. I have collected as many
as 30 in an hour’s search, but
none have the edge ground on
them. The stone is of a close,
flinty nature, very black and
hard. Judging by the accumu-
lation of chippings, this quarry
must have been used for many
generations. As suitable stone
is not widely distributed, the
owners of the quarry bartered
the stone away to other tribes.
The stone is in detached pieces.
These were gouged out with
yam sticks ; the piecesjwere held
on a large rock and chipped into
shape by striking with another
stone. The roughed-out heads
were then taken to the camp and
ground on a slab of sandstone
—the male black held the stone
upright while Mrs. Aboriginal
rubbed the axe-head up and
down until a suitable edge was
obtained. For a handle the
pliable sap-wood of a box-sapling
was taken, a strip split out, and,
while green, bent in the middle
so as to enclose the axe-head,
which was lashed into the han-
dle with kangaroo sinews, the
whole being plastered with the
gum from the grass tree. The
blacks used these stone toma-
hawks for cutting out bark for
canoes, for cutting out ’possums
from hollows, and for opening
beehives. They very seldom
used them in fighting.

“ Elmore ” : lie peculiar
deaths of animals (“WarE.”
3/9/’O3). Wire-netting is the
cause of a number of miserable
deaths of dogs and foxes. When
they are jumping through wire
and netting, if latter is not
securely fastened, they catch
toes of hind foot in one of the top meshes, and
hang head downwards till relieved by death.
* * * *
Near Colbinnabbin (N.S.W.) a female cat
is contentedly suckling a couple of young foxes
that were put among her kittens. The little foxes
bark occasionally, but their foster mother doesn’t
object to the strange row.

THE LATE WILLIAM CUENOW, for many years editor
of SYDNEY MORNING HERALD.
Photo, by J. Hubert Newman, Sydney.
On October 2 a remarkable nest, supposed
to be that of a magpie, was picked up on Hexham
Plains (Vic.). The centre was, as usual, of wool,
grass, &c., but the frame was a 2ft. 6in. network
of scraps of fencing-wire (something like a crino-
or a hen-coop), interwoven with wonderful
B kill. The nest was built on the ground—a corn-
mon practice this year, which, according to native
tradition, is a sure sign of furious gales.
O’HARA’S TIN PATCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tin, tin, tin, and the everlasting din,
Trees a-crashing, water splashing, while pick and anvil
ring:. . T i
Hear the falling of the kauri and the voice of Jack the
Maori,” , . ,
Vibrating voice of thunder, calling “ Now then, stand
from under! ”
Oh, the vines and lawyers cling.
Then the thud, the flight of shadow,
And the sun lights up a meadow,
As we stand all mutely watching the sunbeams gleaming
down,
Brighter than the jewels that bedeck a royal crown.
It’s the opening of a window, the lifting of a latch,
And the dawning of a city, clustering round O'Hara s
Patch.
Swing, swing, swing, the crickets cease to sing,
Palling tendril, tree and creeper as the axe cuts ever
deeper,
JJow the mauls and wedges ring !
Drear and dead, decayed of ages-when the world’s
medialval sages
Wagged their wigs, these trees were twigs; wide their
arms the shadows fling. _ •
And like kingdoms they go under, live their lives and fall
asunder
In the van of push and progress, breaking darkness down,
Haying deep the new foundations of city and of town.
It’s the opening of a window and the lifting of a latch,
P’r’aps another empire’s rising just around O’Hara’s
Patch.
Break, break, break, how the echoes wake !
The trees are falling down like the fall of king and crown,
Like the falling of the spires of some old monastic town.
Hear the superstitions dropping as their branches we are
lopping,
And the old dry-rotted dogmas and the lies so sere and
brown,
Where some tall and ancient wrong
Cast a shadow grim and long
There is light upon the meadow, for the axe has laid it
low,
And the dank aisles of the forest open to the sunrise glow.
Bo we stand all mutely, watching the old systems dodder
down
Where nations used to wilt beneath the petty princelet’s
frown.
It’s the opening of a window wide, the forcing of a latch.
We’re building a new era here, around O’Hara’s Patch.

O. NEWTON.
NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The dark Night swoops down on a drowsy world,
And tears the torch from out the hand of Day ;
Which glares with a great reddened eye of wrath,
Then fadeth downwards in the lurid bay.
The gum-trees shake and deep the gidgeas sigh,
And dews hang on their drooping boughs like tears,
The wind howls in a muffled monotone
Like to a goblin chased by myriad fears.
Then high above the gloom the white moon sits,
A jewel in the long black hair of Night,
And from the sky, sown with a crop of stars,
It pours the rich wine of its borrowed light.
The log of silence doth some strange fowl split
With the loud axe of his unearthly jest;
The dingo flieth with the poisoned bait,
And hollow winds moan on the mountain’s crest.
Till at long last the lengthy ears of Dawn
Appear above the far horizon’s rim ;
And down the murky stairway of the west
The Night departs with all his hearse plumes grim.
And toiling up the ladder of the hours
The laboring Sun doth bear the hod of Day;
And casts the constellations overboard
And girds his burning loins up for the fray.
S.A.

“ Caber ” : Be wood-adders. Any bush-
man knows that the common variety of wood-adder
is about as dangerous as a garden worm, but its
resemblance to the death-adder makeß the average
city Johnnie regard it with
suspicion. When the line
was being constructed across
the main range from Ipswich
to Toowoomba, in 1864, the
navvies brought out by Peto,
Brassey and Betts held most
of our reptiles in holy horror.
One afternoon, Dugald was
bitten by a wood-adder, and
all Holmes’ camp was thrown
into excitement. The bite
was cauterised, and Dugald
filled up with camp brandy.
He was trotted up and down
till nightfall to keed him awake;
but the liquor was doing its work
if the bite was not, and the victim
became mbre and more drowsy and
inert. As a last resort, he was
mounted on a horse and despatched
to Ipswich (60 miles) with a trusty
mate, armed with a whip to liven him
up and ward off the sleep that would
know no awakening. Dugald arrived
wide awake, and Dr. Dorsey explained
that the bite was perfectly harmless, but
that Dugald had been wakened so violently
that he would be laid up for a week. Then
Dugald ungratefully assaulted his faithful mate
Jock, and the riot was so violent that the doctor
wished he had given them both strychnine at
first sight.
“ Jerong ” : Travelling from Broken
Hill to the Darling, in company with
Jimmy the Rooster, we were awfully
meat-hungry. Struck a ground-parrot’s
place on the Darling. Jimmy accosted
the woman who made her appearance at
the door with: “Any chance of a bit of
meat, mum? ” “No ; haven’t a bit in the
house, only a bone my poor husband left
unfinished on the table.” But Jimmy could '
see something hanging in a cart-shed, with
a cloth tied round it. “You have a sheep
or a goat hung up in that shed,” he said,
persuasively. She burst into sobbing, “No,”
she said; “that’s my poor husband. We had
a row last night, and because I wouldn’t give
in to him over the women’s vote, he went to
the shed and hung himself.” Then some big
sobs, and, through her tears: “You can have
the bone.” Jimmy brought along the bone that
the suicide le/t unfinished, and we devoured it.

“Ciro”: In a recent B. par. “Scally-
wag ” speaks of the rarity of birds meeting with
accidents. Just saw a gate-woman at a wayside
railway crossing, down south, run out and pick up
a black swan that had struck against a telegraph
wire. In the early seventies, when trollying of a
morning near the Murray, used to pick up swan,
wild duck, and numerous spurwing plover beside
the railway line ; and when wire fenoes began to
replace the stone walls on the plains near Mount
Cotterell, scores of quail would be found along
the dividing fences. On fast locomotives, too,
have seen ground larks, in their excitement, fly
right in front of the engine and get killed.
MRS. WOOD (MISS ESSIE JENYNS).
Photo, by her husband.
“War E.” : Travellers this year are a
heavy tax on the shearers’ tucker bill; every
shearing-shed has a host to feed night and morm'
ing. At Goree (N.S.W.), as many as 300 received
supper and breakfast at one go, and at the roll-
call at North Yanko (N.S.W.), fully 600 men were
present in excess of requirements. All are willing
to work. What sort of “ patriotism ” would there
be now, if Australia called for volunteers at ss.
a day !

“Periwinkle”: The average Bill, Harry,
and Jim promise to be just as superstitious as
the blackfellow. It is the exception rather than
the rule in the country to find any male not wear-
ing an iron ring as a charm against rheumatism.
Every lad in his teens around here fancies he has
a touch of the disease, and makes a bee-line for
the blacksmith’s to be fitted with a section sawn
off a rusty old gun-barrel. Blacksmiths are
making a good thing out of the craze, as a barrel
cut into sections will run into a couple of. pounds.
Where the anvil-man is an artist, he drills a few
holes round the ring, fills them with copper and
zinc wire, and the delusion is complete at 7s. 6d.
to 12s. 6d., and the simple inhabitant goes forth
as happy as the Hottentot with his amulet.
“ Ne’er-do-weel ” : A fright
and sturdy Bananalander, about 14
years of age, a remarkably plucky
and intelligent lad, told me that he
and a companion of his own age
were fishing in one of the few
remaining deep waterholes in the
Moonie during the recent drought,
when they saw a huge shape like
“the bottom of a large over-
turned boat, without the keel, ”
suddenly appear in the water at
their feet. I asked him to describe
the appearance more particularly,
but he could tell me very little
more, except that it was black,
quite smooth, and shining “ like
marble.” Asked if they were
frightened, he replied, “ My word !
we cleared ! ” He said he did not
notice any head or tail—could not,
as the creature was not far enough
out of the water. He. ridiculed
the idea that it might have been a
large fish—“no cod in the Moonie
was ever a quarter the size.” His
companion corroborated his story
in every particular, and no amount
of. cross-examination could shake
either of them. Many people be-
lieve a large species of fresh-water
seal is to be found in our inland
waters, and the blacks point to
tracks for which it is hard to
account otherwise.
* * *
“ Elmore ” : How far do ants
travel from home for tucker ?
Mixed some oats through a bag of
“ cocky chaff,” and turned it out in
a corner of small paddock for my
hack. Returning, about three
hours after, struck a trail of ants,
each struggling laboriously witli
an oat. Stepped distance, and
made it 380 yards. They were
what we call the “ meat ” ant— purple body, red
head. , * * »

Pakeha, and Honga, who happened to _ co , r ?°
along, thought he would put in a word to help the
sale if he saw a chance. He s not very
said the prospective buyer as he walked round the
horse. “But he’s blanky old, though, chimed
Honga, who here thought he saw his chance.
The Phil May Fund.
Even after the 15 years Which have elapsed since
the departure from Australia of that gifted artist,
t he late Phil May, there are many here who
vividly remember not only his unrivalled powers
as a delineator of character in black and white, but
his genial and generous nature, and the charming
personality of his hospitable and ever-devoted
wife Genius, as a rule, has no time, to look after
its domestic affairs , and Phil May was no excep-
tion to this, for he died leaving his widow practi-
cally penniless. A movement to raise a fund to
buy an annuity for Mrs. May has been started in
London under the auspices of a committee includ-
ing editor Burnand, of Punch, playright Pinero,
and Shannon, the celebrated portrait-painter.
To assist it, The Bulletin, which will start the
local list with a donation of £2OO, asks for sub-
scriptions from Australia. Any money sent to
this office for the fund will be duly acknowledged
and remitted to the proper quarter in London.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will betaken of
communications iwnllaiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any ease re contributions voluntarily sub-
milted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and m
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.
Robt. T. : Thanks, bub you are under some
misapprehension. (1) Tint Bulletin fought the naval
tribute as hard as it could ; it warned Barton before he
went to London not to betray Australian interests in
that regard, and then sought-publishing several car-
toons, leaders and other articles—to get the Australian
Parliament to repudiate his action in the matter. The
cause was lost mainly because opponents in the House of
Reps, left the fight until too late, and partly because
R. E. O’Connor bamboozled the Senate into acquiescence.
(2) The Bulletin has given no countenance to any
Imperialistic ideas mixed up with Chamberlain’s tariff
policy, and has from the first objected to Australia being
pledged to any preferential trade proposals which are not
based on sound business lines. But The Bulletin is not
o-oing to be such a considerable ass as to scream against
Britain adopting Protection, if that country has the sense
to take the step, nor yet to advise Australia against reci-
procity with Britain provided the terms are advantageous.
(3) Tiie Bulletin isn’t “anti-English” ; it’s merely pro-

The Maoriland Farmers’ Union.
Jeshurun waxed fat and kicked.
—Scripture, or thereabouts.
The spectre of the Farmers’ Union grows steadily
larger in Maoriland politics. A good few years
ago Ballance and Seddon and John M'Kenzie
came to the rescue of what was then about the
most hopeless class in Maoriland; —the big land-
grabbers’ serfs, and made men of them. By a
policy of discouraging big estates with a gradu-
ated land-tax, by resuming bloated properties and
cutting them up for settlement, by the btate Land
Bank, and in other ways, the Government built
up with marvellous rapidity a class of small
farmers tilling their o.vn land and living well on
the proceeds. They were very humble at first,
and very grateful, but the feeling did not last
long. The work of breaking up the big estates
isn’t half or quarter finished yet, but already the
first batches of small settlers have paid off their
liabilities and grown pleasantly affluent, and begin
to have a fine large bloated contempt for the
wage-earning class from which they came. They
talk considerably of the blessings of Freetrade,
the evils of Socialistic legislation (though it was
Socialistic legislation which made most of them
what they are), of the need for cheap labor, of the
“Free Breakfast Table” fetish, and similar
follies. Putting the case in as few words as
possible they sum up the two alternatives in this
wise
Protection means good wages all round (the farmer
will also have to pay good wages), and the growth of large
manufacturing towns, which will afford a convenient and
reliable market for the farmer’s produce.
Freetrade means the destruction of local manufac-
tures and the grievous diminution of the local market.
But the destruction of local manufactures will make
labor a drug in the market. The farmer, who is r already
affluent, will be able to get cheap labor in tHb general
poverty and scarcity of work, and that, he thinks, will
make him more affluent. He will export more to England
to make up for the decay of the local market. He will
be able to treat his employee like a hog instead of like a
human being, because in the general scarcity of work the
employee will become humble. And the decay of manu-
factures will weaken the democratic trades-union ele-
ment, and enable the farmer, vvho begins to feel like a
blastiferous Torj r , now that the State’s Socialistic policy
has supplied him with land and made him affluent, to
run the country on Tory lines.
It is a curious combination of circumstances that
the class which an advanced Socialistic policy
lifted out of the pit should promise, as soon as
its numbers increase a little more, to be the
new Tory, Freetrade, sweating, cheap labor
organisation of Maoriland. Yet, after all,
the result is only what might be ex-
pected. In spite of this blatant ingratitude there
is no reason why Maoriland should slacken for
an instant its policy of breaking up the big
estates and settling the people on the land. But
there is also the best of reasons why it should put
double and treble vigor into its Protective policy,
and build up its cities and its city industries with
extra energy. Though Maoriland’s manufactures
under heavy Protection are increasing faster than
those of any other State in Australasia, yet even
now it imports nearly £12,000,000 worth of goods
per annum, and if it shut out half those goods
there is room in the making of the other half for
a vast increase in manufactures and manufactur-
ing population. The small farmer is invaluable
in one sense ; he supplies the bone and sinew of
the community, and its best fighting material,
and it is from this class that come the
big men with the 46-inch chests who
save the race from physical deterioration.
Yet withal the small farmer has a tendency
to inourable Toryism. The city worker is the
backbone of democracy, the man who leads the
way in all progressive movements the political
brain of the community, and, as a rule, for keep-
ing the country in the right political crack,
one toiler in a city bone-mill is worth ten
farmers. The Victorian Alps are probably the
most beautiful and healthful scenery in Australia,
and the best place to grow a magnificent soldiery
and a sturdy race that will keep the nation alive,
yet for political regeneration and for devising
means for the saving of Victoria from stagnation
and barbarism, there is more hope among the
grimy streets and smellful boiling-down works of
Footscray than in all the Alps. Therefore it
would be well for Seddon to dodge warily between
two extremes, and to regard the Farmer’s Union
with respect as a splendid beast of burden, but
with suspicion otherwise.
0. H. Read, from Tassy’s humble islet: —
What Tasmania is suffering from mostly at present is
the silly old story, proclaimed from various places by
several kinds of sil'k-hatter, that this country was never
before in such a prosperous condition as at this
moment. Governor Havelock and Bishop Mercer, as
new-comers, may be excused for telling the old sweet
tale; they have merely copied the blessed daily press;
but when Tassy’s Grand Old Adye Douglas rises up and
shouts the ancient legend to the whispering gums it is
about time to protest. Adye is old enough to know, and
probably does know, that Tasmania, as a State, was never
in a worse condition than she is in now. She is simply tot-
tering along the brink of total destruction loaded up
with the stupendous debt of 9J millions, and her people
are too tired, or too mean, even to erect a brush fence on
the extreme edge to warn her off danger. One little
push would do the business. And perhaps if she went
over it wouldn’t be much of a disaster. She couldn’t
land in a worse condition, and would have probably got
rid of the awful burden before she reached the bottom.
But prosperous ! Tasmania cannot live without bringing
mutton from Victoria. She cannot even keep herself
in butter. She cannot grow enough spuds for ex-
port without filling up the bags with dirt. Her
rates of wages are shockingly low with the exception
of those on the West Coast, and even they are threatened
with curtailment. Her working hours are disgracefully
long, as a rule up to 84 hours per week ; and I have
known surveyors’ men worked up to 100 hours (Sunday
included) at 255. per work. And this not so very long
ago. They have a peculiar kind of patriotism in Tas-
mania. The haste to send her best men Boer-scalping,
and piano-burning, and Bible-robbing in_ a strange
land was frantically patriotic, but Tasmanian patriots
refuse to pay any kind of a tax to get their own country
out of the muck. Hundreds of politicians and other
imaginary people have sworn on the platform to succour
the “poor working man” when they “get in”; and
every man of them has committed rank perjury. . The
present mess between the Council and Assembly is the
most prosperous-looking business that has struck this
State for years, and I pray that it will be the means of
making a clean sweep of the House of Dodery and
doddering. If not, let the old island go over the brink.
Will somebody be kind enough to give Tassy a Good Big
Push?

About a street corner :
The S.M. Herald has been letting a contributor supply
it with some “ interesting information ” which will pro-
bably come in handy to its enemies some day. The
interesting informant, taking the north-east corner of
George and King streets for an example, shows “ the
appreciation of land values in Sydney during the past 80
years.” In 1828 2 roods 4 perches (228 link 3 to George-
street and 250 to King street) were sold for £63. In 1834
the corner, 50ft. to George-street and 86ft. to King-street,
with l-16th of the original area, went for £4650 ; and in
1852 Wm. Toosood bought the corner, 50ft. by 30ft., with
the old Golden Fleece Hotel, for £7500. Recently, to
round off the corner, the City Council bought 5 l-sth
square yards for £l5OO, which is at the rate of
£720,000 for the block purchased in 1823 for £63. And
William Henry Moore, that first holder back in 1823,’
might have sailed for England next day, and never seen
the land again, nor spent a ha’penny on it; but it would
be worth over half a million nevertheless. Yet when it
is suggested as a fair thing for the community to get back,
by land-tax, a small portion of the value which it has
itself created, there is a cry of spoliation and robbery ;
and the noise of people saying “ Hear, hear,” as Waddell
congratulates the State upon having a deficit of £250,000
for 12 months, and as he promises that taxes shall not be
increased in the future, is deafening.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.

Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage

that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited
The trail of Bent and hard times is over
the Victorian railways and their works. _ The
roofs of the buildings, the office-walls, carriages,
and engines have a universal mantle of black dirt.
Amidst all this grime and microbes you can just
decipher the signal notices and warnings not to
expectorate indiscriminately on the preniises.
When washing-up day comes round Victoria will
only begin to realise that it is an expensive thing
to trust its railways to a man who has no other
qualifications than ability to cut down wages and
bellow rude things at officials.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Prohibition is running Maoriland into an
unlooked-for expense. Prohibited towns, which
used to take the corpse to the public-house, now
have to build morgues.
Already a party by the name of Robinson
suggests in Sydney Telegraph that the Australian
capital should be named Britannia. Would
“ Britannia Metal ” be as good ?
Bathurst didn’t get one vote in the ballot
for the site of the Federal capital; yet it was
about the only town that made any serious effort
to organise an agitation for the capital.
Said that Minister Bill Lyne boasts that
he can block any Federal capital site in the House
of Reps, but Tumut. The House will be pleased
to know that it is so completely in Bill’s pocket.
G. H. Reid has forgotten all about his
fiscal referendum proposal, and now tours the
country with the resurrected Dry Dog at his
heels; and the dog doesn’t smell any the better
for its temporary burial.
A definite statement in G. H. Reid’s
announcement of his policy
The bond of union amongst us is first the removal of
-present Government.
Exactly. Our joint and several office-hunger
keeps us together.
“We are not able,” said George Reid at
his first “Freetrade Campaign” meeting—“we
are not able to pay a secretary, as the other side
can, an enormous salary; we are unable to pay a
secretary at all.” Yet there are plenty of rich
importers left. Perhaps if the Freetrade leader
would try a little real Freetrade these importers
might then think his cause worth putting some
money into.
TAPERING OFF.
DR. CHAMBRRLAIN : "I don’t see how he is to get better while you let him keep swilling that stuff.”
NURSE BALFOUR: “ Yes, I know; but I must break the habit gradually, not all at once. He
used to drink it out of a bath not long since.”
Senator Downer’s retirement will give
Sudden Solomon—the man who created a Ministry
but rested on the seventh day and never resumed
—his longed-for chance to stand for the Senate.
Sudden S. suffers from the impression that the
Senate, being an Upper House, should be Tory and
a fit place for Solomon.
Walpole, the Employers’ Union agitator,
speaking at a Geelong (Vic.) political afternoon-
tea, said the Nicest should hold drawing-room
receptions all over the State, and make a point of
inviting, their friends, particularly the wives of
the thrifty. The spider’s parlor will be open to
the flies —until after next election.
The Victorian Government allowed it to get
abroad the other day that it was “considering”
the application of an old age pensioner that his
pension might be paid in another district. It
seems the . poor old derelict had formerly
“ batched ” in an abandoned boiler near a bush
township, but had moved away; now he wanted
to go back again, as the boiler was more home-
like. An ordinary Government would have
granted his request without any undue publicity,
but the thrifty Irvine-Shiels crowd probably
wanted to see if it couldn’t charge him a shilling
a week rent.
ic. members of Parliament do not take
Irvine’s compulsory resumption move very
seriously, With the various Bills before the House,
the Budget, and the general political muddle
into which Kyabram has thrown things, the
Upper House is not likely to have the pleasure of
firing that measure out for some time to come.
Queensland's new Morgan Ministry begins
well. The Cabinet is going to make the Central
sugar mills pay up arrears. Premier Morgan also
states that loans to local authorities must be
promptly repaid when due, as the policy of the
late Philp crowd in not troubling to collect repay-
ments is a menace to the whole Local Government
system.
Chemist Thomas hoists in Glen Innes
(N.S.W.) election the same faded tail, called
reduction of members, which Lee, Carruthers,
Garland, Wade, and others hoisted before him.
But until the Government can arrange to give the
ejected members a job at demolishing buildings it
seems as if there would never be any real energy
in the reduction movement.
Bit of solid sense from the annual report
of Adelaide Commissioner of Police:
During their (drunkards’) detention no attempt is made
to cure the disease from which these unfortunates are
suffering. From the economic point of view alone the
habitual drunkard should, during detention, receive
special medical treatment with a view to a permanent
cure of his malady, and legislation should be passed
whereby hopeless cases should be dealt with as lunatics
are at present.
There was a meeting of commercial men
the other day to consider how best the State’s
commercial agent in the North could be utilised to
serve N.S.W. interests, and the president of the
“Chamber of Commerce”—the importing soft-
goods crowd—solemnly suggested that the agent’s
duty should be to fossick out particulars as to
cheap Chinese goods which could be exported to
Australia to destroy local manufactures.
Sydney Telegraph assures the public that
Premier Irvine’s heartless conduct in cutting
down the Victorian old age pension fund to
£150,000 won’t deprive any deserving person of a
pension. Perhaps it won't. But when the
pension is cut down to ss. a week, the deserving
person might almost as well be deprived of it all.
Will Major Randal Carey try living on ss. for
just one week and see how far it will go ?

prompted by the Kyabram Reformers, pays
