oall out “ Boodle-doodle-do ” for 30s. weekly.
In spite of Shiels’ windy rhetoric and his
vapid declaration that “the motion seemed to
Eoint to the necessity of getting rid of the State
rovernments altogether,” Toutcher, M.L.A.,
carried through Victorian Assembly by 25 votes
to 15 bis motion that King Ned be asked to con-
sider whether there is any necessity of appointing
State Excellencies in future. The Kyabram
‘ ‘ reformers ” voted against it, as they do against
every reform.
The “retrenching” Iceberg Government
is spending £1,000,000 over the great Waranga
Basin irrigation business, and it hasn’t even
authorised the levying of a water-rate on the
lands to be benefited in order to recoup the out-
lay. It is simply trusting to the landowners who
clamored for the stupendous job to pay for the
water used, which their class has so badly failed
to do elsewhere. The Government hasn’t even
secured a guarantee that they will use the water.
Statute Law Revision Bill, now before
N.S.W. Legislative Council, repeals no less than
88 obsolete and unnecessary Acts of Parliament —
legislative lumber which has accumulated since
1828; it also provides for the correction of several
errors in various Acts. In the Roman Empire
they had a fine system of entrusting to a front
rank lawyer, every 10 years or so, the task of
codifying the law —both the statute law and the
“common law,” as we should call it. As a result
the law was kept fairly clear and up to date. In
Australia amendments and repeals and modifica-
tions are allowed to accumulate until, in some
departments, the law simply can’t be elucidated.
A sensible system would provide for periodical
codification and revision of the statute law at
least.
No wonder Kyabram tosses in
its sleep. Even Tory M.L.A. Swin-
burne lately jeered at the Rodney
farmers before the A.N.A., telling
how these cadgers had admitted to
the Murray River Commission that
their lands had been increased in
value by irrigation from £2 to £4 an
acre, yet their shire secretary ad-
mitted that the municipal valuation,
which has no relation at all' to the
real value but is mostly a fake to
diminish taxation, had actually gone
down, so that they could plead
poverty and “dip their hands in an
unseemly grab in the State purse.”
Whereupon Premier Irvine snuffled
that in future the people who bene-
fited most by irrigation should at
least be made to pay a fair share
of the cost. In future ! Is it yet too
late to put a land-tax on the big
landholders of Rodney ? Prime Minister
Deakin sat and listened to this bunkum and was
too faint-hearted to breathe such words as “ land-
tax.”.
N.S.W. Public Service Board's new regu-
lations have been published. The regulations,
which run into some hundreds, contain nothing
new or startling, but the sequence of the rules
has been rudely changed, and the numbers altered.
So the poor drear devils who have learnt the old
lot off, parrot.-wise, and could quote you 67 or 972
VII. at a moment’s notice, have to start to learn
them all up again.
Carruthers, M.L.A., in S.M. Herald :
It was well to confirm the faith that they had by lessons
from other'countries.. They: ought. to! learn how it was
that Canadaqvas ; adding to its population by immigration,
between 100,000 and 200,000 per'annum. At the same
time, Australia, with all its boasted resources, was losing
thousands yearly.
Carruthers and the Herald ought to have learned
long ago that Canada has a heavy and scientific
Protectionist tariff, while Australia has a mere
apology or compromise tariff, thanks to Car-
ruthere’ political friends.
The Bulletin is waiting to see a daily
paper point out how British capital is being
driven away from the country by such affairs as
Chillagoe, Mt. Garnet, Stannary Hills, Smith’s
Creek, and the like. If foreign capital is so
necessary to develop our “magnificent natural
resources,” it would seem to the ordinary observer
that the crop of disasters North Queensland has
turned out in the last five years, all engineered by
strenuous anti-laborists, would scare away more,
foreign capital than 6000 hatters—let alone six.
The poor, mean, abject attitude in Aus-
tralian politics, which is the special heritage of
the foreign-trade, foreign-borrowing, foreign-title-
seeking party, was shown conspicuously in Aunt
Emma Pulsford’s interjection in the Senate on
the capital sites question :
It would have been advisable for the Government to
ask King Edward to appoint a commission to select a
site.
Australia, in the opinion of Mr. Pulsford, can do
nothing for itself—not even decide the site of its
capital city.
Max Hirsch, the Freetrade single-taxer,
had a bad time at Mildura (Vic.) recently. His
old statement that the community was taxed to
the extent of 3d. in the £ to enable Mildura to
grow currants, turned up in judgment against
him. Compulsory arbitration, he declared, was
dangerous to the workers as it removed from them
the right to strike. The politician, in the interests
of wobble, saddles the worker with strange joys.
He will be all broken up next because the workers
have been deprived of the inestimable privilege of
being hanged without trial.
With the usual anxiety to attend to every-
thing except its own business N.S.W. Assembly
allowed itself to be addressed the other night by
Dacey, M.L.A., about the claim of a cabman
named Hanna against the Australian Government.
Between discussing Bill’s dirt-money and the
claim of some cabman against the Federal Trea-
sury, appointing a Parliamentary Committee to sit
and take evidence re a proposed gratuity to a
Soliceman, and proposing to remonstrate with the
ultan about the row in Macedonia, the weary
hours are filled in somehow.
The Vic. Government was recently struck
by two violent farmers. One abused it at great
length for not supplying reading matter, in the
shape of the Agricultural Journal, gratis—that
is to say, at the expense of the city workers. The
other represented the settlers on Moe swamp.
The Government sold the Moe swamp at £3 an
acre. Then it was induced to drain the swamp at
the public expense, and thereby made the land
worth £lB an acre—a clear profit of £l5 an acre to
the settlers. And now the Moe farmers want a
water-supply at public expense.
The recently issued report of the British
Commission appointed to inquire into the state of
the Imperial Army prior to the Boer war con-
tains ope striking passage'. Referring to the
ammunition question the report reads :
Out of 151,000,000 rounds of small arms ammunition in
stock at the beginning of hostilities, 66,000,000 rounds
proved to be not available. This was Mark IV. The
bullets were expanding, and were forbidden under the
Hague Convention, but they were rejected less on this
ground than because, if used in hot weather and with a
dirty rifle, they were apt to disable the men who fired
them.
When there was even a rumor—a rumor that was
never backed up by any evidence—that the Boers
had used expanding bullets in some cases, the
shocked attitude of Britain was something con-
siderable. And now we are officially informed
that nearly half Britain’s own stock of ammuni-
tion was of the prohibited variety, and that in not
using it against the Boers Britain wasn’t influ-
enced so much by its solemn pledge, or by
motives of humanity, as by fear that the stuff
wouldn’t prove serviceable. No official seems in
the least degree ashamed at the hypocrisy of the
wretched business either.
THE FIGHT FOR A REPUTATION.
The leader of the Opposition clearly proved to Mr.
Irvine that the reduction in old age pensions arose from
his (Mr. Peacock’s) cutting the pension down from 10s. to
8s. a week.- Daily paper.
PEACOOK AND IRVINE ( simultaneously): “ I
downed him first.”
Queensland Labor Treasurer Kidston has
taken the first opportunity to announce that his
Government is not going to run the departments
on Owe’Sullivan lines—
As far as he could affect the matter, there would be no
political influence and no personal influence exercised by
the Minister or anyone else, either in the printing-office
or in any other department under the Treasurer. ... .
A Government institution was often blamed for not being
able to compete with private firms, but it was often on
account of interference. :
If the Morgan-Browne Ministry can keep up to
these honest principles, it will be the kind of
Ministry Queensland has been seeking in vain for
many years.
The sturdy, self-reliant Victorian farmer,
as embodied in the Loddon United Waterworks
Trust, button-holed Premier Irvine lately, and
casually mentioned that it would like to be
relieved of £4OOO of its present £19,000 liabilities
to the State. In December, ’99, this blessed
Loddon Trust was relieved of £1717 of its £21,671
capital liability, and £8846 of its £9245 interest
liability, and it then solemnly promised to go
straight for the future. And since then it has only
struck one rate —which was not enforced—and,
from sheer refusal to attempt to pay its liabilities,
it has accumulated a further interest debt of
£3353. It was this that it wanted wiped out.
Irvine didn’t say that these cheeky deputationists
were attempting to practise plain repudiation,
mixed with fraudulent insolvency, or that they
were a gang of plunderers with less sense of
decency or shame than an habitual pickpocket—
he merely remarked that he could not understand
it, and that he would neither remit nor suspend
their liabilities ; also he threatened to presently
put in a receiver to see if lie couldn’t inculcate a
little common honesty, though he would hold over
this latter action for six months. He wasn’t by
any means as severe as he might have been, but
he at least sent one gang of cadging, swindling
farmers down the Treasury steps somewhat
hurriedly.
KANAKAS IN QUEENSLAND.
“Mr. John W. Anderson, of Warrnambool, Victoria,
gave an interesting account of a recent visit paid by him
to the sugar-growing districts in Queensland. He says
that from, say, 12O worth of sugar produced, the black
laborer receives £1 10s., and this small amount he does
not take back to his island, where it would be of no use,
but buys merchandise from the storekeepers.” —Adelaide
paper.
It is strongly to the interest of the Fede-
ral Government that the capital site question
should be settled this session. If it is. left in
doubt, the “ conspiracy against N.S.W.” cry of
the silly State-frights party in N.S.W. will be
much aided.

213 Dorcas-St., South Melbourne.
, May 10, 1899.
Mr. S. A. PALMER, Agent for Vitadatio.
Dear Sir, —1 have much pleasure in giving my testimony
of what Vitadatio has done for me. I was taken ill two
.years ago last November with jaundice. I was then
living at Kensington, and the doctor who was attending
me discovered that- I was suffering from Hydatids. He
brought me through that illness, but after twelve months
I was again taken bad, suffering terrible agony at times,
and a large lump formed in my stomach, which the doctor
said was hydatids again, and that I would have to undergo
an operation ; but while arrangements were being made
I heard of your wonderful medicine. After taking one
bottle, which made me very ill, I threw up three (3) gall
stones. I then continued the medicine and passed 14
more. While taking the medicine I also passed hydatids.
I took nineteen bottles of Vitadatio, and now I believe I
am thoroughly cured of both hydatids and gall stones,
and feel better and stronger than I have done for years,
wishing you every success with your wonderful medicine,
I am, yours very gratefully,
Mrs. H. WEBSTER.
P.S.—You are at perfect liberty to make what use you
like of this. My cure took plaice twelve months ago.
Anybody wishing to see the gall stones can see them at
45 and 47 Bourke-street, Melbourne.

Legal Notice.
WHEREAS by an order dated the 10th day
of August 1903 of the Chancery Division
of the High Court of Justice (Mr. Justice Keke-
vvich) in an action intituled re MARY CRIPPS
deceased, Prowse v. Whitehorn 1903.—C 2068
the following enquiry, was directed to be made
viz. An enquiry whether JOSEPH STEEL
named in the Will of the said Mary Cripps late of
Burcot in the parish of Dorchester in the County
of Oxfoi’d Spinster was living or dead and if dead
when he died and if he died subsequent to the
death of the said Mary Cripps (13 November 1878)
who is his legal personal representative. Notice
is hereby given that the said Joseph Steel or if he
died after the death of the said Mary Cripps his
legal personal representative are hereby required
on or before the 7th day of JANUARY 1904 to
come in either in person or by their Solicitors and
prove their claims at the Chambers of Mr. Justice
Kekewieh and Mr. Justice Joyce Royal Courts of
Justice Strand London England or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from
the benefit of the said order. THURSDAY the
14th day of JANUARY 1904 at 12 o’clock noon
is appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon
the said claims. Note. It is alleged that the
said Joseph Steel left England for Australia in
May 1865 and that he was in 1868 in the neigh-
bourhood of the Blue Mountains, Australia.
Dated this 28th day of August one thousand
nine hundred and three.
J, C, FOX,

The great art of imitation. A Melbourne
woman recently poured oil over herself and set
herself on fire after losing all her money pony
racing. A Brisbane woman straightway improved
on the horror. She lashed herself to a post with
her feet in a tub of firewood, poured kerosene over
herself and over the firewood, and then applied a
match. When a policeman arrived at the blaze
he found a perfect representation of an ancient
martyr who had perished at the stake. she was
quite dead. The Melbourne woman was less
fortunate ; she lingered many days.
W. N. Willis, M.L.A., as a land agent,
needs no black-tracker to guide him through the
devious paths of the Land Acts. He fights all his
cases, and the harder the task the better he likes
it, and wins all the time. 21 Bligh-street.*
When The Bulletin went to press the air
was filled with language by a small knot of Sydney
parochialists who allege
(1) That N.S.W. should have the sole right to select the
capital of all Australia, the other States paying most of
the bill.
(2) That though the Constitution says that the area of
Federal territory shall be anything over 100 square miles
yet to ask for anything over 100 square miles is robbery.
(3) That though the Constitution says that the capital"
shall be any distance exceeding 100 miles from Sydney,
to put it more than 100 miles from Sydney is brigandage.
(4) That though it was N.S.W.’s own George Reid who
got the 100 miles limit and the 100 square miles minimum
inserted in the Constitution, the business is somehow “ a
conspiracy of the necessitous States.”
(5) That N.S.W. should cut the painter and go away
and federate by itself.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
This year’s Caulfield Cup was practically the
fastest on record, although the time (2.35 i) had been
equalled in 1901, when Wakeful tried to run away with
the race and was beaten half-a-head in the last stride by
big Hymettus. The pace-making this time was shared
by Billali, then T.M.S., and then The Idler, and finally
Sweet Nell, who got a slight lead at the distance post and
won by half a length. The pretty little filly started with
the advantage of the inside berth, and was actually the
first, as well as the last, to show in front of the field.
From the beginning to the end of the struggle she was
always pretty near the rails, and never further back than
third in the order of running. She had more than the
ordinary amount of luck which is essential to victory in a
Caulfield Cup. To say this is not to deny her dash and
courage. Nevertheless, the admirers of Sweet Nell must
admit that in being one of the insiders, and keeping her
place in the forefront of the field, she got a long pull over
the animals that started on the outside and had to make
their way through the mob all the time. On a rough
calculation, Sweet Nell must have travelled a shorter dis-
tance by a dogen lengths in winning the Caulfield Cup
than some of the outside unfortunates travelled in
losing it.
* * * *
Nearly every year one is struck by the fact that
the slow beginners have positively no chance of winning
the mile-and-a-half Cup race at Caulfield. A trier that
gets off badly may contrive to get through a big field of
horses if most of them are non-triers, but when they are
all goltig hell-for-leather a fellow that starts in the rear is
a forlorn hope. Stamina is useless in such a race to the
anljnals that need warming-up to their work. Victory is
for the sprinter that stays longest. Sweet Nell was that
sprinter on Saturday.
* * * *
Apart from the Ballarat filly, Ibex, T.M.S., Air
Motor, Billali, The Idler, Rhapsody, Lieutenant Bill, St.
Modail and Flagship were the only possible winners of
the Cup as the race was run. The best of the others had
lost more ground in the first mile than they could recover
in the next half-mile. Two or three of them, notably St.
Ambrose, did gain a few lengths on the far-off leaders,
also Flagship and Rhapsody travelled, perhaps, a little
faster than Sweet Nell and The Idler in the final run
home, bub the race by that time had resolved
itself Into a match between the black gelding
and Rupert Clarke’s lady representative. Undoubtedly
Turner, on The Idler, had been too eager to get to the
front. He rushed his mount, and continued to rush it
until he was more than a length ahead of everything
rounding the turn for home. Then he appeared to have
the trick in hand, for he was crouching confidently over
The Idler’s neck, with his eyes turned to Sweet Nell.
Her effort had yet to be made. Richardson shook the
filly up for her run. Turner got visibly flustered on dis-
covering that he was losing his lead ; he sat up to ride
The Idler—to “lift” him. But Nell’s run was too long
and lively for the horse. Ilalf-an-hour later every factory
girl in Melbourne was happy. They had unanimously
put a shilling or two on the popular name.
» * » *
After the Caulfield Cup it became quite
commonly known that The Idler’s surprising improve-
ment on his Toorak Handicap “form” admitted of
satisfactory explanation. The gelding Carried a lot of
stable money on the previous Saturday, but was hustled
into an awkward position directly after the barrier rose,
and a cut from the jockey’s whip chanced to hurt him in
his tenderest feelings, sufficiently to account for his poor
performance.
♦ » » •
Flagship is a really remarkable instance of
honorable failure in Caulfield Cups. As a 4-year-old the
horse, carrying bottom weight, finished third to Hymettus
and Wakeful; last year he ran a good second (8.1) to
Lieutenant Bill; this time he was third again (8.7). Not
quite speedy enough to win a Caulfield Cup, the old
battier has thusfar failed to stay the last Jmile of a
Melbourne Cup.
* * » •
Looking at the Caulfield “form” of the Sydney
horses that ran in the Metrop. and other handicaps at the
A.J.C. meeting, one is bound to reckon them a lot of
second-raters. When they are counted out of it the forth-
coming Melbourne Cup is limited, “ on paper,” to a select
few of the locally-owned nags, plus Abundance, Postillion,
and the S.A. champion, Sojourner. The Derby—with
Belah under suspicion—is apparently a gift for Scobie’s
stable ; in fact, the Ballarat trainer is likely to supply
winner and runner-up. Both Derby and Cup will be
quiet betting races this year. The remarkable strength
of Scobie’s team frightens Ikey Mo., and the mystery
that hangs around the intentions of the stable
is a caution to all wise punters. There is a doubt
as to whether Hauturier, at present unpenalised,
will go for the Derby ; victory in the Derby would add
111 b. to his Cup weight. The possibility, or otherwise, of
getting Emir fit for a Cup struggle, even if he will stand
a fairly good preparation for the Derby, is another matter
of vexed opinion. Will F.J.A. compete against Sweet
Nell in the Derby ? His Toorak Handicap penalty has
already brought F.J.A.’s Cup weight up to the standard
appointed for Derby winners; but, then, he and Sweet
Nell are in the same ownership. United States is said to
be coming on nicely, and that well-bred 5-year-old, who
hasn’t won a race for three .years, may prove the best of
Mr. Rupert Clarke’s lot for the Cup, after all. Ikey Mo
is pretty much in the dark concerning the all-important
Ballarat horses, as he was in Clean Sweep’s year. Market
quotations for the present mean very little.
* * * *
St. Ambrose has a long-price chance for the
Cup. The imported son of St. Simon made a respectable
finish in the Caulfield Cup, after getting away last of
everything on the extreme outside of the field. He will
have 71b. less to carry next Tuesday week, and should be
a better-conditioned madcap than he was last Saturday.
Three days earlier St. Ambrose had concluded his train
journey to Flemington by falling backwards out of his
box and hurting himself somewhat. Apparently there
was no internal injury, but the backward somersault
must have upset the thoughtless beast.
* * * *
Re the Caulfield Cup. It is printed in one or
two places that some Noses will have to ask for time.
Why ? There has never been a better betting race in the
Cup’s history. At least a dozen appeared w'ell in, and
were heavily invested upon, while all 25 starters had
backers. Tatterdemalion was once favorite, then
Billali, Ibex, Sweet Nell, and Nothos, and, last of all,
Elderslie, with Air Motor close unon him. Up to his
Win of Coongy Handicap, punters’generally supported
Elderslie solely because of money invested by stable,
for in public Elderslie had done nothing hair-raising.
Last month at V.A.T.C. meet, backed down to 6 to 4, for
Warriston Welter he couldn’t get nearer than fifth. It
isn’t the first time The Idler, after being well bottled up,
has failed his crowd, and there are few, outside those who
dropped their coin, that weep because the “moral”
didn’t come off. After shaping poorly in Toorak Han-
dicap Saturday week, he was supported again to win
between TSCOO and £lO,OOO. Last year The Idler was an
eleventh hour sensation of the betting, but he couldn’t
see the trip out. Party behind T.M.S. was confident
and, ’tis said, owner “Mr. Marvel” promised all and
sundry a high old time if he won.
* *. * •
Of £9OOO prize-money given away in connection
with Caulfield Cup meet, Ballarat trainer Scobie’s stable
scoops £4950, which is whacked among five owners (for
whom he trains) as follows Rupert Clarke, £3500:
R. G. Casey, £lOOO ; J Catto, £300; W. Bailey, £135 ; and
E. E. Clarke, £l5.
* * *
Rider (“Dingo ” Richardson) of Caulfield Cup
winner Sweet Nell, has a remarkable record. Atop of
many other things he piloted Melbourne, Sydney, Austra-
lian, and Tattersall’s Cup winners Clean Sweep, San
Fran, Great Scot and Hoop Iron. Richardson is one of
Randwick trainer Tom Payten’s apprentices, not yet
19, and still able to scale under 7st. Hence it’s likely
that, bar accidents, he’ll add materially to his already
long list of performances. Interesting that Sweet Nell
is the first of her sex to win Caulfield Cup since Grace
Darling scored 18 years ago, and hers is the only instance
of Guineas winner securing the big mile and a half event.
* * * *
Those “ reversals of form ” in the Eclipse
Stakes provided a delectable competition among the
horse reporters of the daily and weekly press. It was as
though a prize had been offered to the scribe who could
most pleasantly and convincingly assure the public that
the result of the Wednesday race, in contrast with the
record of the Caulfield Stakes, run four days earlier, was
nothing to be surprised at. Wakeful was beaten out of a
place when Abundance won, and Abundance failed to get
a “shop’’when Wakeful won, and the “form” in each
instance was above suspicion, it appears. Any person
who dared to snort at the apparent inconsistency of
those conflicting results was beneath the contempt of
the scribes whose business it is to make things pleasant.
The art of satisfactory explanation lies in a careful
ignoring of details,
» * * *
Wakeful started a warm favorite for the Caul-
field Stakes (9 furlongs) and was easily beaten by
Abundance and Postillion, who carried 9st. each. The
two 4-year-okls ran in the rear whilst Wakeful and Ibex
were “ cutting one another’s throats in the van,” and then
they ran past the so-called cut-throats. Finally, Abun-
dance won in the unphenomenal time of 1.56}. In the
Eclipse Stakes (11 furlongs) Wakeful was weighted at 8.13
as before, whereas Postillion met her on lOlbs. better
terms, and Abundance carried 9,5, or 51bs. more than his
previous burden. But as Abundance is a big, strong horse
and a notorious stayer his backers had good reason for
expecting him to win again on “ form.” This time Wake-
ful started at 3 to 1 and finished several lengths ahead of
Postillion and Abundance, instead of two or three lengths
behind them. It was a fast race (2.24), and Wakeful
was always ahead of Abundance, anyhow. Yet she didn’t
“ run herself to a standstill,” neither did the big Cup
horse “ profit by his waiting tactics ” as he had profited
on the Saturday previous. The failure of Postillion
might be ascribed to lack of stamina, but the reversal of
form between the two crack performers can be ascribed
to nothing but the inglorious uncertainty of circus-racing
in Australia. When horse reporters lay themselves out
to find specious explanations of such unsatisfactory sur-
prises they only insult the intelligence (if they have any
intelligence) of backers who bet by the book. Assuming
that Wakeful and Abundance were honestly raced on
both occasions a fair critic of their in-and-out running can
only regard it with amazement.
* * * «
Nobody, looking at Nothos on his return to the
birdcage after shaping so badly in the Coongy Handicap,
would have given the big fellow a 100 to 1 chance of
winning the Caulfield Cup, yet he was quoted at 100 to 7
the same night. The stable excuse for Nothos is “ a
delicate constitution.” A great horse on his day, “ but
he easily goes off.” Judging from the distressed con-
dition of this brother to Duke of Grafton after he had
travelled the Coongy Handicap distance (11 furlongs),
present scribe would class him as a non-stayer, although
he was said to be going great guns at the'finish of the
Metropolitan Handicap race in Sydney. Anyway, his
Melbourne Cup prospects are not bright. A big, showy,
soft-looking gee, with a character for being difficult to
keep up to the mark in condition, is rarely found to be
great on the day he is asked to win a 2 miles handicap
from a big field of triers.
» * * *
It has been commonly stated and believed that
the initials, F. J. A., forming the name of Rupert Clarke’s
speedy little three-year-old, signified Frances J. Adler,
the songful niece of the Simonsens, in whose professional
career Rupert appeared to interest himself what time the
damsel was singing at Rickards’ (Melb.) before she went
to Europe. The fact that F.J.A. is a colt was never
raised as an objection to the “ Francie Adler ” theory.
F.J.A. is now said to signify F. J. Austin, one of the
squattah Austins, and the breeder of the S.A. Derby
winner.
* * * *
The old story. Once prominent jockey J.
Delaney who died of consumption and financial worry
in Sydney, the other day, inside of a dozen years ago rode
several big winners and had accumulated a goodly store
of shekels. Wealth—and the wings of the morning.
* * * *
Small M.L. town Yielding has four days’ races
every year and gives away £lOOO prize money each day,
no one race being worth less than £lOO. Fielding isn’t as
big as Penrith (N.S.W.) and is about a fourth the size of
Singleton.
* * » »
Thoroughbred 19-year-old stallion Niagara,
owned by Mr. W. C. Roberts, of Currawang (N.S.W.),
succumbed to rheumatism day or two since. Niagara
was by Anteros—Frailty, and hence half brother to
Trenton (sire of Wakeful, Revenue and eo.) and Havoc
(who begot A. J.C. Derby winner Belah). He was bred by
Messrs. T. and S. Morrin, in M.L., and did big things on
the turf, but had the bad luck to be foaled same year as
Abercorn and Australian Peer. Niagara ran second to
Abercorn in A. J.C. Derby of 1887, and dead-heated that
nag in Australian Peer’s V.R.C. Derby. At stud he
proved a big success, and last year was twelfth on winning
sire’s list with £3993.
* » * *
Moorefield races, Saturday, supplied something
extraordinary—a twice-run dead-heat of three—High-
flyer (first favorite, 2 to 1 against, and next 5 to 4
on), Loch Lochie, and Barindi. Event was principal race
—Moorefield Handicap (1} mile). Loch Lochie proved
unlucky each time through getting into bad positions.
After the second struggle owners agreed to divide.
Manner in which bets were paid left those who took 2 to
1 about Highflyer with their own money, while those who
laid 5t04 on him lost—£s to £4 would mean a shortage
to the punter of £2 on his own stake. Amount laid by
Mo. and sum handed him or booked by the backer were
added together, and the bag disgorged a third.

Bendigo saw some tall jumping last week.
Moral, Strathmore, Newhaven and Mahonga cleared 6.8;
6.11 saw Mahonga beaten. Newhaven and Mahonga
tried twice before succeeding. Another 3 inches, 7ft.
2in. and rapidly falling darkness was too much, and both
struck the rails. Previous Austral record was Rattler’s
6.10} at Singleton (N.S.W.) show last year.
* * * *
“ Le Chasse” writes: It is hard for any out-
sider to fathom the reason (if any) which induces pony
meeting stewards to disqualify jockeys and exonerate
owners. Even when they go a point nearer to Solomon’s
record and shut down on both, they still miss the most
guilty one of all, the party with the high instep to his
nose, who puts up the glittering temptation and does all
the underground engineering. No jockey stiffens his
mount for the mere fun of the thing, or for pure devil-
ment either. Gaol half-a-dozen of the wire-pullers, and
foul riding will soon be a thing of the past. But you
might as well read a prayer over the real swindler as
inflict a fine.
* * * •
Britain’s new Colonial Secretary, Alfred Lyttel-
ton, isn’t quite unknown to Australians. He was for
many years England’s crack amateur wicketkeeper, but
cricket enthusiasts of the eighties remember him best as
the .Samson, in one of the test matches played in England
by Murdoch’s team. The Australians had scored 500 odd
for the loss of five wickets, and the lion’s tail was muddy
with trailing on the earth. Every member of the home
team had been on to the ball with the exception of
•Lyttelton, the wicketkeeper. He was, at last, asked to
take a hand, and he cheerfully took the remaining five
wickets for 30 runs. Now he starts on a somewhat similar
undertaking, for Balfour’s hesitating tail is very much
down, and will take a lot of lifting.
« » » *
The charms of English cricket, with a three-
day limit, which makes the losing side play for time!
Yorkshire had got Worcester out for 23 (!)and made 76
for one wicket. Then rain stopped all play till 4.30 on
the third day. Hawke’s only chance was to declare his
innings closed, and try to get Worcester out again for
52 or less before 6.30. ‘ There remained an hour and a
half for play when Isaac joined Arnold, but the pair
made a splendid stand. Neither attempted to score. . .
Arnold’s display was a wonderful exhibition of patience ;
he batted nearly two hours for 13.- Isaac was batting
three-quarters of an hour for 3, and Pearson 40 minutes
for 4. A good deal depended upon Pearson, but,
fortunately for his side, he rose to the occasion admir
ably. Slowly the minutes passed. Occasionally the
batsmen scored, but runs were not the object. Arnold’s
display was beyond praise. . . The occupants of the
pavilion gave the unbeaten batsmen a cordial reception
as they came in ’’—but the rest of the spectators, being
good Yorkshiremen, howled for the stonewallers’ blood.
Score of the two hours’ play : six men out, and 27 runs
made.

George Giffen’s team (West Adelaide) fell in
badly in its first match of the season. Post office work
keeps the veteran out of the field every alternate Satur-
day. The West Adelaides won the toss on the day when
they had him with them, and put the other side in, so as
to get the use of Giffen’s bowling. But he took only one
wicket, and a slow afternoon’s play resulted in 5 for 173.
The following Saturday a storm in the night had spoiled
the pitch. The Port Adelaides promptly closed their
innings, and (with Giffen away and one man run out)
went through West Adelaide for 16! Drew, 4 for 9 ;
Waters, 4 for 7
• * « *
“ Scrip ”: Have you ever noticed that many
sporting writers, although capable of turning out really
good stuff, are absolute duffers at picking winners?
Their luck in this respect is often execrable, and as for
selecting first, second and third, they get no nearer to it
than a man who wants to evade an appointment with a
tiger. Forecasting the three is rather a cheeky contract.
Remember many years ago when a Dunedin (M.L.)
sweepist offered £lOO to the successful tipster of first,
second and third in Melbourne Cup. A trio on a local
newspaper put in a big spell of hard labor and won the
prize. There were a couple of million tips sent in
altogether, the winners contributing 125,000; and they
didn't strike the winning sequence until they had turned
their fiftieth thousand.
* * • *
Sydney lightweight Truscott closed on another
fight with £lOO side bet and purse at Sydney National
S.O. last week, beating Bob Burnett on points in 20
rounds. Several rounds were Burnett’s, but majority
belonged to Truscott, who shaped with his usual vigor,
pluck, and endurance. Truscott is of the material of which
world’s champions are made ; he only lacks knowledge of
how to use his hands and feet properly. No gamer man,
or stronger, at the weight, ev4r shaped, and few have his
staying powers. Yet a youngster under 20, prospects are
very bright for Truscott, provided he can acquire skill.
* * * *
Fight at Sydney Gaiety A.C., Monday night,
between holder Bob Turner and Monty Andrews, for
Australian lightweight supremacy, saw first-named go
very near losing his honors. Turner’s fix was not too
good, while Andrews made the best showing of his career;
and finding big spots of weakness in the champion, landed
left and right hooks and swings on side of face and ribs
almost at will, but all failed through lack of power.
Turner began carelessly, underrating Andrews, but
he soon became serious. Andrews was well in
front during the first 10 rounds—once going very near
getting the money through a blow that dazed the
top-notcher, who was saved by the gong clang. After
the tenth round Turner made most play and forced the
game always, though there was more scuffle than effect
in his operations, but the tussle excited because it was
willing and fast always. The verdict (“ a draw ”) appar-
ently pleased the majority, and little exception could be
taken to it. Bar a slight trickle from Andrews’ nose,
his swollen left ear, and a “ mouse ” under one of
Turner’s optics, it was a bloodless battle.
* * * »
First showing in Sydney of recently arrived
Yankee middleweight Frank Nicholls, demonstrated that
he is a hard, gritty fighter, capable of taking and giving
heavy punishment and living through anything within
reason. Though heavyweight Tucker punched all over
the visitor in the first round and made it appear 50 to 1
he would win, Nicholls survived and waited grimly for his
turn. When Tucker did weaken, trouble came to him so
fast and so tellingly that police interference in the 9th
round saved a sadly broken-up pug from being completely
knocked out. New arrival isn’t overburdened with
science, but has a cast-iron constitution and great hitting
power. °
• * * *
At last doughty little fighter Jack McGowan
has, like all bruisers who bruise long enough, gone under
to a punch on the point from a third-rater. Tom Dunne
did it in the fourth round at Kalgoorlie (W.A.) last week
and although McGowan stood up for some time he was so
hopelessly done that the referee called a halt and gave
Dunne the dollars. McGowan has been bruising
regularly, sometimes three times a week, for 16 years
continuously, and is the dad of two families. For Jack
McGowan the end of boxing has come.
* * » *
Micko Walsh and Silver fought a lurid fight for
the Vic. “fedder" supremacy in Melbourne, Saturday
night. Walsh was 2to 1 on, and the fight galloped from
the first. Walsh’s left remained permanently in Silver’s
face the whole first round, and sent him to his corner
v ® r y In the second Walsh tried to finish it right
off, but Sliver had pulled himself together somewhat and
responded with equal fury, and the crowd went mad
while the angels wept. Suddenly Walsh, fighting
wildly, seized Silver by the neck and hurled him
against one of the posts, and referee Joe Stokesburv
promptly and properly gave Silver the decision. Then
one of Walsh’s seconds swooped down and smote
the referee in sixteen places at once. The referee, a
husky fighter himself, responded vigorously, till the
pohce and friends tore them apart, each vowing to pound
the other to a jelly. Time Walsh dropped out of Austra-
lian boxing ; he fouled Turner in Sydney ; he threw up
an engagement with the Gaiety at the last moment, after
putting the proprietor to considerable expense ; then at
Kalgoorlie his conduct was so suspicious that the referee
cried all bets off and awarded the gate to charities. Un-
fortunately the last proceeding was illegal, and the
fighters had to be given the takings.
At Timaru (M.L.) ten of the local Harbor
Board employees essayed a tug-of-war with the same
number of Maoris. The contest was for £5O a side and
the time limit was half-an-hour. The Maoris, who in-
cluded a fragile native weighing 22 stone, were victorious
by four inches, hut the losers lodged a protest on the
ground that the winners had their end of the rope fast to
an immovable object—the 22-stone brown gentleman
aforesaid.
* » * *
Women by nature are not accurate marksmen.
When the careful housewife, with determination on every
feature, seizes a stone with the avowed intention of
dislodging the domestic fowl off the lettuces, it is time
to get your dodging apparatus running smoothly. Yet
woman seems able to shoot just as well as man, or
better. Recently a team of ladies—the wives of officers
of the 7th Regiment—came down from Ballarat to shoot
a match against the crack Commonwealth Ladies Club at
Melbourne. Out of 35 points at 50 yards, with a 4in.
bull’s-eye, six out of the 20 competitors scored the
“possible,” four got 34, five registered 33, and every one
of the others over 30—shooting which would scarcely be
equalled by the Bisley team. The affair ended in favor
of Melbourne by 17 points.

The Adelaide handicapper makes but tifb
local men (Wilksch and Champness) as now better than

the once almost invincible Plugger.
* * » *
S.A. League of Wheelmen’s Spring meet at
Adelaide (Saturday) drew 6000. Eighteen events pro-
duced poor racing generally. The recently-arrived
'Murkan, Downing, though only in Australia a week and
just off the long train trip (Sydney to Adelaide), finished
second from scratch to Wilksch, with G. Morgan third,
in half-mile handicap. Merit of the performance is made
evident when ’tis remembered that Morgan easily won
half-mile Australian championship and mile scratch race
in Sydney a couple of weeks ago. Downing’s reputation
ranks him inferior to his fellow-visitors, Lawson and
M'Farland—and yet he, with everything decidedly
against him, accounted for four of Australia’s best in
Walker, Morgan, Chalmers and Martin. What are we
coming to ? What riders the other two Yankees must
be! Small wonder efforts are being made to induce
negro Major Taylor’s presence again this season.
* * * *
The Americanised idea of running a scratch
bike race was launched at Sydney Sports Ground initial
meet. It was a five mile spin, and points were allotted to
the leading trio past the post each time round. If the
race was run over 10 miles, every spectator would require
a secretary to note riders and points. The idea is too
complicated, and isn’t in the same street as the old-style
race with lap prizes, and first past the post at the finish
declared the winner. The League “amateur” race is
another event which should be wiped off programmes in
future, together with the “midget championship” for
boys under nine years of age. It doesn’t make cyclists of
them—it only strains their hearts.

The resumption claims against. N.S.W.
Government are an interesting commentary on
that public delusion that the State ought always
to be asked something over the value of the article.
The Daltons’ claim for the resumed Miller’s Point
wharf, for instance, was £105,000; the Govern-
ment offered £75,000, and after a prolonged
hearing the court decided for the Government.
The resumption scheme of over 20 years ago ran
on very similar lines. John Lucas of that day,
the ponderous M.L.O., claimed £76,900 and was
awarded £4500, and there were many similar
cases. John McClockin (he deserves a monu-
ment) claimed £400 and received every penny he
claimed.

Suggested candidature for the Senate of
Dr. Bevan (Vic.) recalls the fact that, by the
Constitution Act passed by N.S.W. Legislature
in 1853, and confirmed by the Imperial Parliament
in 1855, ministers of religion were declared
ineligible for seats in the local Parliament. The
debarring clause was probably aimed at Dr. John
Dunmore Lang, who was a member of Parlia-
ment, but was absent in England when the Act
was passed. His outspokenness rendered him
obnoxious to the ascendant Tory party. The
clause was repealed in 1857, and Dr. Lang was.
re-elected in 1859. Victoria and Westralia still,
bar clergymen out of Parliament.
Writes a man from Moree (N.S.W.):
About the quaintest circus that ever reached Moree'
was a band of O’Sullivan’s unemployed. The majority
were replicas of the American “ Weary Willie,” attired in
frayed toppers, greasy walking coats and ancient dress;
waistcoats (P.S. —They also wore trousers). Top-hats are
unknown in Moree, and the small fry practised on them
with their catapults. It took half a day to land t lie unem-
ployed at the relief works, as the hand inutinii d, and.
spentsome of its two bob in beer. Its chief feat otherwise-
was ringbarking 50 acres of dead timber !

Remark by John Kidd, an obscure mem-
ber of N.S.W. Ministry : —
I hope there can yet be money found for progressive
public works, and then let the fellows who come after pa
go slow if they like.
That w r as part of a speech in which John Kidd
called wildly for more loan money, because John
Kidd belongs to the crowd that is too lazy and
cowardly to pay for its own public works, and
believes in letting the baby shoulder the bill.
And some day, when the baby refuses to pay
John Kidd’s lazy, cowardly, dishonest bill, the
remnant of a toothless old John, sitting by the tire,
will mumble that the baby isn’t honest.
The ruin of the Vic. railway system and
the disorganisation of the service continue apae£.
At Ballarat six senior drivers tendered their’
resignations in one week, being anxious to take:
their compensation and make a fresh start else-
where. The Government refused to accept the.
resignations, which action squares with fihiels’
attitude in denying the right to compensation df
employees incapacitated through illness. Prac-
tically the Kyabram Government says to its em-
ployees : “You can work such hours as we order
at the wage we offer, or you can leave ; but, as we
decline to give you the compensation which thie..
law says you are entitled to, if you decide to
leave you will have to go without it.” Which’
is just plain robbery and repudiation.

Blackwood’s Magazine, on Britain’s fiscal
foolishness:—
It will be observed that the treaties of commerce which
Canada very properly rebelled against were negotiated
40 years ago, when fiscal altruism was in its most acute
and quixotic stage. That with Germany dates from 1865,
and the one with Belgium from 1869. Self-government
had been granted to our principal colonies a few years
before, and one of the pedantic conditions to it was that
no colony should impose differential duties on any of its
imports. While this Oobden veto continued in force, local
produce could not cross the border from one colony into
another without paying the same duty as foreign produce.
In fact, it often had to pay considerably more, as, for
instance, when South Australian wines, worth, perhaps,
three shillings per gallon, had to be charged the same
duty per gallon in Victoria arid New South Wales as
French Burgundy, fetching five and ten times the price.
Not till this betine had been in operation for a-quarter-of-
a-ceritury did the colonies get rid of it.
“ S.W.,” from south-western Victoria :—
Re your par. about squatter Thomas Shaw’s alarm anent
Irvine’s closer settlement proposals : What compensation
does the Government propose to give to the poor land-
holders of 10,000 acres or so who have for years past been
using the public roads? There are something like 40
miles of fenced-in roads in the Victorian western district,
and the would-be owners have had the use of them gratis
so long that, when the local councils are appealed to by
ratepayers to have them opened, the greedy squatter
calls it confiscation, protests, employs a solicitor, and
holds on like grim death to his wrongfully-acquired land.
When finally a man is despatched by one of these slow-
moving governing bodies to remove the gates and fences
and open up the road the trouble is not at an end. The
irate ratepayer, who has up to this stage probably written
16 letters, is obliged to spend more money in stamps to
point out to the council that the obstructions have been
replaced, and the road stolen again.

Great Fingall Consols, Day Dawn (W.A.), is
being vigorously opened up, preparatory to starting the
addition to the mill. The main shaft is being sunk at
the rate of 50ft. monthly, and is now down about 250 ft.
below the No. 9 level. The latter level, which is at a
vertical depth of 750 ft., has been driven about 700 ft. The
reef averages about Bft., and is worth over an oz. per ton.
No. 5 level has been carried 900 ft. south, having followed
the new chute of ore, which is of much lower grade than
the main chute. Though this new chute is 6ft. to 7ft.
wide, the ore averages barely XOdwt. It adds enor-
mously to the reserves, but reduces the general average.
The erection of the extra 40 head of stamps is being
pushed ahead, and, before long, the mine should rank,
for a time at least, as the premier producer of the State.
The 6s. div., payable on 15th inst., should be followed by
large quarterly divs.
The South Fingall (W.A.) will pub down
another bore-hole in the attempt to locate the elusive
lode ; but as three or four co.’s situated at all points of
the compass allege that they have the lode in their pro-
perties, it must be of the octopus variety if their state-
ments are reliable. It is more likely that the parent co.
had collared nearly everything of value.
* * * •
Murchison Associated, Day Dawn (W.A.), a
mile north of the Great Fingall, is bidding for public
favor. The co. is a sixth reconstruction, and the ground
has been worked for eight years by Bewick, Moreing and
00. without result. The original underlay shaft is to be
pushed down from 300 to 500 ft., while anew vertical shaft
has been started to intersect the reef at 1000 ft. An air
compressor and rock drills are being installed, and it
certainly looks as if the Moreing people believe they are
on a good thing. As it took the same managers five
years to discover the merits of the Great Fingall, it is not
altogether surprising that they have taken a few years
longer to prove this property. The Rubicon lease, on
which work is being concentrated, was originally part of
the Fingall property. Of 400,000 shares of ss. 300,000 are
issued paid up to 3s. 6d., and 100,000 are in reserve. The
assessments should prove ample to thoroughly test the
ground.
* * • •
Lake View Consols, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), has
been gradually increasing its reserves, and these are now
estimated at ifeS,ooo tons, containing 89,0000 z., or about
lojdwt. per ton. This original 50-head mill has been
condemned as obsolete and is to be scrapped forthwith.
* * » *
Atlas, Kalpini (W.A.) has already run short of
water, the dam having given out, and one shift has been
dispensed with.
■ « * * *
South Fingall, Day Dawn (W.A.), has put down
the second bore hole in the Government reserve to over
1000 ft., without encountering anything of tangible value.
, * * * *
Including Government mills, the equivalent of
ov r 500 head of stamps, capable of treating nearly
50,000 tons monthly, will shortly be added to the treat-
ment plants of W.A. The chief additions are: Golden
Horseshoe, 50 head ; Orova-Brown Hill, 20 (started);
North Kalgurli, 10 (started); Eclipse, 10 (started); Asso-
ciated, 20; Great Boulder, 20; L.V. Consols, 50 (to
replace original 50); Devon Consols, 15; Associated
Northern, equal to 20; South Kalgurli, equal to 20;
Bellevue, 40; Island Eureka South, 5; Queensland Menzies,
10; Great Fingall, 40; Cue G.M. and Prospecting, 20 ; Mur-
chison Associated, 10 ; Hannan’s Main Reef, 10 ; Princess
Royal North, 10; Cumberland, 10; Lady Robinson, 10
(started); South Gippsland, 5; Bedchambers, Black
Range, 10 ; Greenmount, Southern Cross, 20 ; Nullagine,
20 ; Lalla Rookh, 10 ; Baden Powell, 10 ; Golden Ridge,
10 ; Lake Way, 10 ; Field’s Find, Yalgoo, 20 ; Mt. Jack-
son, 10.
* * * *
.- The Coolgardie water scheme (W.A.) has
proved a decided success, at least so far_ as conserving
water is concerned. The Mundaring dam is overflowing,
and contains 4600 million gallons—enough to last the
goldfields for the next three or four yean, so that the
water famine bogey is permanently squelched. There
has been great delay in connecting the mines with the
Mt. Charlotte reservoir, but this month should see the
work completed and all the mines fully supplied. This
will mean a considerable reduction in working costs, and
in cost of repairs, as the mineralised salt, and even con-
densed, water is very destructive to pipes, fittings,
pumps, boilers, &c. The mines have contracted to take
500,000 gallons daily at 6s. per 1000, and to allow
their salt water to run to waste. As this salt-water
amounts to well on for half-a-million gallons a day,
and will be supplemented by the overflow of fresh from
the mines, Boulder City may shortly have to buy an ark
* * * *
Between May and October, Mt. Morgan slumped
over a million and a-quarter in market value. Partly, no
doubt, it was due to the reaction from the Charters
To\vers boom ; but it was due still more to the mine’s own
results. In the July-September three months of 1902 the
co. got 37,2080 z. of" gold from 58,401 tons ; in the same
time this year it got only 26,0650 z. from 62,354 tons. The
fall in value of stone averaged 17s. 6d. per ton. The
steam shovel is at work now getting off more of the over-
burden, and pressure is put on in a dozen places to
straighten matters a little.
The Miners’ Accident Relief Bill, introduced to
Queensland Assembly and carried to the second reading,
runs on much the same lines as the Act which has been
doing fine work in N.S.W. for two years past. The prin-
cipal a teration is in the proportion of the contributions ;
in N.S.W., the miner pays 4Jd. per week and the co. and

it is proposed that each shall pay 3d. Instead, also, of
each mine having its local committee of management, as

holders of miners’ rights) should serve for each field.

The S.M. Herald says proudly that
George Reid has “burnt his boats” and that
his recent language makes it “ impossible for him
to join hands either with Labor members or with
Mr. Kingston.” The S.M. Herald doesn’t do
justice to the dodging capacity of its tin god. At
the very time the ill-informed paper made that
remark Reid was negotiating for the support of
the Labor party, and if terms can be arranged he
will take back all his unpleasant.remarks and say
that he never made them. It is a ease of a long
worm for a small hole, and there are many small
holes in politics for the kind of politician who
ikes holes.
Phil May in Melbourne.
On Thursday next, the 29th, Messrs. Gemmell,
Tuokett, and Cos. will auction the 139 original
Pen-Drawings by Phil May, which were reserved
for sale in Melbourne, and were not exhibited in
Sydney last month.
Some of May’s finest work—in portrait-drawing,
in cartoon work, and in humorous illustration—is
included among the lots that Messrs. Gemmell,
Tuckett, and Cos. will dispose of next week. It
was in Melbourne that Phil May passed the last
months of his Australian life, sketching the local
celebrities and identities, studying character at
the Centennial Exhibition, and enjoying a lively
time. His fame was growing daily—his Art was
then “on its mettle. ”
Nobody of Melbourne note in 1888 escaped Phil
May’s kindly caricature. Among the portrait
drawings there are likenesses of John (now
Chief Justice) Madden, Alfred Deakin, J. L.
Purves, David Syme, Duncan Gillies, the
mercurial L. L. Smith, Dr. Neild, Haddon, of the
Argus, and Editor Winter, of the Herald, Fitz-
Gibbon, “Eureka” Lalor, David Gaunson, Dr.
Bromby, the Finks (Theodore and B. J.), Butters
the Indestructible, Thomas Bent the engine-
drivers’ idol, Benjy Benjamin, Police Com-
missioner Chomley, Surgeon Fitzgerald, Panton,
P.M., Bryan OLoghlen, and Salmon Wil-
son, coupled with Henniker Heaton. A por-
trait of George Coppin is a striking example of
the artist’s genius. Miss Amy Sherwin figures as
the famous Australian songstress of ’BB, and many
theatrical people, looking round G.T.’s gallery,
will pause at the portrait of “Billy Elton, as Ben
Barnacle. ”
Some of the aforenamed men of the period are
again drawn in the well-remembered picture,
“On the Melbourne Block.” There are two fine
pages of travel-sketches ; and some remarkable
drawings satirising British politics—with like-
nesses of Queen victoria, the Prince of Wales
(now King Edward), Gladstone, Lord Salisbury,
and others. And there is the last Xmas Number
Supplement that Phil May did for The Bulletin
—a charming series of sketches illustrating some
parody verses by Edmund Fisher, entitled
“ Measuring the Baby.” There are some striking
drawings dealing with English subjects, which it
was originally intended to reserve for English
sale.. The whole of these have now been added to
the Melbourne catalogue. A number of examples
have been lost or destroyed ; others have been
sold privately; and this auction sale will be the
last that it is possible to make. Once dispersed,
it is unlikely that the drawings will ever again be
collected for exhibition.
The drawings will be on view at the gallery of
Gemmell, Tuckett and Cos., 359 Collins-street,
Melbourne, from to-day, and will be offered for
sale at 2 p.m. on Thursday, the 29th. Judging
from.the interest taken in the approaching sale,
it is expected that a good many Cup visitors will
hasten their arrival in order to be present at the
artistic “function.”

“ J.A.0.” : Ada Orossley is said to have once studied
the part of Lazarillo, in “ Maritana,” with the late
Madame Fanny Simonsen, at a time when Martin of that
ilk was running a very successful season of elderly and
middle-aged opera, and it was proposed to produce the
opera with Miss Crossley in the cast. She tried on her
boy’s costume one day at Madame’s house at St. Hilda,
and Harcourt Lee, Simonsen’s chorus master, almost
wept with joy over her magnificent proportions, and said
he didn’t care if she could sing or not; those limbs alone
would fix up the faded old opera for a long run. How-
ever, I do not believe that the performance ever came off
—worse luck 1

away a little thing which Marshall Hall composed for the
’cello and the ’cellist. Both the Hambourgs think you
can’t meet anywhere a finer musician than Marshall Hall,
