Callistimon writes—
Re Western Division boundaries (8., 3/9/’O3), the situa
tion in the west is this In 1884 half of the runs were
resumed for small holdings. Owners of stations were
invited to send in plans showing which half would suit
them best, and an incompetent Minister dealt with them.
Result was that mallee and porcupine country, the land
furthest from towns and rivers, and without frontage—
in short, the worst land that could possibly be got for
small holders was what was offered to them. Of Lake
Victoria (1,400,000 acres) 700,000 acres were resumed, and
not one yard of frontage to permanent water.
Even the Maoriland system of repurchase
and subdivision of estates, backed up by assistance
from the State Land Bank, is hardly able to
counterbalance the natural modern tendency ot
landed estates to grow bigger by swallowing the l r
small neighbors. There are, in fact, 1799 fewer
M.L. country owners than there were 10 years
ago. The very big estates are fewer; but the
very small ones are fewer also. _ The only per-
manent remedy is to decide what is the maximum
area one owner should hold, and make l* l ® gradu-
ated land-tax so heavy above that point that le
holding of a greater area is impossible. And as
the population increases the possible area must
decrease.
“ Creagh ” writes
The Bulletin’s remarks about unnecessary municipal
Councils In N.S.W., remind me of a county councilor
which I had some experience in a sister State. It was tne
most farcical menagerie of hairiness and bad sprung~"
ever adjourned for beer ; and it had a habit of debating
in a chorus. At meetings the members provKled
with writing paper which they generally left covered with
cabalistic arrangements of noughts and crosses, or para-
lytic attempts to spell the commonest monosvßaoies.
The business of the county, which was on a pretty large
scale, was bossed by the clerk and the engineer, the coun-
cillors being mostly too ignorant to do anythmgbut what
they were told. But, although a body of men to whom
the' very first principles of good manners were niaaen
knowledge, they had grown quite accustomed to consider
themselves fine gentlemen, and to make grave remarks
about the “ dignity of the council."
“ Spring,” looking down from the gallery :
A noticeable feature about N.S.W. Legislative Council
is its go-as-you-pleaseness. In the Lower House, when a
member loses a place or forgets a word, he turns red ana
the Speaker almost bursts. In the Upper House the time
wasted while the honorable and prodigiously incapable
member stumbles around looking for the right page
would turn a civil servant green with envy. Members of
the Assembly have a look of expectancy—seem always
expecting the great Loan crash. The Councillors politi-
cally expect nothing. The President sits inert in a chair
four sizes too big for him. He looks like a huddled-up
mass of torpid contentment. The pink-and-purple old
gentlemen who totter, and ramble in their speech,
and repeat each other’s speeches, sit round with a
feeling that nothing matters their end is near.
The visitors’ gallery is crowded with empty chairs
looking like the skeletons of the House’s vanished youth.
Occasionally a lost stranger drifts in, sidles into a seat,
sees the old men staring up at him, nervously pretends
to take an interest in the proceedings, and sneaks out.
Then the ancients comfort themselves that they are
working under the public eye. A Land and Income Tax
Amending Bill is being discussed. Out of their retire-
ment the old men have rattled up in sociables and large,
safe broughams. One sits on a separate chair with
his thumb in his mouth, vainly trying to follow the
business. It was cruel to drag him forth, but the sacred
cause of Property was at stake. While the majority of
them are shockingly out of place as legislators, there are
a few to whom it is a pleasure to listen ; these have been
put there, seemingly, to show up more thoroughly the
shocking dodderiness of the others. There is probably
no one in Australian public life better fitted to deal with
the Council than B. R. Wise. Distinguished-looking,
able, virile, bland, Barney goes straight to the old men’s
hearts. If he can, he leads them by argument. More
often he gets there by flattery. Nothing makes the
House of Dodder preen itself and strut around more
ridiculously than to be told, in Barney’s most cultured
manner, that “ it is a valuable and necessary portion of our
free Constitution.’’ A Councillor reminds one of the cob-
webs on ancient wine bottles. The wine within is just as
good without the cobwebs, but they give it an aged,
crusted appearance; and, unfortunately, Australia’s
mind still has an antique twist.
Laborist Keir Hardie in North American
•Review
Sir H. Campbell-Bannerman, the leader of the Liberal
party, in a recent speech, declared his belief that twelve
millions of our population were always living at, or Under,
the poverty line. In plain language, this means that
twelve millions of the British people are improperly fed,
insufficiently clothed, and inadequately housed. The
census returns tell us that 480,277 houses of one room are
registered in England, Scotland, and Wales, and that
these contain a living population of 1,571,504. From one
to two rooms is a very short step in' the" social scale,
but on the same authority from which we have just
quoted, we learn that 44 per cent, of the people of Scot-
land are accommodated in houses of one or two rooms.
Speaking with a great deal of practical experience, I
assert that in three cases out of five the householder of
two rooms will be found to be indulging in one or two
lodgers, from which it follows that a worse form of over-
crowding occurs than when there is only one apartment.
With this condition of things staring them in the face,
with no hope perceivable of any improvement, there is no
wonder that the more thoughtful leaders of the working
class have made up their minds to see how far a Labor
party can be instrumental in securing reforms.
Maffra (Vic.), the home of the dead-beet
anti-Federal-Capital movement, has more than
one public robbery to its discredit. In addition
to the sugar-beet factory, it lobbied out of the
Victorian Government a bush-shanty railway line
to somewhere in the back country, which is one of
the worst-paying of Victoria’s many hopeless
branches.


THE AUSTRALIAN ATTITUDE.
[FROM THE LONDON “TIMES ,” AUGUST 31,
1903.]
Australia has been perhaps more dis-
cussed of late than any other British
colony, both by investors who want to lend
money to it, and by politicians who for
Imperial purposes want to get money from
it. The one party is inclined to consider
it extravagant, the other laments its stin-
giness; both appeal to its “loyalty,” as
an argument relevant to the matter in
hand. It is possibly worth while, there-
fore, to consider what sort of plaoe Aus-
tralia really is, and what people inhabit
it; to appreciate more carefully the con-
ditions under which they live, and their
normal atttude towards outside affairs,
and so to understand a little better how
far the arguments and influences which
appeal to us have a like, or any, effect
upon them.
The Englishman of the home islands is
apt to believe in his heart that there is
only one pattern of Englishman—the pat-
tern by which he himself was cut. In
Canada, however, and in South Africa he
will admit (when he thinks of it) that
the influence of other white races and
the neighborhood of other civilized forms
of government have somewhat modified
conditions, and that the English settler in
Ontario or Natal may for that reason not be
a simple replica of the Londoner. But
he knows of no such influence in Austra-
lia; there, he thinks, in a continent un-
vexed by international politics and savage
wars, the English race breeds true to the
old stock, self-governing according to the
race’s approved institutions, disturbed only
by a malign force called the “Labor par-
ty,’’ that seems allied to the socialism of
Europe. Ealing and West Middlesex tem-
porarily under the thumb of Battersea and
West Ham—that idea of Australia, if not
avowed by nine men out of ten, is at any
rate implied in the fashion of their talk
about it and of the letters they write to
the ; London papers. It is not altogether
easy to blame them: they judge by what
they see.
THE AUSTRALIAN WHO LIVES IN ENGLAND.
But the Australians one meets in Eng-
land are of two types mainly, and neither
helps one much to understand his fellows
oversea. The politician is always with us,
chosen for his position either because his
predilections put him in touch with Eng-
lish feeling or because his conservatism
has put him out of touoh with Australian
feeling—for the reasons, that is, which
once made Lord Lytton ambassador to
France and sent Sir Stafford Nortbcote
to the House of Lords. We have with us
nowadays also the rich men come home
to enjoy their wealth—the squatters, alas!
too few, the mining speculators, perhaps
too many; and they are, from the very
nature of things, even less inclined to
sympathise with the mass of the people
whom they are here supposed to repre-
sent^
To say this is in no way to dispraise
them; it is one of Australia’s most set-
tled misfortunes to be little influenced by
the men whose guidance would be best
worth having; but if we are to under-
stand a nation we must study the major-
ity and the leaders it chooses, not those
who have left their country because either
they are disgusted with the majority or
the majority is tired of them.
THE AUSTRALIAN WHO VISITS ENGLAND.
The typical Australian rarely comes to
England. He is not rich enough, or not
interested enough. When he does come
—as one of a Lancer squadron, or with
manuscripts or mines to sell, or for a
snatched holiday among the art galleries
—he looks around him with a child’s curi-
ous eyes, sees half the outsides of things,
has sudden insights into the true mean-
ing of some facts we take too much for
granted, says little to us, and is all the
more voluble at home. We feel sure we
have impressed him—not in the least sus-
pecting how. He believes he has got at
the truth about us; and his version of
things seen sideways, never revised, and
soon, expanded by a bold imagination, is
spread widely among his comrades, to be-
come the plaything of untutored and half-
malicious fantasy. We hopefully take
him for a docile child; he is apt to think
us as clumsily cunning, as stupid, and as
greedy as are giants in fairy tales. Both
estimates are wrong, but one not more so
than the other. Here are the elements of
a very pretty misunderstanding—one
which, but for the late war, would have
already had serious results; which will yet
have very serious results, if we persist in
believing that a war which we have more
than half forgotten is likely to be longer
remembered with enthusiasm at the Anti-
podes.
THE AUSTRALIAN IN AUSTRALIA.
Who, then, is the “typical Australian”?
I do not mean the best type, I mean
the ordinary type; for our business is
with the mass of citizens whose voice
makes Australian policy.
At once we are brought face to face
with an effect of Federation which has not
yet been , clearly understood. Ten years
ago the politics of the several States were
decided for them, each in its own chief
town. Sydney governed New South
Wales; Melbourne, one may almost say,
was Victoria; the interests of Brisbane
were supreme in the Queensland Legis-
lature, except when some more northern
seaport made itself disagreeable in the
hope of beooming anew colony’s capital.
In each case a concentrated population of
seaport townsfolk held its own with ease
against the scattered and disunited bush
electorates; and townsfolk—seaport folk
more especially—are much alike through-
out British territories. The various classes
live together in bulk; thedr profession or
trade dominates them, rather than their
place of abode. Lawyer discusses burn-
ing questions with lawyer, merchant with
merchant, bricklayer with bricklayer, be
it in Sydney or Toronto or Brisbane; in
each place, as a rule, they oome to the
lawyer’s or merchant’s or bricklayer’s con-
clusion. Furthermore, the element of situ-
ation in Sydney politics, when it did in-
trude, came not so much from having New
South Wales behind iit as from having
Melbourne just round the comer; in rail-
way-making, for instance, the Sydney Leg-
islature’s object was rather to secure a
district’s trade for Sydney (as against Mel-
bourne) than to develop it for the good
of the colony at large. The country dis-
tricts grumbled, but had not sufficient
union among themselves to make very act-
ive opposition. So lines were constructed,
and rates were fixed, to the great advan-
tage of the Sydney middleman; and the
use of the railways to carry Sydney news-
papers up country free of charge helped
the process of concentration still further,
by stifling the growth of an independent
provincial Press.
THE TWO AUSTRALIANS.
Ten years ago, therefore, or even later,
the typical Australian for political pur-
poses was the townsman —a man eminent-
ly parochial, as all townsmen are, but
swayed (when his own town’s interests
were not specially concerned) by much the
same influences as affect, say, Liverpool.
The politicians, indeed—we shall come to
them later—are a class apart. But the
professional men and merchants and trades-
people and artisans are of the type we
know, and the “Labor” members of Sydney
or Melbourne, or even Broken Hill, do
not differ widely from those whom Bat-
tersea and the Welsh miners send, or
would like to send, to Westminster. The
Legislatures, roughly speaking, consisted
of a Lower House, something like a Lon-
don borough council, and an Upper House,
resembling an enlarged Bench of not too
enlightened county magistrates. These
were the people with whom travellers (dis-
tinguished ones especially) came into con-
tact, a,nd whom British statesmanship had
to take into account.
Federation has considerably altered all
this. For seven States, each ruled by a
compact body of townsfolk taking advan-
tage of divided country districts, it is
substituting one Commonwealth, ruled by
a fairly compact body of country folk tak-
ing advantage of the divisions between
the large towns. The change is by no
means complete yet. The country party
does not know its own strength. It did
not at the first elections appreciate tho
greatness of its gain in having an Upper
House chosen by manhood suffrage instead
of by Ministerial nomination or by the
restricted suffrages of some States. Still
leaderless, distrusting alike the Sydney
freetraders and the protectionist manu-
facturers of Melbourne, it is inclined to
join with the “Labor” members of the
towns, with whom it has certain definite
aims in common. The Queenslander, who
wants a white Australia, and therefore
demands the stoppage of Kanaka labor in
his State, is easily persuaded to accept as
part of his programme the abolition of
Lascar labor in mailboats, which is purely
a trade union question of seaport inter-
est. There are Socialist leagues in the
cities, whose doctrine the average bush-
man will repudiate if you challenge him;
but in the actual laws they propose he
sees immediate advantage, and so joins
hands with them over matters of practical
politics without feeling himself thereby
committed to their political or philosoph-
ical views. He is not necessarily for that
reason a Socialist- he may not even be
a trade unionist. If he sees no advantage,
he is just as ready to oppose “Labor ”;
the Victorian farmers, jealous of town ag-
grandisement, have joined the Conserva-
tives against tihe city Labor Party there.
He is just an up-country Australian bring-
ing into politics the methods of his daily
life.
THE BUSHMAN —THE BIG, GROWN CHILD.
What his daily life is no one knows who
has not shared it. It has never been
completely put into writing. Henry Law-
son has oome nearest to doing that, but
most of his work deals rather with the
varying fantastic kaleidoscope of the road-
side and inn verandah than with the mon-
otonous and lonely round of bush work.
Observers who watch him are apt to make
too little of his troubles, because they
do not seem to have broken his spirit;
observers who mark his surroundings—
their oomfortlessness, their continual pet-
ty tragedy—often make too melancholy a
picture of him.
His life is one long struggle with the
unexpected, that stiffens his self-depend-
ence and cultivates his sense of humor.
Whatever his racial characteristics may be
—Scot, or Erse, or the dogged, half-intelli-
gent persistence of the Englishman—they
are moulded to one shape by stress of
this fact, that he lives beneath a sky which
gives him no sure rainfall, upon a soil
too porous to hold for any length of time
the rain that may come. So he be.comes
a gambler and a nomad; he wagers his
year’s work against the caprices of Na-
ture, and, if he loses, goes far afield with
a (comparatively) light heart to try his
luck elsewhere. Above all, in such sur-
roundings he remains perpetually a child.
He is bound to go tentatively, step by
step, through a world whose laws he does
not understand, and he brings to bear- on
it a child’s wide-eyed inquisitiveness, a
child’s fearless bearing in situations whose
real danger is hidden from him, a child’s^
irrepressible enthusiasm for ideas that will
be forgotten in a week. Like a child, he
is good at makeshifts, and often prefers
them to more permanent arrangements..
And he is especially like a child in this,
that his code of social ethics is based on
the family. The bush folk are of his
family, every one of them ipso facto a
mate of his, to be welcomed and treated
as such unless some meanness demands ex-
pulsion; outsiders are for that very reason
to be suspected, people to whom he owes
few or no duties except that of hospitality
—though the best of them may, after due
trial made, be admitted among his com-
rades. Within that family it is the car-
dinal virtue to be “straight,” and proper-
ty is shared to an extent that might al-
most be called oommunism. What goes
on outside, interests the bushman immense-
ly when he has time to think of it, but
not necessarily as a matter affecting him-
self ; he regards most of politics in this
light, and has been known to send a mem-
ber to Parliament much as a boy puts
pennies in the slot of some mechanical de-
vice—just to see what will happen. The
part of political affairs which does unmis-
takably affect him is in his mind a form
of weather, to be used or endured as sun
and rain are, without ethical scruples;
he revels in great loan expenditure as in
good rains, and bears taxation with the
same spirit that carries him through
drought.
COMRADESHIP V. COMPETITION.
Some day, no doubt, he will grow up.
He will learn that Parliaments are not
meant solely for the provision of bridge-
building or scrub-cleaning jobs, or even
for the ensuring of higher wages to shear-
ers. He will learn to regard life with
more forethought and less cheerfulness, to
strive cunningly for what he wants in-
stead of requiring it openly, to be more
lavish of his assurances and stingier with
his enthusiasm. Politicians will find him
easier to deal with, perhaps, when his
mind is filled with a belief, not in com-
radeship, but in competition.
For my part, I prefer him as he is. But,
for all who value him as a part of the
Empire, who wish to keep him in it for
its sake more than for his own, to leaven
with his fine youthfulness our middle-aged
civilization, that very youthfulness has
one great disadvantage. These child’s
qualities of his include the most perilous
quality of all; he is so much misunder-
stood, and so resentful of being misunder-
stood, that he accepts whole-heartedly, and
without examination, the friendship and
advice of any one who seems genuinely
sympathetic. Once convince him that you
have some skill in his pursuits and some
share, in his feelings, and you gain a hold
on him that may never be shaken off.
Wherefore he is prone at times to fall
among strange friends and under influ-
ences not altogether desirable.
“ THE BULLETIN ” : IMPORTANT, DANGEROUS,
BEYOND SUSPICION, CANDID, GRIM.
Among these influences the most import-
ant—and most dangerous—is that of The
Bulletin. It is hard to over-estimate the
extent to which thia journal modifies the
opinions (one might almost say the char-
acter) of its readers. Most Australian
newspapers alter no one’s opinion, being
read only by those who already agree with
them; one or two only—the Melbourne
Age is the most notable example—have at
times directed from their editorial chair
the policy of Ministries. But this has
come about beoause their readers know
Who is talking. The organ (real or sup-
posed) of some “ ring ” or clique is suspected ;
the organ controlled by a single man and
used to express his personal views becomes
for that reason credible, and sometimes
convincing. The Bulletin is beyond sus-
picion in these matters; its editorial col-
umns express the definite beliefs of two
or three individuals whom its readers
know—not of any party; its candor verges
on the cynioal, but the Australian has no
objection to humor in his politics or
grimness in his jests. Moreover, it is con-
sistent and persistent; its policies are not
shaped by the rise and fall of parties or
by the vagaries of individual politicians;
it will back any man, just so far as he
will carry out some fragment of,its pro-
gramme and not an inch further; and,
above all, it insists that each of these
fragments is a part of a definite, well-or-
dered plan. Thus every valuable measure
it advocates (and it has advocated many)
becomes one more step towards its ulti-
mate goal; and the bushman, deeply im-
pressed by a steadfastness and a disregard
of personalities, which he does not natur-
ally share, is being led to accept Federa-
tion and honest finance as arguments in
favor of independence and a republic.
That in its most pronounced form is
The Bulletin’s desire. To that consumma-
tion all its efforts are bent. For guiding
mein’s minds in that direction it finds no
casuistry too subtle, no caricature too
gross. (One thinks, indeed, that its own
vehemence has often damaged! the cause
it backs.)
“JOHN BULL-COHEN.”
With all its eloquence, literary and pic-
torial, it strives to make the bushman
believe in John Bull-Cohen, the griping
moneylender, the slave-driver, the nation
whose only interest in anything human or
divine is centred in its money value. Ava-
rice and treachery, cowardice and stupid
meanness, all the vices the bushman hates
most, are combined in the Bull-Cohen mon-
ster. There is no sneer, remember, at the
race to which the Australian mainly be-
longs ; The Bulletin’s point is that the
Briton no longer governs himself in Eng-
land—that he is here the victim of greedy
British financiers, and that his kin in Aus-
tralia should cut loose before the same
grip is irrevocably tightened on them.
It would seem that so gross a carica-
ture must defeat its own ends. It does,
in fact, hinder them. But we must re-
member that it is there in front of the
hushman’s eyes week after week. He may be
(I cannot say that he always is) indignant
at first, scornful after a little; then he
gets used to it, passes it oyer, accepts it
as a part of the paper, like the color of
the cover. Still, it is there! Then some
little thing happens, some awkward phrase
is quoted from a London newspaper, some
trifling friction occurs between the Im-
perial. Government anid a State Ministry;
at once it is symbolized by clever artists
in anew attribute of Bull-Cohen, and the
caricature is brought in the bushman’s
mind one degree nearer reality.
DETESTED, YET READ.
Other influences, too, are at work. The
Bulletin is not a political journal only;
it is the nurse and the critic, sometimes
severe but sometimes friendly, of every
young Australian who wants to write about
the things he feels and sees. It will print,
and pay for, anything connected with Aus-
tralia that is put clearly or said well, be
it love-verse or snake-story, or note on
the habits of a blackfellow tribe. It is
read for this reason by very many who
detest its politics: and they, too, have
under their eyes week by week the grue-
some features of Bull-Oohen.
Moreover, in political matters the edit-
or’s lead is not often unmistakably fan-
atic and wrong-headed. It was The Bul-
letin that, when Bourke teamsters rioted
against the competition of camels driven
by Pathans, told them plainly (and got it-
self much disliked therefor) that they
might object to Pathans in the name of
a white Australia, but that camels had
come to stay. It was The Bulletin that
educated hush Australia up to Federa-
tion.
THE ONE PAPER THAT PREACHES HONEST
FINANCE.
It is The Bulletin that has steadily
preaohed honest finance; that has year
after year denounced tiie rottenness of
using loan money for repairs and insigni-
ficant non-reproduotive public works; tnat
has persistently told the Labor party,
“It is your duty, not only to insist on free
education and old-age pensions, and the
minimum wage for Government employees,
but to see also that the necessary funds
are provided by straightforward taxation
and by economy in non-essential matters.”
Here are two extracts from a recent lead-
er, worth studying as a sample of what
this journal teaches and demands from
its foiiowers :
The Bulletin led 'the way in denounc-
ing the borrowing policy, the hush-it-up
policy, the policy of drift, the policy of
being a financial ostrich and burying
this country’s joint and several head in
the* sand. It was the first paper to per-
sistently declare that the Australian
wasn’t necessarily a poor, feeble, helpless
being, unable to get on without per-
petual loans; that he was as good a
man—if he only knew it—as the Dane,
or the Boer, or the Prussian, and as
able to pay his own way, and keep his
national finances clean; that this “coun-
try of magnificent natural resources”
could pay for its own roads and bridges
and court-houses and asylums, and do its
repairs to public buildings, and buy
crockery for the Jeinolan accommoda-
tion house, without pawning itself to
Bull for money- and that a regular an-
nual deficit and a constant cooking of
the accounts, and an interminable whin-
ing about our inability to pay taxes,
and a persistent lie to the effect that
benevolent asylums and dredging opera-
tions and the like are “permanent and
reproductive works” were not necessarily
a part of the Australian character. It
was the first paper which pointed out,
in and out of season, that a country
with the biggest debt per head on this
earth was in a dangerous situation, and
that the perpetual increase of that debt
must mean national insolvency, just as
surely as the perpetual getting worse of
a man who is already ill must mean
death. It was the first paper to de-
clare that the helpless frame of mind
induced by constant dependence on loans
waa preparing the way for repudiation.
For ten years or more The Bulletin has
constantly advocated, not the repudia-
tion, but the repayment of our public
debt, and it has stood almost alone in
holding the theory that the Australian
was as capable of independence as the
Frenchman, or the Yankee, or the Ger-
man
There is no easy remedy for the trou-
ble. It is ,the result of 30 jears of
steadily increasing folly, of silly braying
about imaginary, riches, of refusal to
pay sufficient taxes to keep the country
sqlvent, of insane extravagance and in-
competence. The only honest way back
p,ow is the long, hard road uphill. If
the Australian Government will take
over the existing State debts (also if
the silly, parochial vanity of ihe Stato
Governments;will let it do so) and pledge
itself (the State Governments joining in
the pledge) to borrow no more till they
are paid off, a.nd will establish even a
small sinking fund for their repayment,
it can probably renew the old loans, even
now as they fall due at such a reduction of
interest as will itself more than cover
the sinking fund. After that it will re-
main for the States to cut off every item
of needless expenditure (the hospital
subsidy and the old-age pension vote are
not needless expenditure and are not
included), to sack their agents-general,
to reduce their legislatures .to one House
each, to put up with a plain lieutenant-
governor each at £IOOO a year, to sup-
press the blatant draper who wants £75

Q. A#
Q- I have been feeling very poorly lately, and have just been told by my doctor that I am suffer-
ing from uric poisoning. I shall be glad if you will tell me just what uric poisoning means. Is it a
serious matter ?
P. oisonin ? is caused by the retention in the blood of various substances which should
leave the body in solution. The retention of these substances is due to a diseased or inactive condi-
tion of the kidneys. When the kidneys are working perfectly, they filter and extract from the blood
of the average individual about three pints of urine every day. In this quantity of urine should be
dissolved various waste material produced by the wear and tear of the tissues of the body. This is
dead matter, and its presence in the blood is poisonous. The three pints of normal urine should con-
tain about ten grains in weight of uric acid, an ounce of urea, together with other animal and mineral
matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
Q. Then I am to understand that the substances you mention when not eliminated from the body
m th® natural manner constitute what is known as uric poisoning. What are the usual symptoms bv
which the presence of these poisons is manifested ?
A. Now you are asking a rather large question. Many complaints which are commonly called
diseases are not actually diseases in themselves. For instance, Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia
J* u “ l hag , o, Sciatica, Gravel, Stone, and Bladder Troubles, are all caused by uric poisons'
Indigestion, Anaemia, Persistent Headache and General Debility are often solely due to
the same cause. In fact, if the kidneys are doing their work freely and thoroughly, none of the
complaints mentioned could trouble us as the causative poisons would be absent.
Q. I had no idea that so much depended upon the efficient action of the kidneys. I suppose
that when anyone is suffering from Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Sciatica
Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles, Anaemia, Debility, Persistent Headache or
Indigestion, the scientific method of effecting a cure would be to directly treat the patient for the
kidneys ?
Exactly. In fact, that is the only way in which a radical and permanent cure can be effected
I he kidneys must be restored to health and activity, so that they may be enabled to remove the daily
production of poisons in the body, or the patient must continue to suffer.
Q. I have always been under the impression that the liver had a great deal to do with the
maintenance of our general health, but it seems that the kidneys are the chief cause of most of the
disorders from which we suffer?
A. The work done by the liver is of the utmost importance and it is closely associated with the
work done by the kidneys. Indeed, when anything is the matter with the liver the kidneys are almost
always directly affected, and the contrary is likewise true. In the liver various substances are actually
made from the blood. Iwo or three pounds of bile are thus made every day. The liver takes sugar
from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to
the blood, gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is
insoluble, into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles
which have lived their life and are useful no longer.
Q. As the functions of the kidneys and liver are so intimately related, I gather that if there is
reason to suspect that either organ is not doing its work efficiently, a curative agent should be
employed which would act equally upon the kidneys .and liver?
A. Yes, that is the case, and it wa3 the realization of this important fact which lead to the dis-
covery of that invaluable medicine Warner’s Safe Cure. About thirty years ago, certain medical
men, knowing that if they could find a medicine which would beneficially affect alike the kidneys and
liver, they could control most of the common disorders, devoted themselves to the search for such a
remedy., After many disappointments, their efforts were rewarded, and a medicine now known as
Warner s bafe Cure was proved to possess the required properties in the fullest degree. Warner’s
Safe Cure has a marvellously stimulating and healing effect upon both the kidneys and liver and
by restoring those vital organs to health and activity, it necessarily cures all disorders due to the
retention in the blood of urinary and biliary poisons, such as

Even Bright’s Disease, probably the most fatal of all diseases, yields to treatment by

A PAPER WHOSE INFLUENCE GROWS DAILY.
When we remember that the Australian
likes straight talk, and that no other
paper has the habit of such straight talk
as this; that the ideal it insists on, of
a country free from mortgages, is the
ideal which every drought-stricken selector
hopes for as he hopes for heaven; that
the people it holds up to soortn are those
whom the bushman already abhors—the
absentee, the useless official, the legislator
who will sell his vote—'need we wonder
that this paper’s influence grows daily,
that rebuffs (such as those it suffered in
the earlier war-days) do it no permanent
harm, and that its most extravagant as-
sertions come dangerously near accept-
ance in the minds of a majority of Aus-
tralians ?
A SORT OE DEVIL’S CONSCIENCE.
Some will think, no doubt, that this is
making too much fuss about a single news-
paper!—though no one will who knows the
hush. In any case, it was worth while to
discuss this one at length, because it is
the summing up of the anti-imperial
spirit.
We in England are apt to think that
the expressed loyalty of Legislatures and
the daily Press is the unanimous voice of
a continent, and that the cry separa-
tion may be neglected as mere socialist
and demagogic bitterness. At any rate,
we believe, nothing can alter such patriot-
ism as the Common wealth displayed dur-
ing the late war. But the history of that
outburst has yet to be written. It was
a spasm of intense feeling, preceded by—
and likely to be succeeded by—a period
of growing indifference to things outside
the Southern Pacific; and all through it,
like the voice of a sort of devil’s con-
science, the bushman heard his Bulletin
murmuring persistently, “Yes, yes, you’re
a little excited now, but you will get over
it, and then you’ll see what a fool you
have made of yourself.” No Britom likes
to think he has played the fool rampant-
ly, and the Australian is already uneasy
about his recent behavior. If we, from
ignorance or from stupidity, play into the
hands of his Anti-British mentors, we may
find him marching out of the house in
a huff before we know that any dispute
has really begun.
THE “bulletin” MORE AUSTRALIAN THAN THE
PARLIAMENTS.
For we shall be trusting no doubt to
Ministerial statements and the resolutions
of Assemblies. And it must never be for-
gotten that in Australia Ministers and As-
semblies do not necessarily represent the
opinions of the electors, except in mat-
tern which they were expressly elected to
deal with. At an election there may be
no important issue raised; in that case
the voter chooses a candidate whom he
likes personally, or one whom he thinks
best fitted to secure for his district a
ran share of public money. Or there may

voter’s personal views of that issue, In
that point (and in connexion with the dis-
tribution of public money) the elected
member is pledged to vote as his electing
majority desire; on other matters he is
usually left free to do as he will; but
ms actions do not bind his constituents.
He can vote and talk as he likes, how-
ever much _ they disagree with him, until
the point in dispute becomes one of im-
mediate importance in their minds; then
jj ik® about his business. Thus
suddenly fell nearly all the State Minis-
tries in 1899, after holding offioe for a
period unheard of before; they hadl been
Kept m power to carry through Federa-
tion, and might do what else they liked
till it was safe—but not afterwards. So,
too, none of its other virtues will save
the present Commonwealth Ministry if its
increase of the naval subsidy happens to
occupy a prominent place in the mind of

THE INFLUENCES FOE SEPARATION —
How can such a protest be averted, and
how much we counteract the influences
which work for separation? (I assume
that we do not want separation; if we do,
we nay be quite content with things as
they are.) Not, certainly, by denying
their existence or minimizing their im-
portance; not by asserting that no one
troubles about the opinions of the most
widely ead journal in Australia, or abus-
ing those opinions in bulk as mere ranting
Socialism. The essence of the danger lies
in this—that the arch-Separatists combine
with their Bull-Cohen extravagance, plaus-
ible and often sound direction on matters
purely Australian, while the loudest shout-
ing for the Imperial connexion comes from
those who first (as in New South Wales)
pile up their State debt with unconscion-
able borrowings and then (as in Victoria)
try to readjust their finances by niggardly
retrenchment in the money-earning bran-
ches of the public service.
Any one who will inquire into the earn-
ings (actual and probable) of New South
Wales branch lines—called for loan pur-
poses “reproductive”—or into the recent
administration of Victorian railways, will
soon understand what the last sentence
means. And the remedy is, by changes of
Ministry or of policv to dissociate Imper-
ialism from bad local administration. The
Australian, more than most people, pro-
ceeds from the known to the unknown;
he judges of men’s trustworthiness in
things outside his knowledge mainly by
thear skill in matters on which he is
an expert. An Australian Bishop once
met a squatter of his diocese to whose dis-
trict he had recently sent anew curate.
“Look here, my lord.” the squatter burst
out, “I wouldn’t trust him with a flock
of scrubbers across the home paddock; do
you think I’ll trust him with my immor-
tal soiil?’’ Nor is the bushman likely to
trust much, in matters of high policy, meu
whom he believes negligent or wrong-
headed in State affairs.
AND THE REMEDY.
This remedy, however, is for application
in Australia by the men out there; what
is there for us to do in England? In the
first place, it may be said, we have to
revise our standards of “loyalty,” and to
alter our attitude towards the rest of the
Empire. If the Australian is a child in
many of his ways—his easily hurt pride,
his intense but temporary devotions, his
quick forgettings, his unexpected points
of view—we seem sometimes to have
reached an age at which relatives find one
more burdensome and far less attractive.
One knows people who have grown so self-
centred, so incapable of conceiving any
other centre of their world, that with the
kindest possible intentions they make fam-
ily life unbearable. They sincerely believe
that all their children are happiest when
serving them. “I would not for the world,”
says such a parent, “leave one wish of
my child’s unsatisfied • but dear Dora cares
for nothing but my comfort.” And dear
Dora, if she is not crushed out of indi-
viduality altogether one day flares up un-
accountably and runs away, much to the
scandal of the neighbors and the surprise
of a loving and forgiving mother.
THE TRUE IMPERIAL CREED.
Perhaps the first article of the true Im-
perial creed should run thus:—“The colo-
nies do not belong to Eingland. They, and
England, belong to the Empire.” If that
is our creed, we have an unhappy knack
of seeming to disbelieve it. It certainly
is the creed of the colonies; if we wish
to argue Imperial matters with them, we
must either accept it as ours or try to
alter their belief in it. To ignore it only
makes matters worse. To say, “But you
are England’s; think of what you owe to
the Englishmen who founded you,” pro-
vokes the easy retort: “We are the sons
of those Englishmen, and know what we
owe to them. You are the sons of the
Englishmen who did not found colonies—
what do we owe to you?” To talk about
loyalty brings up the question, “Loyalty
to what P” and if he say, “To the Empire,”
one answer is, “Let the Empire, then,
judge of our loyalty”; the significance of
which is not understood till we appreciate
how great an intimacy has sprung up dur-
ing war-time between the men of all Bri-
tish colonies. England is still t'he head
of the family, no doubt; but the headship
is now not so much that of a father among
his small children as that of the eldest
son among younger brothers.
HUMOR HIM A LITTLE—
It will be also worth our while to study
colonial feeling more closely, and to let
sentiment have its way sometimes, even
in the face of logic. We are not really a
logical people, though we sometimes ap-
peal to logic when it is quite in our fav-
or. . Thus we chose the time when Aus-
tralia was rather proud of itself for having
given men and money in an Imperial cause
to demand a larger contribution towards
the joint permanent naval expenditure.
Logic in this case was so much on our
side that some of us (in the name of Im-
perial Federation, too!) have been anxious
to cry “Pay or part company!”—a dan-
gerous challenge indeed. But simultane-
ously we were illogical enough to require
that the Australian section of the Fleet
should not be tied down to the defence of
Australian ports at the very moment when
we were creating a Home Squadron for
the express purpose of defending English
ports. There may have been excellent
reasons for adopting both courses, but
their concurrence is unfortunate in the im-
pression it makes on the colonial mind.
Nothing could well he more logical than
a certain tax on tea of which historians
tell us; and few acts of British legisla-
tion have been more ill-judged.
Again, we might take account of the
fact that Australians are very suspicious
of decorated and betitled politicians.
Rightly or waiongly, they have been led
to the conclusion that knighthoods and
K.C.M.G.’s are payment for services ren-
dered (or to be rendered) to the Imperial
Government; and the best men in Aus-
tralian political life now either decline
these honors suavely or accept them with
ldsgiving. Of course, such suspicions are
unjust and wrong: but by understand-
ing and humoring them a little we might
at least manage not to weaken the influ-
ence of our friends.
AND TRY 'lO KNOW HIM BETTER.
However, to examine in detail possible
alterations in our own behaviour is beyond
tne scope of this article. It is a plea for
closer study, rather than an attempt to
forecast the conclusions of that study. No
two. people see the same rainbow ; I do
not suppose that every student will see
the Australian just as I have seen him.
I have, maybe, laid stress too exclusively
on the distinctive points in his character,
slurring the question how far they are
modified by the more ordinary qualities
common to all the race. The picture drawn
here may not be the whole truth; it is
the truth, and nothing but the truth.
These are motives, and among the chief
motives, that stimulate him to action;
these are influences —there are few more
powerful —that guide his decisions. And
it is because we need him in the Empire
—him and his youthfulness, his joy in
comradeship, his alert mind and unbreak-
able will—that I fear lest careless words
and negligent assumptions and pedantic
departmentalisms should drive him, alien-
ated from us, into the arms of the narrow
and fanatic Separatist.
Senator Tilman (U.S.A.) on lynching : —
Let the {rood work go on. We have shot and hanged
and burned the negro in North Carolina until he almost
knows his place there.
That’s the ruffian spirit that must arise in Aus-
tralia if a colored race is ever allowed to settle
here. White Australia is most of all necessary
for the black man.
There is trouble in the Melbourne G.P.O.
Some officials there were feeling thirsty, and they
called upon one of their number, a Cold Tea
gentleman, to pay for drinks. The C.T.G.
offered his favorite beverage, which was refused
with contumely. Then, ’tis said, when the Cold
Tea gentleman departed to his work, the Hot Rum
gentlemen took the C.T.G.’s bicycle lamp and
pawned it, and, having drunk the Cold Tea
gentleman’s health, they presented him with the
pawn-ticket. The Cold Tea gentleman didn’t see
the humor of the incident, and reported the
matter to the Deputy-P.M.G. ; a large and violent
inquiry followed, and the prospects of thoso
Hot Rum gentlemen are said to be gloomy.

The Mill,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I stood beside an Ancient Mill
Upon a day of dream,
No sound of life I heard, and still
As slumber flowed the stream.
I saw the white flood race and reel,
And plunge to depths profound;
The vast, inexorable Wheel
Turned slowly round and round.
The faces in the stream swept by,
So far away, so near;
And there was Clamor to the eye,
But Silence to the ear.
And I beheld the vanished years
Of human history:
Mine eyes were filled with sudden tears —
Why came this sight to me ?
Far down the Flood went Race and Creed :
Plato of Academe,
And Moses white, like wisps of weed,
Were borne along the stream.
I saw the purple empires pass,
The kings of old Romance,
Like dying breath upon a glass,
Like visions in a trance.
I saw a drowned hand gloved in mail,
And for a moment shone
The eyes of Caesar, and the pale
Face of Napoleon.
Helen, the fairest of all lands,
And Deirdri and Elaine,
Their white, appealing, jewelled hands
Stretched out to me in vain.
The might of Rome, with face aghast,
And Greece, with gods and song,
With all the empires of the past
Were swept like straws along.
And stars and systems down the flood
Flashed onward silently;
The life was frozen in my blood,
My heart was drawn from me.
The stream ran on with even flow,
My feet the systems kissed—
And then I heard a laughter low,
Malicious in the mist.
The Lord’s Prayer, trembling I began,
But I forgot the Prayer ;
I saw the grim Red Fisherman
For empires fishing there.
I saw an Ancient sitting still—
In sleep his head did nod ;
It was the Owner of the Mill
That was the Mill of God.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Cooks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Paddt the Scholar calculates that 78‘5 per cent,
of all males in the mulga are now cooks finished
cooks, with references. Every station with scrub
has been cutting it down, for six years and more,
for sheep-feed, consequently there arose a great
demand for cooks. These had to be obtained
somehow, so that anybody who could boil water
without burning it was put into the galley.
The aforesaid Paddy, a product of the Victorian
school system, has been in nearly every scrub-
cutting camp in the West, and is looked upon as
a great authority on scrub-cutting ethics. He
classes cooks into two species^—those who require
that all hands shall sinooge, and those who smooge
to all hands. He names several varieties and
numerous sub-varieties. Two in particular of the
varieties he mentions with loathing ; the first is
the cook who is good, but leaves at a moment’s
notice; the other, not quite so bad, is
the cook who has, besides the usual roast and
fry, only one dish. This kind is usually called
after his “shef-dover.”
Nowadays the first question a new man on a job
asks is not “What sort of bloke is 4 the bloke but
“What sort of a poisoner have yer got?” And
that settles whether the new man stops on or not.
It may be remarked here that “the bloke” is
generio for “boss.”
Talkin’ about cooks, we had a fair pull at old
Leader’s leg last summer. There was a real good
crowd of chaps in the camp, not a “scab” among
them. They nearly all had stands to go to by-
and-by, and so didn’t care much whether they
stopped on the job or not. We had old Flapjack
Bob for a cook, and it was flapjacks mornin’, noon
an’ night. We had ’em with plums, with curran’s,
with bullocky’s joy. We had ’em flat an’ rolled,
and in fancy shapes he cut out with old tins.
Mick Smith was the first to leave the job,
an’ he got his note and went into the frontage
for his cheque. Yer know old Leader is
by way of bein’ a philanthropist, and asks
questions, “What’ll yer do with the money?
Don’t, like a good man, spend it on the cursed
drink,” and all that sorter stuff. He was soft
enought to ask Mick why he was leavin’. Mick
told him that the cook was all right (he didn’t
want the chap to lose his job), but there wasn’t
. enough flapjacks in the kewseen to suit him; he
was very fond of ’em. All right.
Then Jim Davis goes in for his cheque. He
told Leader that he was going where ’e could get
flapjacks. The old man said nothing. All right.
After that Sammy Gibbs went in and told him
that he liked the job, the money, and the cook,
but there wasn’t flapjacks on the table often
enough. “That’s funny,” says the old chap;
“ the other men left for the same reason. ” Sammy
said that if he’d known that, he would ’a’ spoke to
the bloke about it. All right.
When Combo Thomson went in an’ gave the
same reason, old Leader sez, “I can’t have the
men leaving for such a trifle as flapjacks.” He
was simple to think that in the bush a man gives
the true reason why he’s tired of a job. He don’t
always know, himself. Anyway, the old man gets
his horses in, and drove out to the camp. The
chaps were all out at the scrub. The very first
thing he sez to the cook was, “ W’y the ’ell don’t
you give the men flapjacks?” Of course, when
we all came in to dinner and flapjacks, with
“joy ” on ’em, “ the bloke ” gave the old chap the
full strength of the thing; and now I hear Leader
don’t ask any man why he leaves.
At another place I was on, summer before last,
near Bourke, there was a woman died, and her
man wanted to do things stylish and give ’er a
real coffin. There was only one carpenter on the
whole place, an’ he was in our camp. We had a
German cove cookin’ for us called Dutch ’Arry, a
real poisoner. He’d heard of Blue Monge and
dished it up to us first day—’ot, with burnt sugar
in the gravy. Holy MbSe's ! We used to tucker
at a long table made Of G-inCh boards, and this
was a proper place for a carpenter’s bench ;
accordingly the carpenter built the coffin on it.
Just before it was finished it was standing on its
end on the ground, leaning against the table, when
out comes Murdoch, the manager, to have a look
at the sheep. We was at dinner when he rode up
an’ I seen him look very shy at the box. You
know old Packsaddle? Well, he up and said,
“Look here, boss, it’s hard lines. We are bein’
poisoned one end of the table, and our coffin’s
waitin’ for us at the other. ’Tain’t decent.”
Dutch Harry swore about it till he was nearly
dead and chucked up the job, an’ I took it on for
a few days until they could get a cook from town.
I was all right on soda bread and Widgee roll,
but when I tried my hand at yeast bread !!
I used three-parts ot a bag of flour, and had
batches of dough buried all over the place, for
fear of the chaps laughing. I thought one of
them must come right. I never worked so hard
in my life, but the stuff wouldn’t rise when I
wanted it. It wasn’t the yeast either, for next
day there was the stuff risin’ from the grave all
round the camp. The last batch I tried bad two
bottles of Dutchy’s yeast in it, and it rose out of
the sand about 18in., like a great big mushroom.
“The bloke” walked right into one I buried
behind his tent, and it nearly hobbled him.
You’ve heard of ropy bread. Well, it was that.
There’ll have to be an inspector of cooks soon,
like they have for boilers, or we’ll all be in the
grave before our time; or else be cookin’ for our-
selves, which is nearly as bad.
Q.
TARBOY.
“ Burglar Bill ” : I think the Comptroller-
General of Prisons should be asked to supply a
pair of leg-irons to hitch the Cardinal and the
Primate together when they promenade at public
functions. Then if either should fall, through
slipping on the stair carpet, the other would be
compelled to wait until he gets up. Besides, a
young Cardinal or Primate might be inclined to
take advantage of his youthfulness and get in
front of the older man, and thus stir up trouble.
Think the leg-irons would be a good idea. I have
a pair for sale.
An axiom for political women in judging
the politician’s character: A man (like BillLyne,
for instance) who ostentatiously sees ladies about,
parades them round the town, rushes to them in
the lobbies, follows them ever with a fatherly
gallantry and a sentimental buttonhole, need give
no anxiety as to his moral character. It’s all
right. Public attentions of that sort bar the idea
of private attentions of a more mischievous kind.
It is the quiet, cynical, ostentatiously stand-off-
I-can’t-be-bothered-witli-women man who wants
watching, such as . Names supplied at
The Bulletin office to any representative of a
Ladies’ Moral Society on production of her gim-
let and other guarantees of good faith.
“Pisen.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is no greater slave to his “pisen” than
the average bushman. Billjim can do many
things, but one simple feat he cannot perform—
travel 100 miles with a cheque in his pocket and
keep it “unbroken.” Once “stiff,” he can go
from Sydney to the Gulf “on his cheek.’’ Funny,
isn’t it, that a man who can jam a half-broken
colt over a 4ft. jump cannot coax himself past the
nearest “shanty ”?
It’s easily known when the “fit” is upon him.
If summer-time, he complains of the “ ’eat ” on his
“’ead” since ‘ ‘that fall”; or, if winter, he is a
victim of the tooth-ache “oreful bad." Or per-
haps he has qualms in re a “ fav’rite sister,” who
resides in New England, or some distant spot,
and whom he has not seen “since God knows
when.” Failing these, he suddenly remembers
“a good ole ’orse” left “spelling” on Billa-
bogolong, situated anything up to 800 miles
away, which he “must git.” He’s never at
a loss for an excuse (most of them hackneyed)
and “ draw ” his “ bit ’’ he will —and off. Before
leaving he’ll make yon conversant with every
mile of the road he intends to journey, and will
emphatically state the date of arrival at his desti-
nation—“bar somethin’ outer the common
’appens.” You must take it all seriously, repress-
ing even the faintest smile. It hurts —or, maybe,
“ riles ” —him if you insinuate the least doubt as
to his ability to go direct to his “sister” or his
“ ’orse.” He spends his last Sunday on the place
cleaning and fixing up his “ gear, ” trimming his
horse’s feet and “ dolling” its mane and tail. It’s
pathetic to see the trouble he goes to on account
of his never-to-be-accomplished trip.
Old “ Caddy’s ” case appeared to me particu-
larly hard. He was an aged ex-“lag,”and had
been trying to reach Sydney for over 30 years.
The desire to revisit the scenes of his old-time
tortures amounted to a mania—but the other
mania was too strong for him. Four desperate
attempts had he made, and only once suc-
ceeded, in passing the first pub. (the second
stopped him on that occasion). The fifth
“forlorn hope” was about to be tried when
something even more sudden than “pisen”
frustrated his hopes. He dropped dead whilst
timing his Waterbury by the sunset on the
eve of his departure. The Government scooped
the savings that were to have taken him (with
considerable luck) to his promised land. Not even
a run for his money !
Another acquaintance of mine, Billy E
(educated as a clergyman), swore-off in the
strongest possible terms, calling on God to strike
him into the most fanciful shapes should he “do
it again.” He did “do it again,” and, returning
from town with his mate—both “tanked”—was
waddied to death by that individual.
LOUIS M.

Blood Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Stained with dark bloodstains, Life's roadways
smile and sever—
( White seas ! oh, wash red stains away !)
Hose-stains a/nd blood-stains—yet you are mine
for ever ;
This starless night will surely pass, and dawn will
herald day.
My love, my Snow Star, the great seas roll
between us—
Rolling long, lonely years away.
Snow Star ! Ah, Snow Star ! if only God had
seen us
That day we loved, and laughed, and watched the
sunset turn to gray !
Sunset and rose-light ! —We planned a path of
roses,
Heedless that each rose hides a thorn.
Love-light and rose-light! We said, “When
evening closes
We’ll kiss and float through dreamy ways to
laughter-girdled morn.”
Life’s battle called us to Hast and West asunder
(What mattered all the vows we swore !)
Life’s battle called us with bugle-blare and
thunder
(The sea-bird’s scream and challenge to the
breakers on the shore).
Some day—ah ! some day—we’ll drift, and hear
Love’s river
Rustling the lilies as it flows.
Some day—yes, some day—God’s angel will
deliver
His brimming cup of joy to us and render what he
owes.
Then at the sunset we'll reach a vale of roses
With never a blood-stain nor a thorn.
Snow Star ! my Snow Star / when sweet blue even-
ing closes
We'll love and dream through starry night to toft
dew-jewelled morn.
H.Xi.
WILL LAWSON.
A Bad Night.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were taking down “fats” from the head of
the Brisbane for a large firm of butchers. Jolly
hard work it was, too—three of us to 500 head ;
pack-horses, too, and rainy weather to start with.
One day we drove from Cressbroke reserve to
just outside Mount Esk—ten miles of a lane. We
tailed the cattle on the reserves till we had barely
time to reach our camp by sundown. While we
were tailing I was reading some papers that had
been given us at Colinton, and was particularly
struck with an account of how a man had been
thrown from his horse and then killed by some
bullocks he was driving.
It was after sundown when we arrived at the
paddock where we were to camp, and, to our dis-
comfiture, a road party had pitched its camp on
the other side of the rcfad, opposite the gate of the
paddock we were bound for. We put the cattle
up to the gate, but through they would not go.
They rung and rung and broke both up and down
the lane two or three times.
At last we forced them through the gate, and
let them rip for a bit, while one of us rode round
the.fence and the others hobbled-out and pitched
the tent. When we were fixed up we put the
cattle on their camp in the corner of the paddock
next the gate by which they had come in, so that
the lane ran right past the camp, and the road
party was just opposite to it. We debated a bit
about putting them there, but decided to do so, as
it was by far and away the best camp, and we
thought there was no danger of anyone coming
down the lane. I held them on the camp till they
were all down, and then rode into the township.
It was about 10.30 when I came out again,
entering the paddock at the opposite end to our
camp. To my amazement I found the cattle all
over the paddock. I rode through them to the
tent, and found my two mates turned in, but not
asleep.
“Do you know the cattle are off the camn.
Sam?” I said.
“Course I do,” he replied, “I ain’t deaf, an’ a
feller can’t get much sleep with them brutes
a-bellerin’ all over the bloomin’ show. We
couldn’t do nothin’ ; you ’ad the on’y moke, an’
we know’d you wouldn’t be long.”
“ How long have they been off ? ”
“Not long! ’Arf ’our at the outside. Some
flamin’ idiot galloped up the lane singin’ ‘Roll
the ole chariot along. ’ Drunk ’e wer, I reckon.
Any’ow, ’e put ’em orf.”
“Well, come and give us a hand to put them
back. I’ll get you a horse.”
I brought him a horse, and we put them back
on their camp. They soon settled down again,
and we left them and hobbled-out our mokes.
We wanted to start before daylight next morn-
ing, so as to clear the town before people were
about. It was a bright, frosty, moonlight night,
and instead of camping in the tent, I just slipped
off my boots and leggings, and, rolled-up in my
blankets, lay down alongside the fire, which we
had started right in the middle of a fallen tree-
wood was scarce. The fire was on my right, and
the tent 15 yards or so on my left.
I dropped off to sleep as soon as I lay down,
and must have been asleep about an hour when
something punching me in the back woke me.
Curiously enough I had been dreaming about
the man who was killed by bullocks, so I suppose
it was a sort of instinct made me refrain from
springing up. Whether or no, I slowly turned
my head under the blanket, and then very care-
fully pulled it off my face.
Within a few inches of my face I saw a huge
pair of horns, surmounting what appeared to my
excited imagination a ferocious white head. I
was in a blue funk, and yet I recognised the
beast (or fancied I did), as one of the crawlers.
When I recovered myself a little I slowly,
inch by inch, turned my head, and all around
me I saw shadowy, indistinct forms of cattle.
The cattle were off their camp again, and I
was lying in the middle of the mob ! I can tell
you I thanked my stars I had not sprung up when
that brute first woke me, for had I done so I must
have rushed them, and whichever way they
rushed they were bound to have come over me, to
day nothing of the tent and those in it.
. Well, there _I lay, playing 'possum, and shiver-
in £ and sweating witn funk. Beast by beast they

NE'ER-DO-WEEL.
Poor Land.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WnfiN I see a poor man rushing after a block of
Poor land it suggests images of a starved mouse
chasing a cat, ora hungry dog rushing into the
water to take a bite out of a crocodile. If he be a
good young man, and gets the land, he may con-
sider that he has overtaken thereat or fixed his
teeth in the crocodile.
Getting a living out of poor land is like extract-
ing the back tooth of a lion.
Are you industrious ? Poor land is lazy. Per-
sistent ? Poor land is patient. Are you quick ?
It laggeth. Toil never so hardly, poor land still
loafeth. Live parsimoniously, it will be extrava-
gant. Treat life as a play, it will make it a
tragedy. If you are proud, it will make you
humble. Rich ? It will impoverish you.
Have you boundless energy ? It will employ
it. Strength ? It will sap it. Youth ? It will
take it. Black hair? It will whiten it. Straight
backs it soon bends.
. Would you feed it ? It remains hungry. Starve
it—it will starve you. Let it run wild—it will
worry you. Keep it well in hand—it will bite you.
Give it your money—it demands yourself. Give
it yourself—it wants your wife and your children.
Generation after generation poor land will devour,
and not be satisfied.
It is the grave of hope and the hell of industry.
Poor land is a liar. It promises freedom and
binds its victims with a hundred chains. It
vaunts the independence it confers and makes the
wretches who are lured by its blandishments life-
long serfs of the soil. It says success is just round
the corner of Next Season, yet knows that it is in
the backblocks of Never. It tells you that your
ship will soon be coming home, yet knows it lies
stranded on the sands of Time.
Poor land makes neighbors cantankerous and
friends few.. It is the great-grandfather of
murder. It is the father of poor fences, which
are the parents of neigh bors’ quarrels, which are
forebears of assassination. If you are proud of
your strength and feel ready to combat anything,
have a tussle with pneumonia, or play with an
angry ox, or take the devil by the tail and swing
him thrice round your head, and then hit his
horns against the wall, but leave poor land
alone.
Samson is reported to have settled 1000 Philis-
tines in one scrimmage, but it isn’t recorded that
he settled on poor land.
M.L.
KATI KATI.
Blighted Hopes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A heavy thunder-storm had swept over Wilga,
leaving the drought-stricken plains gratefully
fragrant. Ralston, who had been left in charge
of the homestead since the removal of the sheep,
found four feet of water in the underground tank.
That meant to him pure drinking-water for some
weeks, and (he shrugged his shoulders in glad
anticipation) a shower-bath.
» * * •
A stolid, German-looking swagman knocked
tentatively at the office door. Ralston pointed to
an elevated malt-tank. “ G’day. If you’ll pump
two foot of water into that tank I’ll give you
extra rations and tobacco.” Then he finished the
letter to his employers to the welcome clink-clank
of the pump.
“ The indicator will tell you when you’ve got
two foot in her,” he said to the man as he passed
through the yard to the stables.
* * * *
When Ralston returned from the Carriers’
Arms the German was sitting on his swag, and
the pointer of the tank indicator stood at Oft.
“Someting wrong vid der pump,” he repeated
scornfully, and tried the handle, A dry gurgle
came from below. Then he went hurriedly to the
bathroom. When he came out the swagman - fled
before his whisky-flavored oaths.
* * * *
“ How vos I.ter know,” the German said in the
Carriers Arms the next morning, “ dat some
vun left de bathroom tap—vot you call it’
turned-on, and all de vater vos loss ? ”
VIC.
